The RED PAGE
The Long Roll —Mary Johnston (Constable).
A much solider novel than By Order of
the Company, or The Old Dominion, be-
ing closer to fact and more crammed
■avith characters. In the centre of many
interesting figures is Stonewall Jackson,
and one follows the Civil War from the
Southerner’s point of view—not breath-
lessly, but with much appreciation of the
pains taken by the writer.
The Story Girl —L. M. Montgomery (Geo.
Robertsorj and Co.). “The Story Girl”
invents for her companions little alle-
gories and fairy tales, some of which are
very neat. The sentiment and humor in
this book, as in Anne of Green Gables,
will delight any girl under ninety.
Her Husband’s Country —Sybil Spottiswood
(Heinemann). Like Dividing Waters,
this is a tale of an English girl who
married a.German soldier, and was un-
happy.. The Germans are not presented
in a favorable light, but Germany ap-
pears to be the husband’s country. The
story is worth reading.
The Ship of Coral —-H. de V. Stacpoole
(Dymock’s). “A tropical romance,”
with more adventure in it than The
Blue Lagoon —of which, by the way, a
shilling reprint has been issued. The
two men on the island, and Captain
Sagesse, of “La Belle Arlesienne,” are
variants of stock characters; yet the
yarn is told in a fresh and attractive
manner.
The R.A. Exhibition
Regular visitors to the exhibitions of the
Royal Art Society of N.S.W. will notice an
improvement in this year’s Show. The
society has adopted the plan of grouping
each painter’s work separately. By this
means the goodness of the good is rendered
more apparent, while the badness of the bad
shrieks so that the spectator passes on hur-
riedly. The number of the absolutely bad
canvases is less than usual. Rumor says
that some members of the Selection Commit-
tee were amongst the totally rejected. It
was understood at one time that to be on
the selection committee meant that a good
swag of your own stuff would be accepted,
likewise a proportion 'of your pupils’ elegant
views of Pinchgut and portraits of grand-
mother. If that time is past, there is hope
of progress. Evidently the old society is
bucking up, and if it gets some more vigor-
ous new blood into its body, it may get out
of the ruts and push ahead.
The dominant note of the R.A.S. in the
past has been Stodge. It has stood royally
for the Tame and the Respectable. A col-
lection of men who were supposed to be
artists by the grace of God went to the
trouble of getting themselves labelled
“Royal,” while they allowed square rods of
tripe to cover their walls. It was Very unexcit-
ing tripe, for propriety and conventionality
seemed to be the first considerations of the
painters. In the present show there is not
even a bare leg to flurry Mrs. Wowser.
Clothes are treated with the deference due
to their high respectability. The macintosh
in one and the scarf in another of Miss
W. Lister Lister.
Chapman’s pictures, the dresses on J. S'.
Watkins’ ladies, and some other softgoods
exhibited could be pawned at any Mont-de-
Piet6. It is, of course, better to have a
simple thing cleverly painted such as Miss
MacPherson’s bowl of eggs than a “demmed
unpleasant body”; but the absence of the
nude is an evidence of low technical aim.
One misses, also, any attempt to get the
richness and intensity of color which is
prodigally displayed in our bush scenery.
Most of the landscapes are tame, and help
to support the foreign theory that Australia
is a monotonous country. It would be a
good thing for the society to have an orgy of
vermilion, or break into a riot of Prus-
sian blue for ‘a change. Datillo Rubbo’s gaso-
meter is welcome as a splash of color, and
J. R. Jackson’s “The Sunday Afternoon
Nan” is commendable as a move in the Im-
pressionist direction.
Hans ITeysen has posed all his bush
scenes according to one formula; but the
Norman Carter.
A. Dattilo, Rubbo.
J. S. Watkins.
painting is so jolly good that the subject
ceases to be important. J. Salvana’s real-
ism gives the timber and the earth; while
Heysen seems to get the soul of the place as
well. Norman Garter’s sofa is an agree-
able harmony in low tones, keyed up by
throwing a black cat into the middle. The
clean, deft strokes of Longstaff’s portrait
make some of the other heads look sick.
There are a few other exhibits which help
to justify the society’s existence, and show
that there is enough talent there to keep the
craft afloat.
It is only fair to judge the bulk of the ex-
hibits as one does minor verse. There are
no Shakespeares or Titians in Australia yet; i
but there are a good many capable crafts-
men who have with little encouragement
done creditable work. Some of them go
away to bigger art centres, and are put
in a high place there; but in nearly every
case their merit has been discerned before
they left. What little encouragement there
is given to art in a practical country like
this should be concentrated upon the men
who are worth while, and an art society is
expected to help in that direction by rigor-
ously repressing the duffers. An untrained
observer can see with the naked eye that
the proper medium of some of the exhibitors
is mud.
The motonony of subject and tameness of
treatment are mainly due to the subordina-
tion of art to material considerations. The
artist says he must live though the callous
may fail to see the necessity. The only pic-
tures which the average painter can sell are
the dinky little land- or sea-scapes which run
up to five guineas. Teaching is his main-
stay, and the class ties him to the town;
therefore we rarely get any paintings of
characteristic Australian scenes such as
Lambert’s “Across the Blacksoil Plains,”
Roberts’ “Golden Fleece,” or Officer’s views
of outback country.
This, perhaps, is inevitable in a young
country where art has not been taken seri-
ously. There are signs of a more enlight-
ened public interest in painting, and of a
recognition by the Government that local art
deserves consideration with other native in-
dustries. The R.A.S. should do its best to
foster these promising developments by
raising the general average, and if it cannot
get away from the false gods of Respecta-
bility, it can at least encourage its pupils to
nourish their color sense and break away
from the trite in safe directions.
Ezra Pound
One of the strangest figures amongst the
poets of the day is Ezra Pound, whose
Personae and Exultations were reviewed
here on the 24/2/TO. It is believed that he
is of mixed blood, was born in America, and
educated in England. Pie is well acquainted
with Italian and Provencal literature, and
his lectures on The Spirit of Romance were
a curious mixture of learning and preten-
tiousness. He worked on the good principle
that “Art is a joyous thing,” and made a
plea for greater levity, a more befitting
levity, in the study of the arts. His defini-
tion of poetry is worth quoting.
Poetry is a sort of inspired mathematics,
which gives us equations, not for abstract
figures, triangles, spheres and the like, but
equations for human emotions. If one have
a mind which inclines to magic rather than
to science, one will prefer to speak of these
equations as spells or incantations. Speak-
ing generally, the spells or equations of
“classic” art invoke the beauty of the normal,
and spells of “romantic” art are said to in-
voke the beauty of the unusual.
The two little books of verse above men-
tioned contain a good deal that is spasmodic,
bizarre and disagreeable ; but also some un-
doubted poetry. So with his latest volume—
The Canzoni of Ezra Pound (Elkin
Mathews, 3s. 6d.).
His exercises in intricate forms such as
the canzone and sestina are interesting to
technicians, and tiresome to the ordinary
reader. There are some translations from
the French of Du Bellay, the German of
Heine, and the Italian of Leopardi and
others. The chief interest of the book is in
a number of explosions in verse, more force-
ful than elegant. The poems quoted here
are fairly representative, and the book con-
tains others which are worth the attention
of any student of poetry.
BONNET.
If on the tally-board of ivasted days
They daily write me for proud idleness,
Let high Hell summons me, and I confess, .
No overt act the preferred charge allays
To-day I thought—what boots it what I
thought?
Poppies and gold! Why should I blurt it
out ?
Or hawk the magic of her name about
Deaf doors and dungeons where no truth is
bought?
Who calls me idle? I have thought of her.
Who calls me idle? By Cod’s truth, I’ve
seen
’jl ne arrowy sunlight in her golden snares.
Let him among you all stand summonser
Who hath done better things! Let whoso
hath been
With worthier works concerned display his
wares !

When brightest colors seem but dull in hue,
And noblest arts are shown mechanical,
When study serves but to heap clue on clue
That no great line hath been or ever shall,
But hath a savor like some second stew
Of many pot-lots with a smack of all.
’Twas one man’s field, another’s hops the
brew,
’Twas vagrant accident not Fate’s fore-call.
Horace, that thing of thine is overhauled,
And “Wood Notes Wild” vveaves a concocted
sonnet.
Here aery Shelley on the text hath called,
And here, great Scott, the Murex Keats
comes on it,
And all the lot howl “Sweet Simplicity /”
’Tis Art to hide our theft exquisitely.

AU SALON.
Her grave sweet haughtiness
Pleaseth me, and in likewise
Her quiet ironies
Others are beautiful, none more, some less.
I suppose when poetry comes down to facts,
When our souls are returned to the gods
and the,, spheres they belong in,
Here in the every-day where our acts
Rise up and judge us ;
I suppose there are a few dozen verities
That no shift of mood can shake from us;
One place where we’d rather have tea
(Thus far hath modernity brought us)

Talk of the latest success, give wing to some
scandal

that we prefer to play up to.,

the absolute unimportant.
“Man is as lazy as he dares to be,” accord-
ing to Emerson, and the Debating Society
young man is like the rest of the genus. If
anyone will supply him with a few rounds of
solid, shot when he goes into battle, he can
provide his own Greek fire and Chinese stink-
pots. E. A. Vidler has collected a store of
ammunition in The Australian Debater
(Geo. Robertson and Co., 2s. Od.), which
will serve if the fight is on any one of 27
questions. “Did Shakespeare write Bacon?”
and a few others are out of the old
stock; the majority are of immediate and
Australian interest. “A Subsidised National
Theatre” is a subject seldom treated in
books of this kind, apd the arguments on both
sides are given with considerable fulness.
Occasionally, the arguments are of the for-
titer in modo description, such as this one
for firing at the advocates of Woman’s
Equality with Man :- —
“Women who have taken to literary
work have generally devoted themselves to
the writing of temperance tracts or ob-
scene novels, the latter of which, at least,
have done much harm in vitiating the taste
of readers, though it is a fact that that
class of fiction is read only by women.”
* * * *
At the Sign of the Southern Cross, by
Annie MacDonald (Geo. Robertson and Co),
contains some simple verses which may ap-
peal to women, with “Unlucky Dick,”
“Youngster Jack,” “Gordon’s Leap” and
other pieces for recitation. The account of
Gordon’s famous jump at Mount Gambier,
based on the narrative of William Trainor,
who knew the poet, contains this statement:
I recall some fences Gordon built, three of
them fashioned of stockyard rails,
The highest of them was four foot six, sure
death if the horse or rider quails;
He called them Doubtful, Danger and Death;
I’ve taken the first one when he led.
But I watched him ,leap the other two, whilst
I rode round the rails instead.
There must have been something curious
about a mere 4ft. 6in. fence to deserve the
name of “Death.”
Collars and Cuffs, the Adventures of a
Jackeroo, by St. Clare Grondona (George
Robertson and Co., Is.), will do for new-
chums to send to the people at Home. C.
and C. is the great original Jackeroo by the
name of Smith, who walloped the burly and
insulting bushman to a standstill, vaulted
on to the unbroken colt and galloped the tail
off it, and performed other deeds of valor
with consummate ease. The natives were
astonished, and Red Davies, who suffered
from the gentleman’s biff, ever afterwards
fed out of his hand. Yes, let the tale go
home (with Saltbush Bill’s) “to the Public
Schools—of the pluck of the English swell,
how the drover fought for his very life, but
blood in the end will tell.”
After the Sho[?].
In a survey of the English novels pub-
lished in the year ending June last the Lon-
don Nation finds that the most significant
work is that of Wells, Galsworthy and
Belloc.
“For felicity of style and atmospheric deli-
cacy we hold that Miss Rosalind Murray’s
The Leading Note was by far the most in-
teresting performance of the year, and for
creative originality and psychological skill
Walter De La Mare’s The Return takes a
prominent place. Among newcomers the
two most promising are, perhaps, the author
of Martha Vine and D. H. Laurence, whose
country story, The White Peacock, is full of
beauty and charm. We are still extraor-
dinarily poor in interpreters of the life of
the working classes, and Stephen Reynolds’
Alongshore, Neil Lyons’ Sixpenny Pieces,
and W. 11. Davies’ A Weak Woman, all
most penetrating in their shrewd insight
into the workings of minds unsophisticated
by “education,” stand out in grateful relief
both in subject and treatment.”
The writer has a word or two for Arnold
Bennet’s faithfulness to the bourgeois ideal,
arid offers the opinion that the ordinary
novel is far too long, “too full of unnecessary
detail and undigested material,” and that
modern English fiction is suffering from a
superfluity of comfortable tissue and a cer-
tain indifference to spiritual issues.

VARIOVS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Priest to the Temple.
I am as lazy as a Summer sea,
Divinely lazy, grandly indolent;
In joyous wealth of lavish lassitude
I mark the happy clouds that float and brood
In sleepy skies. A measureless content
Fills all this greener place of herb and tree.
Spent are the breezes of the chill, sweet south;
The master sun, full-rayed with Summer flame,
Drives down the westward slope. In the deep shade
Is set a. quiet shrine, where one white maid
Tends a cool altar. Chaste and free from blame
Are all the holy kisses of her mouth.
Fair is the priestess, but the inner dusk
Is fragrant with the breath of deity;
The lucent sandals of a goddess press
These shadows, and the waft of her clear dress
Winnows the leaves like wings of memory
Beating in dreams the sweets of rose and musk.
Deep in the cool of grasses lush and rare
Her worshippers lie prone, and over them
Grows the vast sweetness of her quiet eyes,
And the slow smile on perfect lips made wise
By happy silence. One red, glowing gem
Flames in the darkness of her braided hair.
Here is no voice of revel; feast and song
Dwell never in these shadows, and no drum,
Throbbing its vibrant thunders from afar,
Can set the green a-shudder. Love and war,
Rude labor, and the many-hearted hum
Of silver prayer faint in an air so strong.
Here no flute ripples with a shepherd strain,
Nor harp-string with the finger of the wind
Stirs vagrant melody. Twin mirth and wail
Perish upon the threshold, and the pale
Chaplet of thought is withered. Gems that bind
The brow of memory melt to dreams again.
For at the outer portal of the shade
There droops a crystal veil of ecstasy
That purifies the spirit with cold fire,
Cleansing the taint of motive and desire
From every vein of throbbing energy,
Till will and reason like the winds are laid.
Dear goddess of the shadow-haunted eyes,
Bend softly over me, teach me to know
The measured cadences of things that are,
The reverent indolence of sun and star,
The listless gladness of all flowers that blow,.
And make me as the trees and mountains wise!
This hour is thine. My languid soul in thrall
Sees naught beyond the rippling of thine hair,
Feels naught beyond the fragrance of thy breath,
Tastes naught beyond the kiss that lingereth
On my rapt lips. A god’s large bliss I share,
And dreaming nothing, find that nothing all.
N.S.W.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.
Memory.
Summer comes round on land and sea,
Clad in a radiant panoply,
Crowned and costumed in burning gold.
But where are the lights that shone of old ?
Beauty is born of each rare hour —-
Blade and frondage and feather and flower,
Color and perfume on all the ways.
But where is the beauty of old-time days?
Having much, we sigh for the little we lack,
And, aching and ailing, the heart goes back
From the golden Present —the near, rich lights—
To the Past with dead grey noons and nights.
Why? . . . Because in the sunlit South
There was no mouth with the red of her mouth ;
No hair like hers ’neath its arching skies,
And no eyes blue as her blue, sweet eyes.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
The Tyrant.
0 pitiless Sea, thou hast flooded mine eyes
With tears that are salt as thy brine!
Deep down in thy cavernous bosom, where lies
The wife thou hast riven from mine ?
Oh, where hast thou hidden my bride of a year ;
The treasure I trusted to thee?
My hope has become but a foil to my fear,
O cruel, insatiate Sea!
Since man’s puny might, but omnipotent soul,
Has dared thy tempestuous whim,
Oh, bitterly heavy, a merciless toll,
Thy storms have exacted of him !
Could woe of the mother, nor weeping of wife,
Suffice thy usurious fee?
Thou leavest indeed but the wreck of my life,
O cruel, insatiate Sea!
Because, like a tiger, with feline caress
Thy ripples kiss lightly the shore,
Shall toll that thou takest of life be the less?
Thy measure of mercy be more?
Deep hid in the slime that lies under thy smile
Are ships thou hast tossed in thy glee,
Discarded, the toys that amused thee awhile,
O cruel, insatiate Sea!
O treacherous Sea! In inveterate hate
Ten thousand of hearts hast thou tossed
On billows of hope and despair alternate,
With “Overdue!” “Missing!” and “Lost!”
The bones of the ships that lie strewn on thy sand,
We gave thee, an offering free.
Could they not content thine unending demand,
O cruel, insatiate Sea!
,s.w.
JOHN WALTERS.
The Procession of the Hours.
The hours upon their tender feet,
To mingled cheers and hoots,
Go .waddling down Time’s dusty street
In old prunella boots.
The arms of three are full of bags
Containing produce cheap ;
Hard work is borne by eight old hags;
Eight more bear home our sleep.
And five convey our shoddy toys —
Our hobbies, hates and loves,
All jumbled up with fears and joys—
In busted cotton gloves.
Straight to Life’s dingy boarding-den
The dull procession wends ;
It hurries in and out again
And starts off when it ends.
Instead of going with it, I
Forego of sleep my share,
And watch it, wond’ring dully why
The hours can’t do their hair.
TT.S.W.

AUSTRALIA’S HIGH CONDITIONER.
In this country George Reid has won singularly wide personal popularity, not only in London, but throughout the principal centres of population in the
provinces. His energy has appeared almost superhuman. Through the long and busy season he has not only dined out nearly every night, but has frequently
lunched, and occasionally, as on the departure of Mr. Irving to Australia, sat out a late supper party.— Henry Lucy m the S. M. HERALD’S London letter.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and. to an extent, themselves) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the
form of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse,
Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 31)00 words, are specially accept-
able), Paragraphs, Letters or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS.
when the address is specified and stamps are en-
closed. But no MS. will be returned, or preserved,
unless these conditions are observed. When MS. is
forwarded without stamps for return it will be un-
derstood that the copy is to be destroyed if not ac-
ceptable. Personal applications for return of MS.
cannot receive attention. The Bulletin desires,
further, those sending MS. to distinctly understand
that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea,
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any pic-
ture which has to be re-drawn—will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 7, 1911.
THE DAY OF GLOOM.
These are sorrowful days among the Free-
traders of Great Britain —days of doubt and
cold feet —days when the plug hat of the
true believer is barely visible above the
waters of despondency—days when forebod-
ing goes about like a swift india-rubber lion
seeking what it may devour. The Freetrader
is obviously not what he was. His brief,
tempestuous creed has gone through three
stages and several sub-stages, and now there
are signs, and very many of them, that the
last toothless period of its existence is not
far off. It is difficult to fix dates, for one
historical period is apt to melt imperceptibly
into another, but the establishment of the
Cobden policy is commonly regarded as hav-
ing begun after a small fashion about 1846
and as having been completed about 1869.
Anyone who is not satisfied with these dates
can fix others for himself, but these are near
enough for ordinary purnoses. During the
early part of this period Britain was talking
Freetrade and beginning to practise it. But
it began so tentatively that it was not till
1869 that the system was in fairly complete
working order. And during these preliminary
years Britain was aided by a number of
special circumstances. In the first place,
centuries of rigorous Protection, and of, gen-
erally, good luck in war, had placed it so far
ahead, not only of all individual competitors,
but of all industrial competitors combined,
that it was veritably the world’s workshop,
and it seemed as if any serious opposition
was an illimitable space of years ahead.
Secondly, the gigantic and incredible influx
of gold from Australasia created a. boom
which covered a multitude of sins. And
thirdly, the Freetrade craze spread to other
countries which now compete seriously
against Britain, and for a number of years
they obligingly obliterated themselves to a
very great extent. From 1851 to some year
which is rather hard to fix—probably in the
’SO’s of last century—was the halcyon period
of Freetrade, when the Bounderby of the new
creed projected himself into the illimitable
future and declined to listen to argument,
and the Pumblechook of the new cult
drowned all hostile criticism with his voice.
* * * *
The change came slowly at first —-so slowly
that it is as impossible to put a finger upon
the precise spot on the calendar as it is to
say that one moment before noon on a certain
day the Roman Empire was still on the up
grade, that at noon sharp it had become a
stationary proposition, and that at one
minute past noon it had accomplished the
first .00000000000001tli of an inch of the
downward slither which, after many cen-
turies, was to end in the pit of perdition.
Other nations drifted to Protection, one at a
time, and with long intervals between. They
began to protect their industries in a very
small way that hardly mattered, and it was
only at further intervals that they added to
their tariffs and got them into real fighting
order. Britain had still its stupendous gold
inflow, which helped to disguise the position
just as the gold of Mexico and Peru once did
in Spain, and none of its rivals save the
United States enjoyed this advantage. The
period was also one of large annexations,
which opened new markets and created a
sort of prosperity, and it was a time of free-
dom from serious war, in which respect some
of Britain’s competitors were less fortunate.
Yet there came a time in the late ’9o’s when
it had to be admitted that things were not
all they had been. No one could say just
when the difference began, but there was a
difference. Then the Boer war possibly gave
a jolt to a constitution which could no longer
stand sudden shocks, just as happened to the
frozen pirate of fiction when he fell over
some small obstacle at the age of between
100 and 200, and his suspended old age hit
him all at once.
* * * *
At all events a day has come when Britain
has to acknowledge that Germany is ahead
of it as a manufacturing Power, and that
the United States is so immeasurably ahead
that it now turns out more than three times
Bz-itain’s value in finished goods. And the
boast that the British workman is the most
prosperous in the world has been regretfully
put away in the lumber room of exploded
events. The German worker is reluctantly
admitted to be as comfortable as his English
rival, and a recent inquiry made by the
Board of Trade in London led to the sorrow-
ful admission that, while the United States
toiler pays 50 per cent, more than the J.
Bull artisan for living expenses, he receives
fully 130 per cent, more pay. Yet so long
as British industries continued to grow, the
mere fact that those of some other countries
were growing faster didn’t constitute an abso-
lute nightmare. The British individual
didn’t leap up horizontally in his sleep, and
strike the ceiling of his modest chamber and
fall back on the bed in a cold perspiration.
He didn’t have to step high as he left his
door, in order to get over the hideous shape
of the Dweller on the Threshold —something
with the head of Cobden, and the body of
the Beast of Revelation, and with the worm
that dieth not for a tail. The wolf didn’t
come so near the door that his noisome
breath poured through the keyhole and
spoiled the victuals, and though the dragon
of Want might be in the streets, he hadn’t
got so far as actually to take off the roof andl
look with a baleful saucer eye inside. Now,
however, another change has become ap-
parent. It is the fear that the long-continued
decline in agriculture may be accom-
panied by one in manufactures, that has
driven the Briton to wear a porous plaster
on his back, in order to counteract the cold
feeling at the base of the stomach.
* * * *
The latest Statesman’s Year Book does
not, unfortunately, bring all its figures up to
the year 1910. If it did it rnigiit perhaps
be more cheering than it is; also it might
not. At all events the figures set forth are
poor enough. The amount of iron ore pro-
duced in the British Isles (1909) was
14,979,979 tons, against 15,731,604 tons in
1907. In the same period the iron ore im-
ported fell from 7,641,934 tons to 6,328,623,
showing an all-round decline in the industry.
The number of blast furnaces was 369 in
1907 and only 318 in 1909, and in these same
years the amount of ore smelted was reduced
from 25,123,759 tons to 23,691,241 tons.
The pig-iron output fell from 10,114,281
tons to 9,531,987 tons; and the pig-iron ex-
ported from 1,943,939 tons to 1,140,695 tons.
These are not wildly hilarious figures in any
respect. The coal dug out of the bowels of
Bull’s country was 267,830,962 tons in
1907, while in 1909 it showed a slight drop,
and the figure was 263,774,312 tons. The
value dropped more than the quantity—
from £120,527,378 in 1907 to £106,274,888 in
1909. The value of coal and coke exported
achieved a notable slump, from £42,118,994
to £37,129,978. The same authority declares
that the amount of cotton retained in Britain
for use in British factories reached the top
figure (2,056,735,7441 b.) in 1907, while in
1910 (these figures are more up-to-date than
the others) it was down to 1,716,763,5521 b.
The wool retained for use in British fac-
tories is tabulated as 590 million lb. in 1907
and 535 million lb. in 1909, but some rather
scrappy later figures suggest that this in-
dustry looked up again in 1910. The flax
industry is given in three-year periods, and
the average annual consumption of flax in
the British factories is set down as distinctlv
greater in 1905-6-7 (226,000,0001 b) than in
1908-9-10 (217,000,0001 b.). Figures are
dreary things at best, but some figures are
drearier than others, and these figures are
distinctly the drearier ones—not the others.
The same oft-quoted book of reference
offers more food for profound reflection. It
gives in three-year periods, and up to the
latest date, the value of the cotton, woollen
and flax industries combined, and declares
that, when the three are added together, the
average annual consumption of raw material
in British factories was 2824 million lb. for
11)05-0-7, and only 2077 million lb. for 1908-
9-10. It reveals that British woollens are
one-fourth, or more than one-fourth, old rags.
It alleges that, in the year 1900, vessels re-
presenting 922,182 tons were built in British
yards for the British mercantile marine;
that in 1907 the figure was 740,812 tons ; in
1908 a mere 413,052 tons; and in 1909 only
510,890 tons. Statistics of this sort suggest
a heroic slump. The total British shipping
engaged in home and foreign trade —in other
words, everything in active service bar river
and canal boats —is alleged to have been
11,288,759 tons in 1907, and only 11,140,801
tons in 1909. The British ships that en-
tered and cleared at British ports for over-
sea voyages dropped from 81,308,000 tons in
1907 to 79,704,000 tons in 1909, while foreign
shipping in British ports increased in the
same time from 51,903,000 tons to 53,504,000
tops. The revenue from the railways of the
United Kingdom sank from £121,545,923 for
the year 1907 to £120,174,052 in 1909. The
net post and telegraph revenue has been
declining for some years, though that doesn’t
necessarily prove anything. The total births
in three kingdoms during 1907 were
1,148,573, as compared with the diminished
figure of 1,145,962 in 1909 (there was a
temporary rise in 1908, because that was
leap-year, and there was an extra day to be
born on). The figures for 1910 are given
as regards Scotland only, and show 4000 less
births for that little kingdom alone than
there were in 1909, which is a further fall of
no small magnitude. Marriages diminished
from 332,072 (1907) to 313,001 (1909).
The births increased (from
47,095 to 49,111), though the total births
slumped. The number of British and Irish
emigrants reached the top in 1910 (in this
department the figures are unusually up-to-
date). So did the bill for the maintenance
of paupers, which was £13,834,419 in 1901;
£16,694,752 in 19G7; and £17,588,212 in
1909.
* * * *
There are still some more interesting
features in the same interesting and fre-
quently-mentioned publication. The number
of convictions for serious crimes was 14,184
in 1907 (a steady effort is made to avoid the
year 1908, which, being leap-year, supplied
an extra day on which any man might be
convicted or hanged) ; it rose in 1909 to
14,987. The new census alleges that out of
the 67 counties which make up Scotland and
Ireland 45 show a loss of population during
the past 10 years; that five of the 12 Welsh
counties exhibit the same result; also three
English counties. On the other hand, the
value of the fish products of British waters
rose from £11,266,283 in 1907 to £11,342,660
in 1910 (1909 was a worse year than 1907),
a rise of £76,377. And foreign commerce —
the persistent dragging of goods here and
there —grows steadily, though apparently
the proportion carried in British vessels
doesn’t increase. In 1907 the imports and
exports of the United Kingdom were worth
£1,163,785,109. The next two years saw a
decline, but the figures for 1910 reached
£1,212,806,055. Even the exports of Britain’s
own produce have grown a little, despite the
decline of so many industries, though in
proportion to population they are on the
down grade; in 1907 they stood at
£426,035,083, or £9 13s. 3d. for each inhabi-
tant; while in 1910, after two years of severe
downfall, they reached £430,589,811, or £9
9s. sd. per inhabitant. One theory is that
the Briton uses less and sells more because
of his poverty; a second view is that the
preference granted to purely British goods
by some parts of Greater Britain induces him
to sell British goods and use foreign; a third
explanation is that both influences are at
work. At all events, it is certain that the
export of British goods is less per inhabitant
than it was in 1907, while dealings in foreign
goods have gone up.
* * * *
Even with all the expansion of foreign
commerce, the total result is not cheerful.
There is a feeling in some quarters that 1907
was the last year of prosperity under the old
order of things, and that since then the
slump has been more sudden, more obvious
and more widely diffused than any slump was
before, except in time of war, or during the
exhaustion which followed on a series of
ruinous campaigns. So, nowadays, the Eng-
lishman wakes in the middle of the night and
fancies that the belltopper on the peg is a
devil, or sits up in the dim grey of morning
with an idea that his coat upon the nail is
the freshly-hanged ghost of Cobden. He can
hardly even fall over a donkey on his way
home without wondering if John Bright has
not risen from the dead. In short, he is
nervous and afflicted with the jumps, and
unless there is an early improvement in his
affairs these jumps are liable to become
chronic.
THE TRADE OF WAR.
Some language from “Bilbax” :
The Bulletin, I notice, is prosecuting the Anci-
Soldier agitators and press before the court of Public
Opinion. On behalf of the defendant party I beg
leave to offer a few remarks.
All the cost of war, in blood, suffering and misery,
falls on the working-classes. Working-class fathers,
husbands and sons provide Hie battle-field banquet for
the crows. Working-class mothers, sisters and chil-
dren of the before-mentioned crow’s-meat—-deprived
of the Saturday night dividend it paid when in its
live state —pawn soul and body for a price that is
not always sufficient to maintain those assets in a
marketable condition. Then, for them, comes star-
vation, with its ultimate happy release in the ex-
change of the dismal Hell here for the merrier Hell
down under, where rent and fuel are free, and food
and clothing don’t matter. And the working-class
survivors (those who fought and those who stayed at
home) are taxed while they live to pay the war-bill;
and, similarly, after them, their children and their
children’s children even unto the ninth generation.
To the capitalist class go all the spoils. Whether
victory be scored or not, theirs the profit on guns,
ships, officers’ millinery, and the general war equip-
ment. Theirs the grab of territorv. tribute and trade
when the Lord of Hosts has fought on their side.
And when He deserts, the capitalist man easily stand
from under and let the tax-burden wallop on to the
other fellow. To its Kitchener the grateful State
hands half a million of money as his fitting reward:
to its Tommy Atkins (and only if he be crippled)
twopence a day, or thereabouts.
Soon the crossing of the border by the standing
army of a European country will be the signal for the
revolt of the working-class at home. They know at
last that the main purpose served by a standing
army is the maintenance of working-class thraldom.
Foreign predation is but its incidental use. For
my justification in asserting working-class conscious-
ness of this position, I point to Industrial Unionism —
a movement common to all white-man countries;
based on an understanding of what is here rou-dilv
stated ; international in its aim; only yesterday" re
garded as the impossible fad of a few ; to-dav movin'-
with a speed that dwarfs all else in working-class
history. Industrial Unionists have it that to lend
their fighting-power to the State, until thev have
attained complete political control and complete in-
dustrial organisation, is but to rivet their own chains
the more securely. In their regard the soldier is the
one disabling scab on the working-class body.
The above attitude docs not blind the Australian
working-man to the danger, that The Bulletin in-
sists upon, of his country being invaded by a pre-
datory nation. But he’s too busy just now with an
equally important and more immediate matter. Here
in Queensland are big encampments of workers, fight-
ing on the folded-arms method, with the organised
force of the State arrayed against them, in support or
the capitalist endeavor to withhold any reward to
Labor other than a starvation wage. The story of recent
struggles of the same kind is too fresh in the worker’s
memory to permit of any enthusiasm about the forma-
tion of a lighting force that, in present circum-
stances, would certainly be used against him.
The fact to be faced in this matter is that the
old order is changing. The grossly materialistic-
spirit of the age, working downwards, now permeates
even the working-class. Glory, patriotism, the graves
of our fighting forefathers, the dear old flag, "Just
Before the Battle, Mother,” the blare of trumpets, and
all the old conjurations fail to stir the working-man’s
pulse as of yore. He begins to callously ask where
does he come in, and what is the Wages Board
award in this particular trade?
Here semes to me a fair solution of the difficulty.
We have hundreds of unemployed at any time of the
year: in winter there are thousands idle in the
Southern States. An offer of fair wages for a fixed
term (coincident as far as possible with the slack
season) would enrol battalions in the big centres.
And, with a clearly-defined arrangement that the
worker might spend any unemployed period in thus
acquiring a military training (and wages), this
would bring practically the whole of the working-
class into the army. The laborer is just as worthy
of his hire when working for his country as when
privately employed, and just as much a scab if he
takes less than fair wages for soldiering in defence
of other people’s property, as when he undercuts his
mate who is fighting for tucker for wife and children.
The day of the slave-soldier is passing; and the
above are probably the best terms on which the
State can hope to lure the present-day worker into
the fighting-trade.
* * * *
To which The Bulletin offers a few dis-
respectful remarks in reply :
(1) All the cost of war in blood, etc., doesn’t
fall on the working«classes .under a system of compul-
sory military training. The rich mall’s son has to get
into the ranks just the sanle as the poor man’s. If the
officers, as is the case in many countries, come from
the wealthy classes, so much the worse for them.
They get the most bullets in proportion to their
numbers. For instance, in the- Franco-German war
the death-rate among privates was--:-Engineers, 17 per
1000 ; cavalry and artillery, 27 ; infantry, 52 ; while
officers died at the rate of 76 per 1000, and that was
quite an ordinary case. As for the poor paying all
the bill, they don’t —unless they pay champagne
duty, income-tax, tax on armorial bearings, and
100 things of that sort.
(2) The rich don’t get all the profit on guns, etc.
-—not in Australia, and Australia is the place that
affects us. This country is starting its own arms
factory, and it has schemes for making its uniforms
in Government workshops, and building its-ships in
Government yards. What profits there are go to
the workers. As to the idea (re the “grab of terri-
tory”) that the Australian Labor Government may
have schemes for annexing Japan, or some foreign
country, and dividing up the soil among the great
Australian capitalists, it is too childish to be worth
consideration.
(3) As regards the statement that “the main pur-
pose served by a standing army is the maintenance of
working-class thraldom” —who in thunder wants a
standing army ? The whole purpose of universal mili-
tary training is to avoid the need for a standing
army. When the Parliament which controls the citi-
zen force is one in which every adult citizen and
citizeness has an equal vote, and when the military
force consists of male voters who are also the male
workers —well, that force can’t be used to systemati-
cally oppress the worker, unless that same worker is
a tyrant, a ruffian and a non-unionist at heart, .also
a habitual suicide. All the talk about things that
happen in despotic countries in Europe is as - much
beside the question as the battles of the kites and
crows. The view that an army of the people, solely
controlled by the people (by all the people) is liable
to go in for wanton war simply because the people
want to be killed for the benefit of capitalists, reads
like blank insanity.
(1) The proposal of “Bilbax” to have a sort of
well-paid periodical standing army of unemployed
(presumably to shoot down the employed when
they want better wages) doesn’t appeal to this paper.
That army would include most of the able-bodied
scamps and wasters of the community, and- it. would
thus tend to form a separate caste, and a very dan-
gerous one. It wouldn’t include “practically the
whole working-class,” for the whole working-class
doesn’t suffer from spells of unemployment. And if
it did “include practically the whole working-class,”
why should the workman alone be shot at?
(5) “Bilbax’s” theory seems to be that the only
armies in existence should belong to despotic coun-
tries which are likely to invade their neighbors, and
that countries like Australia, where the people are the
sole rulers, and where - aggressive war is. impossible,
unless folk, like “Bilbax-” become mad for blood,
should have no defence-at all.- In short, all the arms
the world possesses should be in the hands of bad
characters, and the good characters should rely- on
prayer.
(6) The Bulletin again urges that the policy of
non-resistance should be first tried locally and on a
small scale. Let us abolish the police, and see if
our own people can be relied upon not to do
burglary, stoush and other crimes. If we find vre
can fully trust them, let us begin to think m
trusting other nations. It is rash to go the whole
experimental or untried hog in an instant.
(7) The Bulletin undertakes to devote next Sun-
day to thinking over “Bilbax’s" other propositions.
The idea that patriotism should be abolished, anc
that nobody should do anything for the defence of his
country and his fellow-man (and woman) except for
full current wages, is certainly interesting. T“ e
proposal that everybody should receive _ full pay for
the time spent in his military education natural!.'
raises the question whether children at school and
youths at college shouldn’t also be paid for lost
time. And though it is mere ignorance to talk of
Kitchener receiving half a million, and of a pri-
vate’s pension being 2d. per day (the minimum is
Bd.), still such discrepancies as that between
Kitchener’s reward and the reward of T. Atkins
are striking. There are many things of the kind m
this world. One is the discrepancy between the pay
of the caretaker or cleaner at the Trades Hall and tne
pay of the eminent Labor man who is Prime Minister
of Australia. Why not tackle this local anomaly
first ?
CHRISTIAN BURIAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The average citizen who mixes Heaven
with the House of Hanover, and who is
half-satisfied that every British war is under
the especial guidance of the Almighty, shud-
ders at the imagined horrors of the Parsers
Towers of Silence, and then buries his dear
in sure and certain hope of a glorious resin-
rection. In Sydney he mostly buries him m
Rookwood, where the resurrection is partm
and of great frequency, and occurs usual!'
much earlier than was expected. In the re-
cently-published particulars of the filth.'
abuses of this and other cemeteries the
were the horrors of five in a grave, ot m-
liquescent corruption that oozed at the te
of the clayey rises and snaked along in sim-
gish filthiness to pools that were the dri
of dairy cows. The bitter cry of the re
lord who owns property near by is hear .


for a depreciated dollar roars like a wounded
bull; but there is no audible protest from
the children who drank the milk, because
probably some are dead and others can’t
scream sufficiently loud, and a baby in arms
can’t write an indignant letter to the news-
papers. The adult people have not made a
great fuss either, principally because use
hath made for them a property of easiness.
One gentleman remarked that the crawling
essence of decayeu body “fair hummed” in
hot weather, but generally there was an air
of toleration of an evil which makes the fas-
tidious man feel sick ; and all over Australia
are simply filthy necropoli to poison the
living. The point of view of the child has
not been canvassed at all. Australia, at
great expense, is importing population, much
of it of very mediocre quality of body
and mind; but most of its acts of
omission of hygienic observance are
directed towards, or at least result
in, the great mortality of the best immi-
grant of all—the little foreigner who arrives
per Stork. Tuberculous cows, murderous
milkmen, bread adulterators, sugar fakirs, in-
sanitary-dwelling rentlords —all these are his
assassins. And now his dead grandfather
rises from the grave in poison, and his long-
deceased aunt flows to the dairy to kill him.
* * * *
Nothing can be more ridiculous than the
spectacle of a country equipping itself with
sanitary science to destroy disease, and
maintaining hospitals for its cure, while
carefully sowing all the old, and possibly
some new, diseases. For germ and bacillus
seem to struggle to keep pace with modern
improvements and open new departments in
their murder store every year. Rontgen
rays, radium, the increasingly skilful use of
the knife, antitoxins of diphtheria and bu-
bonic, “606,” and the pitting of malignant
erysipelas against malignant cancer that
each may destroy the other —what are all
these but palliatives when we make treasure-
houses of the carcases of the dead hosts of
cancer and tubercule and diphtheria and bu-
bonic and syphilis, giving all this filth to a
clean earth that it may be fruitful of horror
and multiply too-early death! The earth
and the elements are great alchemists, and
the people who cling slavishly to burial as a
religious observance rely too much on the
laboratory, and overwork it. Because last
year’s dunghill is this year’s rose, the horrors
that clings to the festering body of old Two
Bottles Man, the celebrated convivant, are
forgotten ; the slow-exhaled corruption of the
corpse of the tanner, which will last you
nine years, are overlooked in the interests
of the most squalid of sentimentalities. The
Chinese keeps his dead in his house for 49
days, and for as long after as the astrologer
thinks necessary to ensure a lucky burial.
But he puts the body into a hermetically •
sealed coffer dam with quicklime ; while we
bury ours mostly in flimsy shells of pine
that burst under the gases of dissolution, so
that the living may be poisoned, and the
horrors of death continued in earth that
tries always to be clean. Even the aborigi-
nal knows better. Rarely will he
bury in the earth. Often he will elevate the
body to a platform that it may dry the more
quickly, though he makes little use of birds
in the Parsee manner. But we build hypo-
critically the granite sepulchre, topped by
the marble angel spreading his wings ; and
below the angel, and rotting in the dark, is
the filthy and abandoned house of all these
commemorated virtues, slowly reeking till it
leaves a husk and disarticulated bones.
Cremation would only be a step back to
the hygiene of the ancients, who were suffi-
ciently Pagan to be clean. Christianity,
cowering in its catacombs, had in those
catacombs to dispose of its dead, sealing them
up in pigeon-holes, and protected against
pestilence by the dry, open and absorbent
character of the tufaceous rock in which
these subterranean cities were excavated. By
the time Christianity became strong enough
to rise to the surface, burial had become a
habit; and the victors naturally destroyed
the Pagan practice of cremation, as they
frowned on the other clean Pagan habit of
the bath. Further, to these simple souls it
appeared that the Resurrection and the
Life (which was always to begin in a month
or a year) would scarcely be possible to a
handful of white ashes. Flesh which had
been through the bodies of worms, which had
been eaten by beasts, atomised by dynamite
or disintegrated by the slimes of the ocean,
could be reassembled on its bones by an
Omnipotence; but apparently that Om-
nipotence was powerless to reconstruct the
results of cremation! Thereby they foolishly
proved that they only half believed in
miracles —that they knew the Almighty’s
power to reclothe a skeleton, but doubted
His ability to reform from ash. And so it is
still. * * *
Then sentimentality—which talks of a
long, dreamless sleep in God’s acre—fails to
recognise that John Jones’s bones may be
evicted for a newer late-departed, though
John Jones has bought his grave in fee
simple; for an undertaker, like any other
tradesman, can do such things. In most
religious countries, such as the Philippines,
Christian burial is only temporary —as at
El Paco, where at intervals the Spanish
authorities throw out bones and skulls, by
the ton to make room for newer dead. Simi-
larly, in Gelsomino (Florence), the rich may
buy their graves in perpetuity, but the poor
or thrifty hire their graves for 10 or 20 or
30 years, and are thrown out at the expira-
tion of the lease. Yet the humorless people
who delight in love-making in cemeteries need
not be despoiled of the Sunday walk, the
reading of inscriptions, the passage through
the avenue of tombs, the admiration of the
wreath and the granite angel and the chubby
cherubim in trachyte, the gabbling of poetical
language by the parson for his fee of Bs. per
burial (hurried near the end as he sees the
next candidate for the Resurrection and the
Life coming slowly through the gate in a
gloomv hearse with all its funeral ravens
flapping on the top) ; nor need they miss the
idyll of the widow bringing fresh flowers . to
the departed, and preparing to take notice
of him who is just arriving. Memorial
visits to the urn might be made just as
well as to the festering grave; loving decora-
tion of the tomb of the dear ashes as of the
sepulchre of the dear corruption. The
mausolea of the departed great might, even
be erected in parks and gardens, in the .cen-
tres of the living, for to the living the ciner-
ary urn would offer no offence.
* * * *
Further, cremation is cheap. How many
widows and widowers and poor families suffer
long privations to pay exorbitant funeral
charges on the time-payment system? So
the cost of hiding the corpse makes life
harder for the survivors, while the hidden
body threatens the living with its corrup-
tion. In the interests of the poor, the grave,
with its trappings and its coffin and its
mourning coaches and its hearse with glass
sides, must go. The living are impoverished
and murdered by the dead. The earth is
overworked as a chemist. Let man help her
with a furnace. And it isn’t a fad for a
private member ; it’s a great and epoch-mak-
ing reform to be achieved by any Govern-
ment that has courage and means business.
Randolph Bedford.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Lost Legions.
According to the official figures published
last week, out of the 153,000 boys of be-
tween 14 and 17 years of age who, by virtue
of the Defence Act, should be undergoing
training, only 95,000-odd are actually so em-
ployed. Apart from those who have been
declared medically unfit, 55,910 are exempt
owing to their homes being five miles. or
more from any training centre. Australia’s
provision for its own defence is far too in-
adequate to permit of the sacrifice of this
army. Some means should, therefore, be
swiftly devised, and as swiftly translated
into law, by which these youths should be
enabled to do their duty by their country.
Perhaps the soundest scheme would be to
assemble all the exempted boys under canvas
and drill them for a set period every year.
It would pay the Commonwealth a hundred-
fold to provide the whole of the cash for
their travelling and living expenses during
(say) 14 days in each 12 months.
There may come a time when the youths
thus drawn into the defence net may mean
to Australia the difference between safety
and national extinction. Incidentally, this
step would do away with an anomalous
state of things whereby J. Smith, Jun.,
who lives 4£ miles from the training area,
lias to go through with the specified number
of drills every year, or be classed as an
“inefficient” (a circumstance that imposes
on him the duty of putting in his drills, after
he has reached the age of 26), while J.
Brown, Jun., who inhabits an adjoining
street, is not called on to do any military
service' whatsoever. It is quite conceivable
that, if the position remains unchanged,
parents who for various insane reasons are
averse to having their sons trained, will de-
liberately take to frustrating the area officer
by settling outside his sphere of influence
a practice that will considerably swell the
already large number of the exempt, r ur-
thermore, the State might well make things
somewhat easier for the lads who attend
drills under difficulties. It is rough on a
youngster who has been earning a strenuous
living in a shop or on a cart all day to have
to walk three or four miles to drill in the
evening. In the case of those who live re-
mote from trams or trains, some provision
might be made for transporting them to the
training places free of charge ; or they might
be given the option of joining the annual
camp above-mentioned. It is as essential
for the welfare of the nation that the mili-
tary service should become popular (which
it can only do if it is run on equitable lines)
as it is that every available unit should be
drafted into it.

The False-Alarm Riot.
The “Liberal” press of N. S. Wales saw, last
week, in the Lithgow strike, what looked like
a chance to instruct the nervous, elderly-
female brand of elector in regard to the
Menace of Labor; and so, on Wednesday, the
Sydney Telegkaph came out this way :
RIOT.
VIOLENCE AT LITHGOW.
RAID ON BLAST FURNACE.
POLICE INJURED.
REFUGE IN ENGINE-ROOM.
A “violent mob” was said to have been in
“full possession” of the town, and of the whole
of the Ironworks (barring the blast furnace)
from 10 p.m. the previous night till the time
the Telegraph went to press. On Thurs-
day the S. M. Herald printed the follow-
ing :
ANARCHY.
EIGHT HOURS OF CHAOS.
RIOTS RENEWED.
THE IRONMASTER BESIEGED.
STRIKERS ARRESTED.
POLICE CHARGE THE MOB.
Also, it announced :
Lithgow is in a state of turmoil. Anything might
happen. The mob is an ugly mood once more. The
police and strikers have come into conflict, batons
have been used freely, and sore heads have resulted.
Arrests have been made.
On Friday the same paper’s scare-headings
ran :
BITTER CONFLICT.
DYNAMITE OUTRAGE.
HOSTILE DEMONSTRATION.
WAR ON WOMEN.
And, lower down, in narrating how the
reckless and bloodthirsty strikers tried to
get at the “loyalists” (presumably with
murderous intent), it printed this:—
The police stood in front of the crowd, and draw-
ing their batons barred further progress. The mob
seethed and tried to break through. Meanwhile the
loyalists made good their escape. The police, like
the rearguard of a retreating army, followed slowly
in the wake of the fugitives with batons half-drawn
ready for any emergency. The mob was in a de-
cidedly ugly humor. “They are spoiling for fight,”
said a senior officer, “and, by , they will get
it.”
At the top of the town Inspector Goulder, having
seen his charges safely housed, formed up his men
and marched towards the town. The strikers fol-
lowed, hooting. The inspector halted his squad, and
ordered the crowd to disperse. They refused.
“Scatter them,” said the chief, and the police, with
drawn truncheons, charged. The crowd fled in all
directions.
Columns of this embittered and inflammatory
stuff —the purely imaginative or Yellow
Journalese presentment of the case —were
unloaded on the public by both the morning
dailies. And then, on Saturday night, Syd-
ney Evening News —which has no labor
leanings, and is hardly the paper to neglect
a legitimate sensation —came out with the
plain, drab truth, as follows :
Superintendent Johnson/ who is in charge of the
police at Lithgow, in his official report denies that
the police either drew or attempted to draw their
batons. There had been some interference, he says,
with the houses of free laborers, but not with any
grievous effect. The, worst was the discharge of a
dynamite cap, which, the superintendent writes, did
no further damage than to crack a pane of glass.
People of an analytical cast of mind—all
those, in fact, with sufficient intelligence to
understand that a mob of genuine rioters and
anarchists could not be in “full possession”
of any town, for one hour, much less eight,
without murder, robbery, outrage and in-
finite damage being done —appreciated from
the start that 99 per cent, of the “Liberal”
yarns concerning the Lithgow chaos were
untrue. But these crude tarradiddles are
not designed for that class. It seems that
half a dozen mischievous yahoos wrecked an
unguarded motor-car; that some boys threw
a number of articles belonging to “free la-
borers” into a dam; and that an unspecified
practical joker cracked a pane of glass by
exploding a dynamite cap. On these hap-
penings—little worse, when all is said and
done, than the offences that are perpetrated
during a “rag” at any well-conducted British
University—the dailies built up a story
which caused one of the visiting operatic
stars to say: “If the Lithgow strike had
occurred in South America it would have
been called a revolution.” And, of course,
if the affair had been anything like it was
represented to be in the “Liberal” press,
it would have been a revolution. The fact
that local Labor troubles never are what
they seem in the “Liberal” press is one of
the* strongest reasons why Labor needs a
daily of its own.

A Bonfire Under a Melting Pot.
The taking of the recent census has brought
in its train a call for a re-adjustment of elec-
toral boundaries in Australia. Queensland
lias already refixed its electoral subdivisions ;
N. S. Wales is about to do the same ; and the
Australian Parliament proposes to follow
suit. This new alignment of a few of the
electoral frontiers calls attention to the
whole matter of legislative and adminis-
trative partition. If a large map of Aus-
tralia were taken to-day, and the whole of'
the various boundaries were shown, and the
authority or purpose for which each of them
was set up were plainly stated thereon, the
result would look like the work of a de-
mented'centipede dipped in ink, and fleeing
from the horrors of the Inquisition: For
legislative purposes there are the Federal
electorates, State Upper and Lower House
electorates, and the wards and ridings of the
various councils and shires. For land pur-
poses there are divisions, counties, districts
and parishes; then there are police, postal
and defence districts, and, to boot, a series
of lines marking out the sphere of a host of
sheep boards, cattle boards, rabbit boards,
wild-dog boards, and the like. (This is say-
ing nothing of the fact that every sect has
divided up the country privately into allot-
ments for the supply of spiritual grass to its
flocks.) So much for the map—but it is the
citizen who counts. He is in three or four
electoral divisions whose boundaries do not
coincide. This entads three or four roll
collections. When money is badly wanted
for defence, closer settlement and decent
footpaths, the saving of money that one roll
collection would mean is worth making.
Take Smith, for instance. He is on the
Brown ward roll of the Robinson muni-
cipality ; on the Jones State electoral roll
for the Lower House, and on the Jenkins roll
for the Upper House. When the Federal
roll is collected, he is in Brigg’s electorate
for the Reps., and he votes for the Senate as
an inhabitant of the State of Jobson. His
letters go to Simpson post office, his chil-
dren to Ciiolmondeley school; and he regis-
ters his dog in Noiieolk-Howaro police dis-
trict. His house or farm is in Plantagenet
county, in Percy division, and the parish of
Jlezekiaii. His boys are trainees in Bill-
Jim military division; and the Jack Board,
the Tom Trust and the Harry something
else either tax him or register him, or collect
him. The whole matter calls for a good roar-
ing bonfire, on top of which a decent-sized
melting-pot should be placed. A few highly-
qualified surveyors, a few intelligent under-
secretaries, and a few statisticians, with a
Federal Judge as president, should be put on
the job. Halfpenny municipalities, two-
penny parliaments, and a job lot of nuis-
ances would serve for the bonfire, and the
rest might go into the pot. If the experts
cared to put the old measures of weight,
capacity and value in the fire, and the metric
system in the pot, all the better. The result
should be a scientific partition of Australia
for all legislative, administrative and other
purposes. The present jumble is a satire on
the public intelligence.

The Wowser and His Figures.
The Wowser tribe in Victoria is working
hard to get “Scripture-reading in State,
schools” made into a war-cry for the coming
State elections. Certainly some elections
have been fought on a very poor war-cry;
but, so far as this paper’s recollection goes,
nothing so utterly rotten as this has ever
carried a party to victory. At present, any
accredited preacher can enter any State
school in Victoria and teach the Scripture
to all the youngsters whose parents do not
object. In a fair number of schools this is
done ; and there is such a plenitude of rever-
ends that it is possible in every school. But
the parsons want the work taken off their
hands and given to the school-teachers, who
have more than enough to do already. Also,
the teachers are of many religions and, pos-
sibly, of many different kinds of lack of re-
ligion, and in numerous cases object to
Bible-teaching. The Wowser folk have been
trying to show that “the people” want their
system. A few years ago they got a refer-
endum held on the question; and the result
was a huge majority for the main-
tenance of the present secular arrangement.
During the past few months the Scripture-
school push has been holding little referen-
dums of its own, and it has produced some
astonishing figures. In every case the votes
showed a majority of something like five to
one in favor of Scripture-reading in the
schools. But in the middle of their cam-
paign, a real election happened to follow a
Wowser election. This was at Bendigo East.
The Wowsers had published the result of
their poll, which showed that well over 2000
electors would support the candidate who
favored their views. Then came the real
test, and the Bible candidate went down, and
the unfindableness of the 2000 was something-
terrible. So the average candidate is now
looking sideways at the whole business; and
Parson Nicholson, who is conducting the
Scripture campaign, has had to publish nine
different reasons why the Bendigo East elec-
tion went wrong. But the tenth reason —that
the parsonical referendum wasn’t worth the
paper it was written on —quite escaped Mr.
Nicholson.

The Tired Department of a Tired Minister.
The annual report of Victoria’s Acting-In-
spector-General of Prisons for last year is
a sloppy document, which gives a quantity
of undigested figures, and very little else.
The State’s daily average number of prison- -
ers has decreased during the year from 868 to
855, but the net cost of keeping them has
gone up from £37 9s. to £4O 18s. 3d. per
head. As the gross cost last year was
£5B 4s. Bd. per nead, it follows that these
855 healthy adults, employed at “hard
labor,” have each contributed the large sum
of £l7 6s. sd. during the year towards their
keep, or 6s. Bd. a week! About this lament-
able result of so much alleged toil, the In-
spector-General says nothing. Numbers of
prisoners are employed at the making of
boots, mats, blankets and wire-netting, and as
these articles are either sold at market
prices, or used by other departments and
credited at trade values, it would ap-
pear as though there is shockingly
bad organisation somewhere. And the
same sort of mess is threatened in
connection with the indeterminate sentences
experiment. The administration realises
that something is wrong somewhere, and re-
commends that the system be applied only
to persons whose previous sentences aggre-
gate 10, or at least seven, years. But that
shows that the administration totally mis-
understands the policy of indeterminate sen-
tences. The principle is that the youth who
has made a few false steps, and shows signs
of continuing in the downward track, or the
confirmed criminal who has no intention of
living except by crime, may be taken, in ;
hand, taught a trade, given habits of self-
respect and self-reliance, and discharged as
soon as there is a reasonable prospect of his.
continuing' straight—and no sooner. There
are pickpockets and spielers in Melbourne,
notoriously “living on the game,” whose
half-dozen or so convictions wouldn’t total
three yearsyet, apparently, the Inspector-
General would have them remain at large
till thev are old men before attempting to
reform them. The trouble with Victoria’s
penal system is that no Minister will tackle
reform in earnest. Bent started the wire-
netting industry at Pentridge, and that
ended his effort. Murray has exhaustion ir
Bonnie Dundee
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dundee is a town of only one industry. Its pre-
sent wealth is entirely dependent upon jute. . Nowa-
days, with the increased Eastern competition and
the consequent cutting of wages, women are the
principal workers, although men are largely em-
ployed at a low wage. This movement, displacing
men and introducing women into the mills and fac-
tories, is still going on, and is largely responsible
for the condition of the homes. Matters would be
worse than they are, and greater poverty would exist,
were it not for the fact that the cream of the youth-
ful population is annually skimmed off for Canada
and the United States. . . . The children in Dundee
seem to be worse off than even the children in the
crowded slums of London. The babies of three and
four were solemn and white-faced. During my
whole stay in Dundee I never saw any child playing.
... I asked a nurse about the extraordinary con-
trast between the magnificent new babies and the
stunted children. She said that the babies began
to go off the day the mother went to work at
the mill.— Violet Cecil, in an article, “Some Scot-
tish Homes,” National Review, June, 1911.

The Habit of Being of the Same Opinion
as Wade.
These are times when the ordinary citizen
is almost tempted to wonder if Premier
McGowen, of N.S.W., is a of any
serious value. A plain and honest, but
rather dull man is James McGowen, and
he is a confirmed dweller in a rut. He is
strangely Tory in many things, and in some
things he treats his own party programme
with a contempt that no old squatter could
excel. His great ambition seems to be to
keep his following as purely a pick
and_ shovel party as possible, and to avoid
having any policy of his own on afEairs that
are not exclusively pick and shovel. Where
it is possible, as in the case of the Australian
capital question, to be parochial and anti-
Australian, it is his general principle to
stick as closely as circumstances admit to
the uncouth Gregory Wade. There is a
limitation-of-borrowing plank in the Labor
platform, apparently placed there as a piece
of ornamental hypocrisy, for ex-Minister
Nielsen was allowed to throw mud at this
alleged Labor ideal from the first day of
its adoption, and McGowen himself prefers
his enemies’ habit of gigantic loan-mongering
to his own professed doctrine of economy.
In this matter he treats his own alleged prin-
ciples as if they were dirt, and shows him-
self almost the maddest borrower on the
State s records. Also, there is professedly
\ decentralisation principle of some sort on
the Labor bill of fare, and on this subject
Premier McGowen spoke as follows last
Monday (the reports in the two morning
papers were identical, so there is some rea-
son to suppose that they were for once
threatened with accuracy :
We ought to have nine-tenths of our people in
the interior, growing, or making, or obtaining our
products, and the other tenth in the ONE centre,
handling those products.
After that, and despite the remark about
having nine-tenths of the people in the in-
terior, Premier McGowen (unless he can
explain himself away) had better retire
from the decentralisation business. He still
apparently sticks to the policy of dragging
all the State’s business to 4< ONE centre,”
and that is just the policy which makes it
impossible to get nine-tenths of the popula-
tion into the interior. The reason is that it
burdens a huge proportion of the interior,
including some of the most fertile sections
thereof, with so many superfluous and costly
leagues or scores of leagues of railway car-
riage that it hardly pays to live in these
places, despite their fertility. McGowen
apparently forgets his theoretical belief in
decentralisation, just as he has quite for-
gotten his theoretical belief in economy, so
hereabouts he throws in his lot again with
\Vade in the good old familiar fashion.
Apparently, the habit of throwing in his lot
with Wade has become so fixed as to be
almost incurable.

IT is time some one took Henley, M.L A
for Burwood (N.S.W.), firmly but kindly
by the coat-tail and urged him not to use
superlatives so freely. Just because he and
his push don t like to see Willis in the
chair he lias been splashing round like the
teet ot an elephant in deep mud, and holler-
mg re the great betrayal” and “an out-
raged Parliament,” and uttering verbal sky-
scrapers like this :
There is no parallel to this that can be cited in
any free country. The sacred rights of hon. mem-
bers have been thrown down, trampled on, and de-
Assembly N * Way unheard of in anl J deliberative
England used to be called a free country, yet
the great Oliver Cromwell had a way of
not allowing any selected member to sit
unless he first received Cromwell’s own
certificate, and no elected member got the
certificate unless he promised to think the
same way as Cromwell. Once at least the
Protector simplified matters by electing the
whole Parliament himself, and it was his
habit to get tired even of the certificated
House of Commons, and to chase it away a
few weeks after it met. When there are
stacks of precedents like that to refer to
Henley s language is either shameful ignor-
ance or premeditated hysterics. Superla-
tives are the last refuge of an inferior class
of mind. Witness the case of the old-time
Queensland editor who started his foaming
leader in this wise:—
An outrage, which for heartless cruelty has no
“? history, not ev«i in the bloodstained
days of Nero was witnessed in the drill-shed last
night, when B company of the Caneville volunteers
was disbanded for inefficiency. This unexampled
crime, etc., etc.

newspapers! The Bulletin cannot recall
any similar article in the Argus at the time
when the goldbugs in the Transvaal paid for
the absolutely unpardonable Jamieson raid

“Trovatore” got the first look-in, with loud
applause, at Rome, in January, 1853;
“Traviata” got the boot at its first appear-
ance in Venice a few weeks later, being
nearly killed by the Violetta of fat Mrs.
Gonatelli, at that time one of the stoutest
women on or off the boards. When the
doctor warbled to the huge Violetta, that her
hours were numbered because she was
wasting away in consumption, the riotous
laughter of the audience extinguished the
mu .® lc - “ Is th e fault mine or the actors’?”
wailed Verdi next day. Tenor Graziani, the
Alfredo of that first fatal cast, croaked like
a frog in consequence of a heavy cold ; and
Rantone v aresi, offended at singing in a
part he considered* top small, was too indiffer-
ent to save his duet with fleshy Violetta.
It was the actors’ fault. “Traviata” tri-
umphed everywhere else, and Christine Nils-
son, who made her debut in Paris in 1864,
as the unhappy heroine, set the city in a
blaze with the charm of its vocal color.

Melba showed faultless judgment in choos-
ing the richly-trimmed music of Violetta’s
impassioned part for her first appear-
ance in the current season of grand
opera in Sydney. Six hundred sove-
reigns worth of silver-plate glittered on
the tables of Violetta’s banquet hall when
the Australian Nightingale sent her first
ripple of song across the footlights with
priceless technique. The elixir of youth is
bottled up in that marvellous voice-box. The
velvety roundness of tone is about 18 years
of age—a precocious 18, with the experience
of 20 years added on to give it ballast.
Melba made the sensation of the night, by
walking rapidly across the stage, trilling in
ascending scale as she went off, and disap-
pearing in the region of a silvery top note.
The mere canary stands still on its perch
to give its vocal somersaults a chance. Only
the skylark can beat its wings and send an
ecstasy of song into the heavens at the same
moment. In “Traviata,” Melba’s histrionic
art is only second to her faultless voice pro-
duction. John McCormack shakes a rich,
lyric tenor, and has the added charms of
youth, good looks and a manly stage pres-
ence. A young artist—John isn’t more than
-?“T. e 1S already getting huge dividends out
of his gold-mine throat. Rosina Buckman,
too florid in style for the concert platform,
finds her feet in grand opera. As Flora, the
-A.ustrjlli3.il held her own in the foreign
aviary. Baritone Scandiani made the com-
paratively small part of Germont a triumph.
Magnificently staged, and dressed in gor-
geous plumage, “La Traviata” is unlikely to
be eclipsed by any other production during
the opera season at Her Majesty’s, for Verdi
was master of the Italian school of composi-
tion in its most popular style, and Melba is
the greatest exponent of the Italian art of
musical decoration.
* * * *
“Faust” followed “Traviata.” The former
introduced the second prima-donna, the
fair, handsome Russian, Korolewicz-
Wayda—of course in the role of
Marguerite. She did not lay an in-
stantaneous hold upon her crowded audi-
ence, and the ease of her singing method
seemed to be carried a shade too far; but
she developed the tragic part with such
dramatic power, and the high notes of her
voice were so wonderfully pure, that she had
that section of Pitt-street at her mercy lon°-
before she came to a sudden end in her straw
manger at 11.5, or thereabouts. John
McCormack was the tenor, who, after the
manner of stage tenors, was the cause of
the impressionable lady’s passionate joy and
fatal distress. Again and again he roused
