the audience to very real enthusiasm —-the
enthusiasm that won’t wait for the orchestra
to finish its music, but bursts out of bounds
at the singer’s last note. The flood was par-
ticularly impetuous after the ardent “Salve
Dimora” solo. With all his opportunities
Mephistopheles can very rarely have pro-
vided himself with a better voice than
Edmund Burke supplies—a fine, ringing,
sonorous bass, full of expression; and in the
only light touch in the piece, where Meph.
plucks the mature gooseberry that threatens
to spoil his plot to put Marguerite at the
mercy of the rejuvenated Faust, he showed
also a rich, sardonic humor. Miss Voluntas-
Ranzenberg, Rosina Buckman, and Zanelli
(as Valentin, the distraught brother of Mar-
guerite), were other highly-satisfactory mem-
bers of the cast. Angelini was the con-
ductor, an anguished Angelini when the
chorus wasn’t where it should have been,
and the brass band that played the soldiers
on for their great chorus got away from the
orchestra. But when the generous Mar-
guerite-Wayda, after being herself called
forward half a dozen times, finally brought
Angelini for her bowing companion, the en-
thusiasm probably convinced the gentleman
with the extraordinary forehead of hair that
he had anyhow the sympathy of an audience
that refused to remember the blot in the
presence of a great picture.
#* * *
Miss Ethel Irving is still playing Stella
Ballantyne in “The Witness for the Defence,”
at Sydney Criterion; and her stage-husband
will continue to die a well-merited death till
further notice.
** * *
“The Waltz Dream” was revived at Syd-
ney Theatre Royal on Saturday night, with
many changes in the cast of the melodious
comedy of erotics. Miss Olive Godwin sings
Fanny Dango’s old part of Franzi, and sings
it well, but dances it indifferently. Reginald
Roberts is as tuneful and strenuous as ever
as Niki; and Miss Florence Young (Princess
Helena) looks regal and sings superbly. On
the other hand, the low comedy business of
Langford Kirby (Count Lothar) and Victor
Prince (Joachim) is somewhat tiresome. For
one thing, there is too much of it; for an-
other, the comedians work too hard —laugh-
ter-making being one of the few things on
earth that is not advantaged by dogged
effort. Frank Green and Max Baur do
sound work, and Miss Dorothy Brunton
makes a delightful little band-girl.
** * *
“The Old Woman Who Lived in a Shoe”
is still making the rounds of Sydney suburbs
under the alternate management of “Father”
Liddiard and treasurer Coates. They take
week about, and while one is keeping back
the rush of big audiences that want to
crowd into comparatively small halls, the
other is recovering from the bruises. Coates
reports that at Ashfield the movable ticket-
office and two large, fresh policemen were
overturned in the first rush after the doors
were opened. The policemen must have
been stamped underground out of sight, be-
cause he never saw them again, although he
looked in the nearest bar.
** * *
On Friday night at the Palace (Sydney)
William Anderson discharged “The Man
from Outback” without a caution, and on
Saturday gave “The Christian” a job to
steer Glory Quayle from all the beautiful
and joyous things in life that the Wowser
hates. The packed house passed a unani-
mous vote at the end of each act, and at
other frequent intervals, to the 'effect that
the said Christian deserved his billet, and
ought to be kept at it as long as possible
for the benefit of Wowsers and pagans in
general, and the box office in particular.
Roy Redgrave’s dramatisation of Hall Caine’s
story differs in several respects from former
stagings of the piece in Sydney, and is un-
doubtedly an improvement on most; and his
own long familiarity with the part of John
Storm enables him to hold that combination
of emotions from slopping over. Eugenie
Duggan, too, as Glory, restrained that young
hotblood from galloping at the wrong' turn,
and gave a brainy interpretation of the “kiss-
me-John” episode. Edmund Duggan, as
Father Enderby, and Bert Bailey, as Arch-
deacon Wealthy, could be hired out to any
short-handed Gospel factory as the genuine
article. Indeed, The Bulletin regrets that
the election of the new Bishop of Bathurst
was over before it had a chance of seeing
Bailey as the archdeacon. “The Christian”
may be seen by parsons, pickpockets, philan-
thropists, students of human nature, semi-
drunks, sports, choir-girls, “goofi-night” girls
and even politicians till further notice.
Clement Wragge, the tall, spare lecturer,
thumped the butt-end of his wand on the
darkened stage of Sydney Y.M.C.A. Hall,
and the man at the lantern threw a picture
of a heavy snowstorm on the wall. At least,
it looked like a snowstorm; in reality, it
was a photograph of the heavens taken
through the giant telescope of the Lick Ob-
servatory. All the flakes were stars —un-
known worlds that glimmer away in Space
beyond the reach of the feeble human eye
that can’t even read the account of the
tragedy in the Evening Bloodstain unless
it wears spectacles. A lurid slide which
seemed to represent a blowfly in a blazing
warehouse turned out to represent a com-
parison of this earth and the blazing plains
of hydrogen in the sun. Two or three whirls
of smoky vapor were sun spots—terrific-
storms which, according to Inclement, have
a chest measurement of a few hundred thou-
sand miles, and send out waves which dis-
organise the telegraph services, and irritate
the volcanoes on this suspended and heavily-
taxed pill of ours. Wragge himself is 'a
cataract of tremendous facts and theories:
and most of his time he is standing on tiptoe,
pointing up-up-up into Space with a long, thin
arm, and talking reverently in a sharp, high
key of that snowstorm of which we form
one flake. Then suddenly he drops his voice;
the shower of snowflake world is pushed
aside; and a picture bouquet of flowers or
a dish of fruit is presented to the stunned
listeners as the simple, every-day gift of the
magnificent, blazing old sun—whom he
classes lightly as a second-class sky-cruiser.
The lecturer warms up his cold science nicely
on the stove of enthusiasm, and serves it
highly colored. A stimulating and unusual
entertainment which deals with immense
things, and should be attended by all State
I nghters, mayors and aldermen for the good
of their souls.

m* * *
McLallen and Carson, a mixed team of
roller-skaters with a smart act, are the
latest people to show their card at Sydney
Tivoli. McLallen, the male member, does
a clog dance on his rollers while perched on
top of a stage windmill; but he doesn’t take
it so seriously as a middle-aged performer
who appeared some years ago. That artist
clacked slowly and at great length on the
slate roof oTf a small Eiffel tower, wearing
a heavy black moustache, a Lord Fauntleroy
costume, and the proud smile of a conquering
hero. The “Dapper Dandy,” Louie Tracy,
is still imitating naval and military gaits with
great fidelity to life, and singing a couple of
gallery-catching songs in between ; Clarke and
Mostol (comedians and dancers), Alf Holt
(mimic), Sydney Bach (entertainer), and
Carlton Max (ventriloquist) share a heap of
applause between them; and Fred Bluett is
doing good business with a new song.
** * *
The ice at the Glaciarium has been broken,
and the fragments have probably been sent
to the Japanese expedition which is looking
for the South Pole under the lee of Sydney’s
South Head fort; and on Saturday night
West’s pictures move on from the Princess
Theatre, and return to their old home.
“Enoch Arden” is at present taking a - spell
at West’s, after his long travels; and
Napoleon is back on the Isle of Elba. On
Saturday “The Circus Girl,” a pictorial
drama, will begin flicking for a short season ;
and there will be a full programme of mis-
cellaneous films besides.
** * *
The scissoring of the plot takes a little
of the shine off “Enoch Arden,” now show-
ing at Spencer’s Lyceum. But what is left
is excellent, and the effect is improved by
the recitation of excerpts, Lawrence Camp-
bell supplying the necessary voice and other
accessories. The departure of Enoch, his
stay on the island, and his return are ad-
mirably pictured. The programme also in-
cludes “Yachting at Cowes”; a good animal
picture, “The Circus Stowaway”; several
comic films that raise the loud laugh, or the
gentle giggle ; and the usual dashes through
various foreign parts where the dirt, vege-
tables, liquid, etc., are more artistically ar-
ranged than usual.
** * *
The National Amphitheatre treated itself
last week to a new acrobatic team, “Les
Petits Totos.” They do the usual business,
but it is understood to take on an aclded
charm by reason of the diminutiveness of the
performers. Jordan and Harvey, and
Jules Garrison and his Roman maids are
still on deck; likewise the First Part and a
perfect galaxy of brief-frocked female talent.

STECK.
THE STECK BOUDOIR GRAND is deservedly a
favorite. To encompass the tonal beauty and
sonority of a Grand Piano in a space scarcely
larger than that occupied by an Upright is indeed
an achievement.
Measuring but sft. s 7in. in length, it renders a
Grand Piano of the highest character available, even
for rooms of limited size.
The STECK, njore than any other piano, possesses
a rich mellowness of tone, a sympathetic singing
quality, Which makes it not only the ideal instru-
ment for the great artist in concert, but also the
best piano for the home.

* * * *
Maybe the unimposing outfit of the royal
Court of Denmark was partly accountable
for the slump in attendance at Her Majesty’s
(Melb.) after the first night of “Hamlet.”
The corpse-laden tragedy has been so much
better staged in the past. This revival is
a feast for neither the eye nor the ear, and
what is best in the performance is of least
importance to the largest section of Shaks-
peare’s professed admirers. A magnetic
Hamlet would be excused for lapsing into
incoherency in his long, hysterical rants or
sober-spoken meditations ; but H. B. Irving
has no personal magnetism. He is but an
intermittent reflection of his father’s
individuality, with an occasional echo
of his father’s vocal mannerisms.
Yet in his quiet conversational scenes,
H. B. Irving has great moments. His
dealing with Horatio and Marcellus, after
the Ghost has left him, almost convinces one
of the genuineness of that self-admiring
spectre. In nis talks with Itosencrantz and
Guildenstern the actor becomes the embodi-
ment of Hamlet. On tne other hand, his
“Get thee to a nunnery” outbreak is tire-
some. He weakens as he grows louder, and
his elocution is nearly always bad. For
that matter, the Player Queen —Miss Irene
Browne —speaks and acts her lines better
than anybody else in Denmark, bar Polonius,
whose virtues of diction are strictly Wowser-
like, anyhow. Horatio is insipid, the King
and Queen are uninteresting people, who
show no signs of animation whilst Ophelia
is displaying her madness, and the page,
Osric, needs only a bunch or two of ribbons
to make him pass for Barnaby Rudge. Also,
the ladies of the Court are pathetic in their
cheap finery and demeanor. Viewing it as
a whole, the dismal tragedy does not appear
to this commentator to be well done, nor
half well done. It is worth considering, be-
cause of the gleams of excellence in Irving’s
interpretation of the name part, but popu-
larity wants cause more various than this —
as the Bard might have said.
** * *
“The Speckled Band,” which came on
from Adelaide last week, unwound itself at
the Princess’s on Saturday, and showed all
the successful qualities of the second-rater.
The most vital character is the murder-
planner, who sends a snake to do the job
that less resourceful people do with a pinch
of lead or a grain or so of poison ; and in
Gaston Mervale’s hands this gentleman is a
devil to delight the morbid and appal the
meek. Mervale no doubt overdoes it; never-
theless, his method of corroborating speech
with eloquent hands is often effective and
picturesque. In Rodgers, the butler,_ Athol-
wood presents another and a peevish old
Jaikes; W. Desmond’s Sherlock Holmes has
little personality; and Miss Dix’s portrait
of Enid Stonor suffers a good deal in the
same way.
** * *
“The French Spy”—a Franco-Russian
bellodrama of Cockney parentage —will keep
the Andersonian oof-pot boiling at the King’s
for one more week. It grows depressing
towards the close, when Miss Ross, having
blinded her eyes to keep them from spying
any further, wanders around Paris in charge
of an Irish fiddler, who retires from observa-
tion before starting to play a jig. Luckily,
Baker, from the British Embassy, saves the
poor blind girl’s Honor, which was threat-
ened by a Russian noble during the fiddler’s
absence. But the worst might have befallen
her if Baker had not previously disguised
himself as a holy friar, in order to unnerve
the hated Muscovite. This seems to pave
the way to a happy ending, for the fiddler is
left at liberty to marry a bright young
music-hall maiden (Miss Rae Cooper) with
a sympathetic speaking voice and a figure
that —In tights—amounts to a triumph of
Nature assisted by cotton-wool.

familiar operatic number —“Casta Diva
and a wide assortment of the songs she sings

New arrivals at the Opera House last
Saturday were the pretty Saxony sisters,
who come late in the year with their Apache
dance; and Victor Martin, described as a

the last week of it is not coming too soon.

There are still some places in Melbourne
where an irresponsible policeman might lire
his revolver without hitting a picture
theatre, but neglected fields for enterprise
are being captured every month. Ihis week
two new picturies are spreading their screens
to catch the public eye. The new Lyric at
Prahran has produced an offspring m the
newer Lyric at Brunswick, and the Amal-
gamated Pictures Ltd. has opened a photo,
playhouse at Fitzroy. There seems to be a
time coming when every street will have its
own flickergraphic establishment, and each
of them will be positively the best.

The Literary Theatre in Adelaide is grow-
ing more “popular” in its choice of plays.
“The Earth,” for instance, its latest selec-
tion, was announced as the opening piece for
N’York’s sumptuous new playhouse last
month. J. B. Fagan is the author, and he
has built up a romance on politics and
the press. Felix Janion (who started life as
Jahnsen or thereabouts) owns about two-
thirds of the newspapers in Britain, and the
widely-read Earth in particular. He re-
gards himself as a public power for good.
Trevena, a Cabinet Minister, thinks this
domination a public danger. Both men are
allowed to put their view fairly and fully—
mostly over a cigar. The end of it, of course,
is the triumph of (easy) Virtue, and a final
suggestion that some day the country will
resent being taught what to think,' and be-
lieve, and do, by a handful of rich newspaper
owners. Miss Jean Robertson, as a reckless
countess, did the best work she has yet
shown. Douglas Walsh and Wilfrid Neill,
masterly amateurs both, “held” their audi-
ences; and, in a long cast, the only other
chances went to George Whittle, as the
cheerful subordinate who uses the gimlet.
* * * *
Other first-in-Australia shows in Ade-
laide are supplied by A. R. Shepard, who
essays to make the King s a rival to the
Tivoli, and gets his artists straight off the
mail-boat. The Jtoyal is doing its best to
crowd on to its small stage the great scenic
effects of “The Whip,” railway smash, race-
course, and all. At the Tivoli, Harris and
Vernon put in some moderate singing, tair
talking and superb dancing; Francis and
Alfred are delightful comedy-jugglers, and
Arthur Croxon, with a bagful of tricks,
scores most heavily with a falsetto voice-
production. Alcide Capitaine and the Binns
pair (one of these is a fine actor) are near
the end of their stay. West’s Wondergraph
and the Empire have a new competitor; as
though there were not enough picture-houses
already, the only remaining skating-rink has
put away .its rollers and taken to films. And
tne public seems never to weary of support-
ing them all.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“S.S.” : H. B. Irving’s effort as Hamlet strikes me
as an absolute reversal of the legitimate methods of
art. It is not a struggle to adapt a personality to the
Shaksbaconian character, but an heroic and most
painstaking attempt to make Haifllet fit Irving.
Irving is not sunk in Hamlet so much as Hamlet is
sunk in Irving. Then while the actor insists point-
edly on Hamlet’s sanity, this appears quite the mad-
dest Dane I have ever s#en. Hamlet’s very sus-
picions that others doubt his sanity assume the form
of an obsession ; for no one is so confident of his own
sanity as a confirmed megalomaniac.
“Stargazer”: Critics here, there and everywhere
have been complimenting the various gentlemen who
manipulated “Arms and the Man” on their skill in
retaining in “The Chocolate Soldier” the wit and
vivacity of Shaw. I envy those critics their devilish
perspicacity. I have to confess with sorrow that I
could find no trace of Shaw’s sparkling fancy left in
the adaptation. Of course there are some remnants
of the plot of “Arms and the Man.” A soldier
escaping from the foe, invades a young lady’s bed-
room ; but for the rest it is not Shaw, and doesn’t
remotely resemble the whimsicality of the bright
Irishman who is accused of imitating everybody,
and whom everybody imitates, but who remains
unique. All the Shawian characters are vulgarised
in this opera. The adapters have. made Sergius a
commonplace braggart and their Bluntschli is only
an impudent ass, while the father becomes a con-
ventional low-comedy type, and the women are
robbed of all individuality. “The Chocolate Sol-
dier,” may be almost everything else that is
claimed for it, but. alas, it isn’t Shaw.
Randolph Bedford’s season at Melbourne Princess’s,
commencing on September 23, will run through the
racing carnival, and get the patronage of the visit-
ing crowd that can better appreciate the color and
characterisation of Australian drama than Melbourne
residents. Bedford will produce two plays, and each
of them —especially the one to follow “The Pluck
Up”—is said to be much better built than his
“White Australia” of yore. The slap-dash amateur
dramatist has acquired craftsmanship. By the way,
the opening piece is to carry the full length of the
title of the story on which it is founded—“ The Lady
of the ‘Pluck Up.’ ” Bedford had intended to drop
the first half of the title, and leave the picture-
posters to proclaim the presence of a lady in the
case, but it seems that the first duty of theatrical
advt. is to tickle the interest of womankind. “The
Pluck Up,” short and simple, would not have ap-
pealed to the sex. ,
Frank Gardiner, the man with that impassive bit-
ting Bull countenance which is such an asset to the
born poker player, looms up again in the cable news.
A Court at Paris has ordered him to account to
Carrie Swain, once of “The Tomboy” show, for
the profits of their theatrical partnership. The
awful patience of the man with the Sitting Bull
countenance is evidenced by the fact that it was 15
years before he opened his impassive face to tell the
lady that there was a flaw in their marriage contract.
The evening-dress person in the meloscream generally
gives away the show inside 24 hours.
Frank Tyars, now 63 years old, is a relic of the
original Irving Co., and his staid, matter-of-fact
performance of King Claudius in “Hamlet dates
back to 1877, when he played the part on the second
tour which Irving made through Britain in a star
capacity. Previously Pinero had taken the part on
tour.
“Mime”: Concerning imitation. London stage-
managers enforce it. If an actor like Grossmith or
Huntley makes a hit, the touring companies are
made iip. The performers are instructed to see the
originals, and imitate them in every line. There
have been instances in Australia lately of comedians
being spoilt by having to imitate the forced manner
and speech of Huntley rhrough long runs. Very dif-
ferent was Bancroft’s stage-management. When Ihe
School for Scandal” was rehearsed, he said to Hare,
“I don’t want Phelps’s Sir Peter, but Hare s ; and
that actor cursed his unlucky stars for having gone
to see Phelps at all. But Bancroft was an excep-
tion A much more common experience was that ot
Mansfield, the original Col. Stanley in “The Pirates
of Penzance.” Gilbert enforced a Grossmith
(senior) interpretation at rehearsal, and Mansfield
could see what a marionette Grossmith was in Gil-
bert’s hands. So, at the performance, Mansfield,
who was a genius, struck out an entirely different
line. The plan succeeded. But he could not get
any further employ where Gilbert had any influence.
A Sydney humorist accurately summed up the
physical qualities of the visiting opera singers. Said
he: “They’re all half over life-size.”
The Taits’ next theatre is to go up at Geelong,
where the picture craze has been the_ most violent
thing known since the Oldest Inhabitant cut his
teeth.
“The Witness for the Defence,” which was played
for five weeks in Melbourne, is booked for only three
in Sydney, thus leaving five for the bright and festive
double programme of “Lady Frederick” and ‘ Dolly s
Little Bills.” The politely farcical after-piece, by
the way, is H. A. Jones’s condensation of his own
comedyj “Dolly Reforming Herself,” with the original
moral omitted. It was fixed-up in a hurry for a
sketch performance at the London Hippodrome, and
was then called “Dolly Cutting Herself” a mislead-
ing title, seeing that the tempestuous little spend-
thrift neither cuts nor reforms herself. The amended
title is better.
The Melbourne Cyclorama didn’t find a buyer at
auction. The bidders ran dry at £9750, and the
owners reckon upon getting five figures privately.
Brisbane is to have still another open-air theatre
—at Dutton Park. It will accommodate about 1400
people.
“Vulcan”: Brennan’s Amphitheatres, Limited, be-
comes a solid competitor in the Australian vaude-
ville business these days. Already the circuit com-
prises 11 houses, including those in Sydney, Mel-
bourne, Brisbane and Hobart, while arrangements
have been made for appearances at the Victoria
(Newcastle), King’s (Adelaide), and on the Fuller
circuit(M.L.). An opening will also shortly be made in
several Westralian centres. With this ramification,
the co. will be able to spread itself considerably in
the matter of engagements, and the management ex-
pects to be soon able to offer every artist worth
while at least a year’s work. The co. has broken
out into a new rule that it will give no engagement
to any artist who has already appeared in any other
vaudeville house in Australasia; and that, following
on the Williamson-Clarke-Meynell amalgamation,
doesn’t make things look less blue for the mummer
and mummeress.
Reginald Wykeham and Co., who carried on Hugh
J. Ward’s clever comedy team when that actor-
manager joined J.C.W., are in Sydney re-organising
for a tour of the Commonwealth, with Maoriland to
follow.
.W. S. Percy, who is not going to London until an-
other contract with the Firm expires, has blossomed
as a constructor of cinematograph plays. He won
the prize recently offered by Wests’ Ltd., for a
canned drama, and the invention is now being re-
hearsed in Sydney and on its harbor.
Bilanderson has been' taking off his hat to the
stage-manager of his Sydney dramatic co., for heaving
scenery into position with unusual velocity. Tran-
shipment at Albury delayed the arrival of the dress-
ing for the drama at the Sydney end. The first
section arrived on Friday at 1.30 p.m., and the
second section, which contained the bigger settings,
was not delivered at the Criterion until 3 p.m. on
Saturday. But there was nothing out of place when
the curtain rose at 8 p.m., no hitch during the per-
formance, and the show was out at 10.45. Furious,
scene-banging has also been going on at the Palace'
since the Anderson Co. moved down there. On
Wednesday and Saturday afternoons “The Old
Woman Who Lived in a Shoe” has the stage for her
panto. Her last curtain is at 5 p.m., and between
that and 8 p.m. the panto, settings have to be fired
out and “The Christian” sets shot in. And, despite
the lack of working room, the big job is done with
unfailing regularity.
Bananaland’s Police Offences Bill gives a policeman
above the rank of sergeant power to drop in and tor-
bid “any cinematograph pictures of an immoral,
grading, or pernicious nature.” Commissioner Cahill,
called in to give light on the subject in the House
of Fossils, opined “that pictures representing busn-
ranging escapades should be considered improper.
In the same spirit a horse-breeder would probably
regard the picture of a motor-car factory as “im-
proper.” The House of Fat thought that the at-
tempt to put down Ikey Noses was pernicious.
There is therefore no telling what a constable above
the rank of sergeant may do if his whims are allowed
to run loose. The time may come when some Repub-
lican copper may, with a good show of reason, forbid
pictures that reproduce some of the Court functions
which encourage people to be worms instead of
men.
Edmund Gwenn, so pleasantly remembered in Aus-
tralia, seems all over the place on the London stage.
In a single number of the Referee, he gets good
notices as a Pett Ridge cockney at a hospital
matinee, as Uncle Peter in a new success, The Girl
Who Couldn’t Lie,” and as clown in Punch and
Judy at the great annual theatrical garden-party.
Oscar Asche has struck it so rich with “Kismet”
in London, that he will not want to change the bill
before he closes the season and leaves for Australia.
Though there is a tremendous outcry about
the “prohibitive” cost of labor, especially in
the manufacturing industries, some figures
just issued by the N.S.W. Government Stat-
ist show that, in proportion to the total
value of the output, salaries and wages have
actually decreased in the past 10 years. For
every £lO,OOO worth of goods produced m
1901, £1928 was paid in wages and salaries,
whereas in 1910 only £1750 was paid. But
if the man on wages or salary got less in
proportion to the output, the man who
supplied the material and the fuel took care
that he didn’t. Per £lO,OOO of output,
materials cost £5903 in 1901, and £63-5 in
1910; while the fuel bill sprinted along from
£194 to £238.
Maoriland’s Commandant recently re-
ceived a challenge from one Robert Hogg,
who has been pouring out a cataract of
words on behalf of the “anti-militarists.’
Hogg wants to fight the General, but only
with his mouth. He defies him to debate,
among other things, “whether any scheme of
compulsory service possible of adoption by
the M.L. Government can be considered ade-
quate compared with the forces that might
be arrayed against it.” If Hogg wants to
be Whole-Hogg he ought to barrack for the
abolition of doctors and medicine, too. Every
doctor and every medicine is beaten at some
time or other by hostile forces; nevertheless,
in the struggle to hold off the victory of
Death as long as possible, even Hogg pro-
bably takes a pill now and then.
The Melbourne policeman is searching,
practically as one man, for the corpse of a
young woman who went to a private hospital
recently, was operated on, and thereafter
vanished off the face of the earth. Dr. Pea-
cock, who has been arrested in connection
with the business, is a well-known citizen,
a man of substance, and a septuagenarian,
the theory of the police is that accused (who is
alleged to have given all his servants a holi-
day about the time the girl is suspected of
having passed out in his hospital) has
macerated, or cut up, or burned, or other-
wise effectively disposed of the remains.
They were strengthened in this view on Sat-
urday by unearthing beneath the dead em-
bers of a fire, on one of the doctor’s farms,
four false teeth and some trinkets, alleged
to correspond with those of the departed.
The thing that baffles the average man of the
world is why any prominent medico, who had
had the misfortune to have the victim of an
illegal operation die' on his hands, should
need to run the fearful risk of secretly
burying her. It is notorious that scores of
“high-class” doctors perform curettes with
the same object as their less-reputable con-
freres perform “illegal operations”—one dis-
tinction between the two operations being
that the latter is a criminal offence, while
the former is perfectly legal. Why should a
well-to-do practitioner bury a patient illeg-
ally. and run the risk of the gravest charge
in the calendar, when he might abandon all
perilous mystery and take his stand on the,
fact that he performed a necessary curette?'
But that is only one of half a dozen myster-
ies that have landed the police in a maze,,
without a lantern to show the way out.
EXTRA DRY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
MELBA GRAND OPERA SEASON.
IMPORTANT NOTICE.
PATRONS are particularly requested to be in their
seats to-morrow evening before the curtain rises at
8 o’clock, as after that time the doors will be closed
until the termination of the last act. — Terrifying,
but incorrect announcement in daily paper.
The red-faced man breathed through his nose
And slammed the paper down;
Then like a biplane up he rose
And spluttered into town ;
He sought the plan and tickets two
Cast down upon the board.
“Here! Gimme something else! Do you
Imagine I will see it through
Without a drink? GOOD LORD!!”
The clerk with promptitude remarked,
‘Last’ should, of course, be ‘first.’ ”
“All right!” the heated stallite barked,
And rushed to quench his thirst.
Some people sit ahd drink the score ;
But many blithesome pals
Just tolerate the Crash and roar,
And haunt the costly opera for
The splendid intervals.
N.S.W.

SOCIETY
Aii Adelaide court lias pushed the defini-
tion of an “accident” pretty far. Deceased
was an engine-driver, who had to go down
into a pit for cleaning purposes, and work
in a stooping position, with much heat on
the back of his neck. He staggered out, and
died a few yards away. The Government,
his employer, relied on the admitted and ob-
vious fact that he had a weak heart; hut the
Court held that it was “accidental that the
adverse conditions of the man and the ad-
verse conditions of the work coincided, so
that death resulted.” Verdict for £3OO com-
pensation accordingly, to be divided among
widow and children. The decision is satis-
factory, because, if the job kills the worker,
the employer who put him on to it may
fairly be asked to shoulder the responsi-
bility, even though a stronger man might
have taken no harm. But “stay of proceed-
ings” was granted, and various lawyers are
likely to earn fat fees before the point is
finally settled.
“GRACE BEFORE MEAT”
S. Aus. has been getting thoughtful. Dur-
ing the first eight months of this vear, Ade-
laide had only 9.80 in. of rain, which is over
sin. below its very moderate average. The
pastoral districts expect less, and got it.
Oodnadatta has had but lfin. in 11 months !
The showers in the Far North have, how-
ever, fallen at the right time, so the position
isn’t as bad as the figures ; nevertheless, the
State is beginning to realise that its boom
may not last for ever.
A Ballarat (Vic.) man lias just been fined
10s. because his wife suffers from an unduly
tender heart. The lady has a penchant for
decayed and invalid dogs; she keeps open
house, and they wander in when nothing else
is doing. Then she' nourishes them for a
month or two, and, when they are sleek and
plump, she discharges them into a cold
world. Some, however, decline to be dis-
charged ; perceiving they have a good thing
on, they return persistently after being
heaved out. Lately the dog inspector hap-
pened on this Lost Logs’ld ome, and callously
summoned the lady’s husband, as occupier,
for keeping unregistered dogs. The poor
man explained the circumstances, but got no
sympathy, and it is difficult to see what he
can do without reaping trouble. He might
close his door, but: his wife would probably
re-open it; he might spread abroad some
enticing grilled steak flavored with
strychnine, but the children or even his wife
might consume it. Another plan would be
to speak firmly to the lady, but she might
not be the sort of lady one would naturally
speak firmly to. Looking at the case fore
and aft, The Bulletin doesn’t know what
to recommend, unless the man can come to
an honorable understanding with a sausage
factory.
“Berkeley”: As J. F. Hogan says so (B.
24/8/T1), Cook may have christened the
inlet south of Sydnev “Botanist Bay” ; but
according to the journal in which Sir Joseph
Banks made his notes on the voyage of the
Endeavor —the volume is now in the posses-
sion of the Sydney National Library—the
place was first known as “Stingray Bay.”
In several places Banks has written the
word “Stingray,” and has afterwards crossed
it out and substituted “Botany.” Neither
name is strikingly appropriate, yet both are
an improvement on most of the names given
by Cook to his discoveries. I believe that
Sydney enthusiasts still bear a grudge
against him for his clumsy “Fort Jackson,”
though they can overlook the stupid “New
South Wales.” And what could be more
ridiculous than the name “New Hebrides” be-
stowed on a group of the most gorgeous
islands in the Pacific? Evidently Cook was
so busy pursuing his particular hobby that
he allowed his imaginative faculty to die of
inattention.

From “Palette, Senior” :
The Exhibition of Students’ Work at the Sydney
Art School is a fine tribute to the teaching ability
of Julian Ashton and Mildred Lovett. Here we see
work of the artists who are to fill the centre of
the wall in the shows of 1920-40. The Longs, the
Lamberts, the John Longstaffs, the Streetons and the
Bertram Mackennals. Tom t erry has a back view
of a clerk poring over a ledger—a fine bit of pen
and ink work ; and his charcoal drawing of a girl’s
head is full of, definite and vital technique. Miss
Lovett’s short-skirted kid nursing her elegant ankle
is a better black-and-white than any ever bought by
the N.S.W. National Gallery. Two vases of hers, if
produced in any country but her own, would condemn
her to china-painting for life. Broderick is a name
on a lot of good drawings, and if the students who
tried to visualise Dickens were content to interpret
the history and fiction of Australia, the whole show
would advance a rung or two on the long ladder that
leads to perfection.
In Parramatta, where
asylums, hospitals, gaols,
“h om e s,” etc., are as
thick as wattle-trees,
they must have a very
casual way of dealing
with the superfluous
gentleman whom “no-
body owns,” judging by
the evidence given at in-
quests and inquiries, an-
cient and modern. A
coronial inquiry was held
recently touching the
death at Rookwood of
an inmate who had died,
according to the medical
testimony, while having
a wound in his neck
stitched up. The wound
“had severed the wind-
pipe, but was not seri-
ous.” The coroner, may-
be in some stiff-necked
humor, nevertheless en-
tered a finding to the
effect that death had re-
sulted from those injur-
ies to the neck, although
the authorities of one or
two district asylums
would possibly have been
better pleased if his
Worship had not been
so luridly independent,
and had certified to
“heart-failure” —t hat
blessed term so handy to the profession
when it doesn’t want to be bothered.
The facts appear to be that the man broke
away from Rookwood, and turned up at one
of the Parramatta asylums, where he cut his
throat; but gentlemen have no right to cut
their throats away from their own asylums,
so. after some mysterious delay, he was put
in an ambulance and taken back to his own
retreat, and there he promptly died —not of
“Peart failure,” as he should have done, but
(according to the coroner) from the cut
throat which was so unimportant that it
really shouldn’t have mattered to anybody.

Apparently arriving at the decision that
the teachings of Christianity are of no use,
the Wellington (M.L.) grand jury handed
up the dogger’s lash to Justice Chapman
last week. Thus :
That the grand jury views with alarm the large
number of sexual offences continually : coming before
the Courts, especially against young children, and is
of opinion that these offences-should be treated with
more severity, and by the administration of corporal
punishment where necessary.
Old prophet Job evidently saw that jury in
the far distance when he entered on his tab-
lets: “Ask now the beasts, and they shall
teach thee.”
* * # *
Judging from many recent cases in Aus-
tralia, and especially in Melbourne, there is
urgent need for a Federal law against mis-
cegenation, as in some of the American
States. Not only is the notoriously lustful
Cliow always on the lookout to entice young
white girls, who themselves corrupt others,
but it is becoming a common practice for
women threatened with a charge of vagrancy
to “keep house” for a few days with the dis-
colored filthmonger, who then secures the
woman’s liberty by giving evidence on her
behalf. It would not matter so much if each
degenerate remained with her Ah Muck, but
she doesn’t. She strays and perhaps con-
taminates a dozen or more whites, physically
and morally, before she finally returns to the
dens of Lilly Buck-stleet.
The fight between the doctors and the
friendly societies has reached Adelaide, and
about 80 of the best-known medical men
have advertised that they won’t be on speak-
ing terms with a new “dispensary.” As
usual in these pull-devil pull-baker affairs,
each side swears that it is the Baker. The
doctors say that they are being sweated ; the
societies say that the doctors are a grasping
Trust. The societies say they can get
plenty of doctors to do the work at their
prices and on their terms; the medical
strikers say—“ Very well, get them, and see
how we’ll deal with them!” And, no doubt,
while they are “dealing with” the alleged
blacklegs of their profession, the top-notch
M.D.’s will express their horror at the prob-
ably cruder but far less malignant methods
of the Hebrew Hod-carriers’ Union.
* * * *
Broken Hill has broken out into a union
or an honorable understanding or something
of the kind in the marrying industry. It be-
gan in the old way ; one parson was getting
such a rush of trade that his fellow saints
suspected that his fees were below cost price,
so they formed a union, and drew up a log.
No doubt there will be strikes, and blackleg
ministers, and “scab” brides and bride-
grooms, and secret discounts, and all that;
and perhaps the unionist butcher will refuse
to bring the breakfast steak to the blackleg
couple. But, anyway, the rent-collector will
call whether they are unionist or not. You
can, thank Heaven, always rely upon the
thorough impartiality of the rent-collector!
* * * *
Heath hides in most places, but it is un-
usual to find the grisly old familiar in a
mouth-organ. Yet that was where he waited
for Kenneth Stewart, aged 18, a dentist’s
assistant in South Melbourne. Sucking at a
mouth-organ, Stewart pulled a false tooth
back into his throat. It stuck and tore his
throat; septic poisoning set in, and Stewart
died in hospital. One does not know whether
the moral is against the mouth-organ or the
false tooth.
SPRAINS AND BRUISES.
The right time to treat a sprain or bruise is the
moment you get it, and the right remedy to use is
Chamberlain’s Pain Balm. The quicker you rub it
on, and the more you use it, the quicker your sprain
will be cured.*
Sands’ N.S.W. Directory will show-its fat
red cheeks to the world as usual early in
1912. and the publishers (John Sands, Ltd.)
will be glad to have word of any changes of
address up to the first week in October.
A French journalist of first rank, who
visited Australia recently, is reported to
have said :
You Australians are the strongest national contra-
diction I have yet met. In political progress you
are 50 years ahead of Europe; in religious progress
you are 100 years behind it.
The comment is much more discerning than
usual. The vast majority of Australian
men either do not trouble about religion at
all, or have very broad and vague notions
on the subject. Probably not 5 per cent, of
them subscribe to any definite set of doc-
trines. Yet the clergy are able to bluff the
whole population into acting, in many ways,
as if it were thoroughly religious. It is prob-
able that four-fifths of the male voters favor
a _ sensible 0. D. Act; but the “religious”
minority stands in the way of progress and
cleanliness, and, in the name of snuffle and
cant, manages to prevent a deadly disease
being properly treated. A great majority
of thinking men favor the totalisa-
tor; but, in the two largest States of the
Commonwealth, the book-thumper has joined
with the book-maker to block it. The ma-
jority in favor of a rational Sunday is over-
whelming ; but, though the churches continue
to be. empty, the preacher manages to stop
his rivals from doing any good with their
shows. So it is all along the line of social
reform. The parson apparently feels that
he must do something to hold his job and
justify his existence; so he is always on
the lookout for anything which can be
twisted into being anti-religious. That
much is understandable; why the people put
up with it is the mystery.

1 here is quite a boom in lysol-poisoning;
but those who have a mind to try it as a
means to suicide are hereby warned to think
up some other method. Lysol, taken inter-
nally, is a most painful expedient, the doc-
tors say.
“Chloe,” on the neglect of Australian
writings in Australian schools :
I agree entirely with “Cadonia” (B. 27/7/’ll),
that Australian Universities and Education Depart-
ments have hitherto done hardly anything to further
a knowledge of or foster a regard for Australian
writings, either prose or verse. Having coached
pupils for matriculation and teachers’ examinations
Mature for the past seven years, I can assert
that during’ this period not a single mention has
been made of an Australian writer, nor has anv
specimen of Australian work been dealt with. The
anthology mentioned by “Cadonia” as having been
discussed by the lecturer with a grimace, is a volume
well worthy the attention of the Australian educa-
tionist. Not only has the selection. been well and
carefully made, but the introductory sketch —a brief
history of the birth and progress of Australia’s litera-
ture—is a model of style, and is full of interest to
the student of- Australia’s history. If the technique
of the verse is not uniformly high, the work, at
least, reflects the life and color of Australia and
Maoriland, and in many instances a high literary
standard has been reached —in the ballads particu-
larly. The trouble in most cases is that the subject
is handled by the literary prig who only finds it safe
to tackle volumes through which his way has been
clearly marked by the annotator. The English
classics are served out to the students with so many
carefully-regulated opinions that, to be Successful,
one needs neither taste nor intelligence nor critical
faculty. Only memory is called upon. This sort of
person’s alleged contempt for Australian literature
isn’t, therefore, any reflection on the literature. It
means merely that he is a poof loafer, who must
lean up against somebody else.
TO LAGGARD SPRING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You need to come and gladden us
With sunshine-flushed Septembers,
That brought the buds to blossomhood,
And set the land aglow,
While ranges rang with nesting songs,
Ah, well one heart remembers
Your welcome in the songbirds’ haunts,
Some twenty years ago !
Has cruel Winter ’prisoned you,
And robbed you of your powers?
Or have you to the tempests’ king,
Your pleasant sway resigned?
No birds’ love-song is joyous now,
And tender, new-born flowers
Are drooping on the sodden hills,
Torn with the hail- and wind.
We dread the tyrant Winter’s rule,
It baulks us of our pleasure;
Dead blooms sad Autumn brings to us,
And Summer’s reign we fear:
Again in normal glory, Come!
With wealth of floral treasure,
And put stem Winter’s clouds to flight
Queen season of the year !
VICTORIA.
FRANK BELLMAN.

Six times a week the Sydney D.T. re-
minds us of Hosea Bigelow’s “pious editor,”
who was an advocate for Fat at home, but a
fiery democrat in matters “as fur away as
Paris is.” The latest instance is over recent
Labor troubles on two sides of the world. In
connection with the row at Lithgow, which
is by the Telegraph’s own back door, the
“pious editor” sees the position clearly:—
The idea that during a strike people may with im-
punity go to excesses which would involve them in
serious consequences at any other time, is one of the
most pernicious that could get currency, and should
be confuted in the only effective way, by a practical
example of what it means to those who act upon it
in a country of ordered government.
But “as fur away” as London is, he sees
things in quite a different light
They [the employers] may be in a position to ease
the working conditions and make them more re-
munerative, but only under compulsion will they do
so. The effect of that attitude is to antagonise the
workers and to prompt them to excesses quite un-
justifiable in themselves, but explainable through the
animosity that has been aroused in them.
And thus the “pious editor” makes the best
of both worlds —the world of the workers’
pennies and the world of the Fat adver-
tisers’ pounds.

AN INTENSE MOMENT.
Baby Willie licks his knife whilst having tea at grandma's.
Sydney Telegraph recently belabored “a
certain section'’ of Australians because they
doubted the motives of a Japanese Polar ex-
pedition, which mistook Sydney for the Pole,
and accordingly camped there, under the
forts, and turned a flock of cameras loose on
the surrounding landscape. Miss Rose Scott,
Bruce Smith and other peaceable individuals
were horror-stricken at the “discourtesy”
which the suspicious Australians had heaped
upon their “luckless” visitors. A few days
ago (17/8/T1) the same Telegraph inter-
viewed a Mr. Darcy, a “commercial” just
bacx from Japan and vicinity, who men-
tioned, among other things :
I have never been in a country before where I have
met with such rudeness. They will not allow you to
take any photos, at all. In fact, a friend of mine
was taking photos, when we were going through the
Inland Sea, and a spy on board informed the authori-
ties, and at the first stopping place my friend was
arrested and taken away. The captain of the boat,
however, said he would not leave without him, but
the Japanese refused to give him up until the cap-
tain threatened to make representations to the
British Ambassador. They gave him up then, but
kept the camera, which was a most expensive one.
When boorish Australians commence to
assault and rob everv Japanese who uses a
camera, the commotion raised by I). Tele-
graph, B. Smith and R. Scott will be so
great that every “wireless” apparatus in the
Southern Hemisphere will be put out of
action.
* * * *
“Small Beer”: An ancient Sydney brew-
ery that has seen some changes is to see
some more. Ruilt at Leichhardt, it was
originally King’s, and, later, the Sydney Co-
operative. Then for years it
was deserted: but now it has
been secured by the Marshall
people, whose old brewery at
Paddington was recently trans-
formed into a picture hall.
The brewer who has been put
in charge is one Truntsclinigg,
an Austrian, who has seen
something of the world. For
five years he was brewing at
the Brasserie des Pyramides,
near Cairo, Egypt; it appears
that the Arabs have acquired a
taste for the. liquor that keeps
Canon Boyce, Worrall and co.
so busy.
* * * *
Judge Rogers, presiding at
the current Quarter Sessions in
Sydney, had the hardihood to
create a precedent. A jury had
been locked up all Thursday
night, and on Friday evening
the case was still unfinished.
Now, as the law is understood
in N. S. Wales, juries in cases
of felony may not separate un-
til a verdict is given. Another
rule is that the law in N.S.W.
must not work on Saturdays if
it can possibly help it. Judge
Rogers was, therefore, faced
with the problem of calling the
court together on Saturday
morning or ordering the jury to
be locked up from Friday evening until Mon-
day morning. The latter alternative seemed
over the odds; the former was, of course, un-
thinkable. So Rogers, J., solved the problem
by allowing the jury to go home to its wife
and beer, and dismissing the Law to its
usual Saturday golf. The prisoner wasn’t
asked what he felt like doing.
The Maoriland Old Age Pensions Depart-
ment, which has just issued its annual re-
port, has dug out an interesting illustration
of the way Uncle Sam has to try to wriggle
out of his Constitutional strait-jacket—the
same strangling garment that has caused so
much trouble to Australia, which took it for
a pattern, to a great extent. Sam’s Con-
stitution confers no power on the Federal
Government to grant old age pensions—-
which oversight, by the way, only narrowly
escaped being repeated in the Commonwealth
Constitution. But it does give power, of
course, to “raise and support arms’’; and,
therefore, Congressman Wilson, of Pennsyl-
vania, framed a measure to organise in the
Department of War, under direction of the
President, an army corps, to be known as
the “Old Age Home Guard of the United
States Army.” Amongst the Bill's provi-
sions -were:—-
(1) That the Guard should be composed of per-
sons (men and women), not less than 65 years of
age, who had been citizens of the U.S. for 15 years.
(2) Provided such persons were qualified under
the property or income limitations, they should be
enlisted for the term of their natural life without
regard to their physical condition.
(3) The pay of a private in the Guard should be
£24 annually, paid in quarterly instalments.
(4) Arms and ammunition to be furnished at the
discretion of the Secretary of War, but no susten-
ance, except the pay, provided.
(5) No private should drill or see service with
the U.S. Army.
The Bill did not pass—except as an amaz-
ingly “slick” idea.
From the Solomon Islands, concerning the
latest murder: —
So poor Daniels has gone. He was preaching to
his “boys” at night, standing near the fire to get
light to" read his Bible, when Bang! a rustle in the
bush close handy, and he dropped, dying a few
minutes later, jimmy Malaita, whom I know very
well, says Daniels married a native couple from an-
other village who had run away. The girl had been
sold to another old buck, but as she had broken the
moral code, and was certain to be found out, she
cleared with her lover. The old buck who paid his
good red money (native shell, in lengths) has no
gun, but a repatriated kanaka, who had been to
Queensland and imbibed some of the blessings of
civilisation, is blamed for the actual shooting. Of
course nobody saw the marksman, and equally, of
course, the missionary boys know nothing about it.
When Queensland was trying to keep its cheap nigger
there used to be a touching yarn that the repatriated
Tommy, having been softened by the refining in-
fluencfes of a white man’s land, would be set upon by
the home-staying savage and put to death. Nothing
of that sort happened; on the contrary, this is
merely another instance of how the savage who
stayed at home is always suspected of turning to his
“refined” and “softened” brother when he wants a
particularly bad crime committed.

Though the census was taken over five
months ago the returns are not all in yet.
Statistician Ivnibbs still awaits two —not
from remote corners of Westralia or the Nor-
thern Territory, but from the Ma State —-one
from far up on the Darling, which is perhaps
excusable; and the other from near Yass-
berra, where tbe garden city of Australia is
to be. Apparently the enumerators have lost
themselves in those parched, drouthy districts,
or the inhabitants aren’t sure they are alive,
and didn’t like to fill in cards, or else they are
so scarce that there will never be any return.
Dattilo Rubbo, the Sydney artist, re-
hearses his woes :
“Do not speaka to me ! What tha artist suffra from
in Australia is tha not hava tha model. You will
not believe, so I tella to vou.
“Look at tha chap who lighta tha pipe with a
clean shave in ma peekature. You seea the beautiful
beard ho not got. Why? I scrape it off. That is
why.
“Listen to me. At first he had tha fine redda
whisk’, so beeg, like tha mat at tha door an’ tha re-
flexsion from tha burna match all over it. Beauti-
ful ! But Cristo! the terubble it was. Tha first I
setta heem down, filla tha pipe, lighta tha match,
but- —pouf ! —it is out before I hava tha time to see
tha effect. Then I geeve heem tha box, but it is
strika tha match, strika tha match all tha time. I
theenlc I not mucha tha snapaschott camera nor tha
lightening sketcher notta tha leetle hit ; so 1 getta
tha long taper that bum tha thirty meenut; but tha
pos-eeshi-on is all anyhow an’ I tcarra ma hair, an’
tha model he smoka tha pipe an’ laugh. Then I
getta idea —tha candella end —short, like so—in tha
hand, alight. Splendeed 1 an’ tha ref-lexsion on tha
whisk —bosker! I paint, an’ I paint, an’ I paint,
an’ tha model he nod, when —queek!—tha beeg
smoke, tha burna hair —Sancta Maria ! I joomp ail’
grab at tha flamin’ whisk’, an’ tha model he wake
up, passa me one in tha sto-mac, an’ knocka me
endovatip in tha corna. He tella me tha ‘Dam-
dago,’ an’ ‘what tha helova game you call this —
burna tha beeg hole in ma beautiful whisk, an’
stuffa it down ma neck!’ I say ‘Hold on, ma fine
fellow, you fall of a sleep youself, an’ burna you
whisk’ all to blazes. Whaffor I burn it? I want it,
loaf it, paint it —you no gooda to me witha hole in
you whisk’.
“In tha leetle while we shaka tha hand, an’ I
getta tha see-sor, an’ I say, ‘Ma good fellow, I
maka you looka tha one. I eutta you whisk*. An
he say ‘Lettago!’ But when he look in tha glass, he
send me tha summon for two squid ! •
“But I have tha revenge. I paint heem as 3mu
see, with a clean shave; an’ only it make tha peeka-
ture look lika damfool, I paint heem tha hole in tha
whisk’ —straight!”
Sydney Harbor Trust, pushed for a place
to berth the incoming mail .steamer Ophir
last week, was forced to send her to Pyr-
mont. She had on board 400 passengers for
Sydney and 100 for Brisbane, and they were
landed on the wrong side of the city, with-
out any means of reaching it except by
securing one of the inadequate supply of
taxi and horse cabs offering. To get to the
cabs the passengers had to carry their lug-
gage over railway lines, past snorting loco-
motives, amongst drags, lorries and wharf-
lumpers engaged in loading and discharging
shipping. As the Ophir steamed up to this
back door, she passed a dado of empty
wharves; but they are all leased to rival
companies, and were “not available.” The
whole thing was a glorious advt. for Syd-
ney’s u.nbeautiful administration of its
beautiful harbor.
Writing to a friend in Melbourne, a Cor-
onating pressman tells a passable yarn about
the big press dinner in Loudon. At many of
the Coronation functions, suffragettes—male
and female —attended and interrupted the
speakers, in order to call public attention to
The Cause. Therefore, chuckers-out were
regularly engaged to deal with the dis-
turbers. At the press dinner Louis Botha
was a guest. He started to reply (in
Dutch) to a toast, and his interpreter started
to interpret. Two of the cliucking-out bri-
gade instantly seized the interpreter, and,
before anybody could stop them, they had
bundled him as far as the nearest door,
where a constable was about to hurl him
into outer darkness, when several of the
guests interfered and explained matters.
From the Auckland (M.L.) Herald
(19/S/T1) :
COUNTRY HOME.—£2O a year is offered to
Young Lady who could teach three boys, eldest in
Fifth Standard ; one who is musical and can
milk preferred; urgent. Apply ', Post Office,
Onehunga.
This seems to suggest a new opening in the
cow-slavery line. But it isn’t as definite as
it might be ; ana we are left in doubt as to
whether the music and the milking are to
be kept separate, or whether the piano is to
be nailed up alongside the bail for the
soothing of Strawberry before the pumping
begins.
“Adelaide”: The Sydney “Spitting bri-
gade” (B. 24/S/T1). “Fine them a fiver a
time!” —in italics and with a note of exclam-
ation ! Why italicise? Why exclaim? Just
do it. We do, in Adelaide ; at least, we did.
Nobody spits now —on a pavement, at any
rate. Never was a seemingly-hopeless
habit so simply slain.

The councillors of a northern Victorian
township should consider themselves fortu-
nate in having a president who values his
privileges so highly. One privilege, according
to the explanation he offered, when charged,
the other day, with sly-grog selling, is that of
dispensing hospitality to fellow-councillors;
and that was why he happened to have in
his possession 10 quart and 3 pint bottles
of whisky, 3 of schnapps, 0 of brandy, 5 of
lager beer, 15 of ale, and a demijohn of
whisky. A Bench, which probably knew the
situation of that mayor’s cupboard quite
well, accepted the explanation with much
sympathy.

“Australian”: I don't know how it may
he here and there in the Ma State, but in S.
Aus., lest there should be any mistake about
it, the following order has just been issued
in the Education Gazette: “The Union
Jack must be used on every occasion.” That’s
in connection with the dreary daily process
of “saluting the flag.” A deplorable part of
the business is that the order is issued during
the reign of a Labor Government —and a
Labor Government, too, which looked as
though it were good Australian during the
recent Referenda campaign. I expect to
next read in the Education Gazette that
it is a child’s duty to revere his grand-dad,
but to utterly disregard the father who sup-
plies him with his bread-and-dripping.
A WATTLE DAY SONG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
the skies are blue, the sun smiles down, and I am
feeling gay,
hor Winter's gone and Spring is here and it is
Wattle Day ;
The motor whizzes down the road through groves of
golden trees ;
The posts and rails go waltzing past; I watch them
at my ease ;
Beyond the green-girt, blossomed hills, within the
city’s murk,
Deserted for this afternoon, lies unattractive Work;
A stray regret at intervals flicks past, but —quite
resigned—
I watch the fairy pawnshop-trees all swaying in the
wind.
I\ith eves half-closed, I see the elves —the hook-
nosed elves of Spring—
Small Cohens, drest in gold and green, who on the
branches swing ;
Each bright-eyed gnome beneath a bunch of sign-
board blossom lurks.
And watches o’er our “permanent and reproductive
works” ;
By road and bridge and railway line they do their
int’rest sums,
And chuckle through the golden days when sweet
September comes ;
Each 18-carat tree, I think, is Fairy Cohen’s tent
And only needs to be complete the two words
“Money Lent.”

ALITIES
“Southern Cross” : Re “Jeroi's” dodge of
tar-soaking maize to stop the kangaroo-rats rooting
it up (B. 27/7/’ll), and “Leo Lear’s” device of
poisoned furrows (B. 17/8/’ll), let me hasten to
•shout that nothing will stop ’roo rats from rooting
up things that are eatable. Once, on a moonlight
flight, I sneaked on to a kangaroo-rat that was root-
ing up a stalk of potatoes. Had an old Enfield rifle
loaded with a bullet 2in. long by Lin. diameter,
r ired, and saw the rat fall; but immediately it
arose and did a death-or-glory sprint down the fur-
row, with me and the sheep-dog in pursuit
We caught it before it had finished its course;
hut I was a bit surprised to find that the bullet
Tiad cut the beggar in halves, and we had run down
•only the hind-quarters. Returning, I was still more
astonished to find that the front-quarters had finished
the job of rooting up the stalk of potatoes. Tar'
Poison! Rats!

“Montalex”: I thank “L. 8.” (B. 3/8/’ll).
Like most men who care more for knowledge than
•for the reputation of possessing it, ■ I’m always glad
do be corrected. But some little explanation appears
L.B. says, off the west coast of Ireland, is known
to the Donegal Bay fishermen as the bone-shark;
benee my mistake. Of the other bone-shark men-
tioned by “L. 8.” I know nothing. I will not, how-
ever, have the presumption to say that the creature
Las escaped classification, because it is possible that
the classification has escaped me.
“Mater”: After a gorge of meat, the sta-
tion blacks were suffering from internal cramps. I
cured them all except one with painkiller. He was
very bad, and when I went to see him I found him
being cured by his wife, who was seated by a small
hole, about 10ft. away from him, with a wooden
vessel, known as a boolie-man, beside her. Tommy
was lying under an opossum rug, his face wearing
an expression of perfect confidence. Around his body
was one end of a long string, and the other end was
in the hands of the old woman, and was being drawn
from side to side between her teeth. Her mouth was
filled with water, and she drew the string with such
force as to cause the blood to flow freely from her
gums. She then let the blood, mixed with water,
flow from her mouth into the hole at her side; re-
filled her mouth, pulled again at the string, and so
repeated the performance time after time until the
patient was declared well. I asked the gin the mean-
ing of it. “I’m pulling the debbil-debbil out of
Tommy’s bingy,” she replied.
* * * *
“Waterbag”: Have any of your Abo-rig-
gers ever noticed the attraction or repulsion—l do
not know which it is—that music has for those pets
of the bush, bull-dog ants? Had a visitor to our
camp the other evening, who was giving us a few
verses on a concertina. Suddenly he became aware
of the presence of a few bull-dog ants, and he
stopped in the middle of the tune (he has never
been known to do this before). By the aid of a
lucifer he was found to be surrounded with bullies,
while there were none near any of us. He said that the
only time he remembered being bitten by the ants
was when playing, or just after a play. Suppose both
the attraction and repulsion must depend on the
quality of the player. Some concertina players
would attract the business department of a Black-
hand Society.
* * * *
“Pagin’’: The Abo-gent who said death
adders are found only in rocky country must have
been “our London correspondent” ; and when he lobs
in frantic Australia, I’ll ask him to mount his skates
and glide with me up the Mclntyre, and over the ice-
bound stretches that sweep along the Queensland-
N.S.W: border line. On the run I’m on (Oakhurst,
some 20 miles south-east of Qoondiwindi) we killed
seven death-adders in one day last month, which
isn’t a bad record for mid-winter. And every week
we chop up a few feet of the spike-tailed plague.
These horrors vastly outnumber all the other varie-
ties of snake hereabouts; and the same is true over
a few hundred miles through Goondiwindi, Mun-
gindi, and out past St. George; And this is almost
totally black soil, flat country.
* * * *
“Condamine”: The most effective method
of poisoning foxes is to drag a piece of roasted flesh
—or, better still, a good section of bullock’s tripe—
along a comparatively clear piece of country. Mix
an equal quantity of baking-soda with the strychnine,
and put the dose inside the bait. Distribute the
bait at distances of about 100 yards apart. A fox
striking the trail will always follow it up. The
prepared strychnine acts quickly, and I have known
32 foxes to be got on a trail of one mile in this way.

“Nurse'’: A few Bulletins ago I noticed
that a correspondent wanted to start a Society for
Annihilating “That’s Right” Fiends. I wondered
what he was growling about, but now I know, and
want to join the society. I’ve got the record-breaker
within my clutches, and, though he is indisposed, he
hasn’t got as fatal a malady as he deserves. Bring
him in the paper, and you get “That’s right” for
thanks. “Not much news in it!” “That’s right”
again. Passed a remark on the weather. “That’s
right,” although it wasn’t—it was raining hard.
Told him to ring if he wanted anything, and still
got “That’s right.” The only time he could not fit
it in, in answer to a query, was when I asked him
how he slept. “Like a log,” he said. “That’s
right,” I said, and fled.

The possums introduced from Australia
to Otago (M.L.) a few years ago have increased so
rapidly, that in one locality in 10 weeks a trapper
is alleged to have made £lOO out of the little
chaps’ skins. As ’possum is protected, the trapper
has been invited by the police to take his oath that
there is no truth in the allegation.
“Austin Dodd” describes a great bird-
fight :
Abo-liars put up various Australian birds as claim-
ants for the champion feathered fighting belt. I’ll
back the little black-and-white bird which in
Queensland is called the pee-wee. Saw a lone pee-
wee put up the boss bird-scrap of the centuries.
He fluttered on to a verandah, and lodged on the
window-sill, and the pane faithfully reproduced an
image of himself. Apparently he was the boss pee-
wee of those parts, and owned the only pee-wee harem
thereabouts, and, when he saw another cock pee-wee
sitting preening himself inside the window, lie
naturally felt riled. He ruffled up his feathers,
cocked his head sideways, and, in pee-wee language,
said as plain as could be: “Who in the are
you, anyway?” The bloke inside did ditto repeato,
and in dumb-show returned the query. That riled
the bloke outside some more, and he at once threw
down the gage of battle; in fact, both of him did.
Then the two of him went up a couple of feet in the
air, and fluttered for an opening, after which both
of him again sat down on the sill. Then the two
of him puckered out his throat, and the real one
outside threw a lot of horrible remarks concerning the
ancestry of the image off his chest, and the image
of course could only repeat it in dumb show. The
throat pucker must be something deadly; something
like the word “Rome” in a Yellow Pup meeting, for
both of him went fair dilly. Simultaneously both
of him rose and charged the window-sill like two
cyclones. Clash! They met in the centre of the
pane, and, for a full half-minute wrestled up and
down the glass from top to bottom. Then both of
him sat down for wind, and the bloke outside went
just fair mad when he saw the image still there,
and every action being promptly and faithfully re-
peated made him madder. He was up at once, and
when both of him charged this time, it was a case
of “Let her go, Gallagher!” Rip! He struck a
projecting nail in the sash, and a hatful of feathers
went adrift, and blood trickled down his chest. He
sat down and screamed with ecstasy and rage. The
ecstasy because he saw the feathers flying, and the
blood trickling down the other bloke’s breast; the
rage, because the inside bird —instead of chucking
up the sponge and doing a get, as a knocked-out pee-
wee should —still sat there passing out insulting re-
marks in dumb show. This time the both of him
put it all on. Bang! He struck a projecting beam
and went to the boards on his back, two feet below
the window. But even as he was falling he chortled
with joy, for there was the other bloke going down
head-first inside. He lay on his back, gasping,
clawing himself all over to see if he was all there,
and then scrambled on to his feet. Joy! His op-
ponent had vanished, and he was cock of the walk
once more. Then he caught sight of me, and, as
plainly as a pee-wee could, he said: “Fancy that
bloke having the cheek to think he had a chance
with Me ; he hadn’t Buckley’s.” Giving a trium-
phant whoop he darted round the back to see if he
could catch him and give him a few more to go on
with. Half an hour later I saw him exhibiting his
wounds, and telling his harem of the fierce scrap
he had put up with a pee-wee poacher, and how he
knocked him out in three flies. They all believed
him except one old hen pee-wee, and he cuffed her on
the jaw, and forced her to take refuge in another
tree. 1 think she was his mother-in-law. That was
the best bird-scrap I ever saw, and I’ll back the
pee-wee, all styles, beaks, hits, claws, wings and
clinches allowed, against all comers.
“Condamine” : “Saladin” (B. 17/8/’ll)
recalls that historic personage, “Crooked Mick, from
the Speewah.” The old bush legend of Crooked Mick
was a fine effort of the imagination, and would be
worth publishing. It is long since I heard it, but
I still remember that Speewah Bluff was so tremen-
dous a shed that the boss of the board had to ride
up and down on a bicycle. Crooked Mick himself
used to shear his sheep as they came down a shoot;
his best work was to do two lambs with three blows
of the blades. If a man got the sack at one
end of the shed, he would go to the other end
and get put on; sometimes the next season would
be just starting when he got there. A shilling in at
the first pub after cutting-out netted the winner £ 75.
And, as a last sample of the fabulous person’s qual-
ity, he used to take a single-furrow plough in each
hand for a weight, and thus jump rivers where
bridges were scarce.
McPHERSIN AND McCARTHY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
McPherson was a canny Scot who owned a tidy
farm,
And viewed each small extravagance with very grave
alarm ;
His saving disposition all his neighbors’ wrath in-
curred.
’Twould only take a little loss,” these people all
averred,
“To disturb the mental balance of McPherson.”
He only knocked off ploughing when the day gave
place to night,
Then hurried off to bed at once to save expense in
light;
His kiddies did their “ekker” in the morning, sun
or rain ;
To see them burning candles went against their
father’s grain,
So intensely mean —or frugal—was McPherson.
A “public” there existed, called “The Dingo and the
Bat,'”
And sometimes Mac would wander in to liquefy a
sprat.
He never spent in thirsty haste. Oh, no ! he’d hang
about
In case a friendly feeling should impel a mate to
shout.
He was canny with his drinking, was McPherson.
One evening to the “Dingo,” outside which were
sev’ral drays,
McPherson paid a visit. ’Twas .his first for many
days.
He entered and discovered in the crowded bar a
man
Who kept on saying loudly, “Well, I bet a quid I
can !”
“What th’ de’il does he mean?” thought old
McPherson.
“I have a glass of liquor here, and mean to drink a
drop—
As much or little as I choose —but, when at last I
stop,
There’ll still remain within the glass enough to fill
two more !
A quid to say I do it!” All the others said “Good
lor ! ”
“Oh, he’s daft,” commented stolid old McPherson.
McPherson knew the gambler for a witless loon and
rash,
And reckoned it an easy chance to make a little
cash ;
’Twould be a safe investment, if McCarthy didn’t
bluff.
McPherson chewed it over. Was it reely safe
enough ?....
“Here! I’ll bet a poond ye can’t!” exclaimed
McPherson.
“Ver on! Put up yer money!” said McCarthy with
a grin ;
McPherson showed a sovereign, feeling sure that he
must win.
“Ye’ll drink a bit, but leave enough to fill anither
two ?”
“Quite right,” replied McCarthy, “that’s exactly
what I’ll do!”
“Verra week I’ll see ye dae it,” said McPherson.
McCarthy raised the liquor with a free and easy
pass.
The beer went down—a sixpence made a jingle in the
glass!
“A sixpence fills two glasses,” said McCarthy. “See
th’ sell?”
But Mac saw ruination, and ejaculated “H—11!”
He was very flabbergasted, was McPherson.
“He had th’ sixpence in his mouth,” explained old
Danny Maher,
“An’ sixpence fills two glasses to the brim at anv
bar! ”
At this McCarthy’s visage wore a look of sheer
delight,
And, picking up his winnings, he politely said “Good
night.”
“Aye, guid neit!” replied the furious McPherson.
And now, when it is moonlight (so the neighbors ill
avow),
They see McPherson going hell-for-leather with his
plough ;
He keeps his horses trotting all the weary wav
across,
And cheerful old McCarthy says he’s making up his
loss.
But more taciturn than ever is McPherson.

‘ Gympie Nettle”: Re “Mallarapoonie's”
query as to whether poisoned animals poison the
other animals that eat their flesh (B. 10/8/’ll).
All poisons have a special seat of action. Morphine
acts on the brain, digitalis on the heart, and so on.
When the animal dies the poison is found in a sort
of chemical combination with the part affected, none
remaining in the flesh (or muscles). As regards
strychnine, the seat of action lies in the spinal cord,
and in animals poisoned by strychnine the major
part of the drug is found combined with the cord,
some little being excreted unchanged in the urine.
None of the poison lodges in the muscle, the whole
of it being carried to the spine by the blood. A
harmful effect might be produced on “Mallarapoo-
nie’s”' niggers by eating the cord of their defunct
mongrels, but even then they would have to absorb
18 times as much of the drug as it ordinarily takes
to kill. Combination with the tissue in some Way
attenuates the drug, which fact is made use of by
scientists, e.g., in tetanus, where the bacillus is at-
tenuated by mixing with a monkey’s brain, and then
injected in gradually increasing doses into any other
animal to produce immunity.

“Billy Bluegum’s” last yarn:—
As a pastmaster in the art of snaring insects, the
spider isn’t a detail compared with the blue-tongued
lizard. The upper side of the tail and body is
covered with tiny pores, from which the reptile can,
at will, fire jets of a sticky fluid in any desired
direction, and at a surprisingly long range. Watched
one at work the other day, and from the number of
flies, ants, etc., he gathered in this way into his
cerulean gape, I concluded that he can hold his own,
on a tonnage basis, with a fully-equipped Dread-
nought. Offered him some pipe-juice on the end of
a straw by way of a digestive, at which he broad-
sided and fired bow and stern-chasers in a fashion
that decided me on using a longer straw in future.
Under the influence of the “weed” he soon developed
“tobacco heart” in an aggravated form, and lay ap-
parently dead, but after a few minutes recovered,
and declared his willingness to see it out if I would
shift to leeward. I wasn’t having any. By the
way, it is a popular fallacy that snakes and other
reptiles are easily poisoned with nicotine. My clay,
which is strong enough .to shift the sins from a house
and land agent’s conscience, has been tried on reptiles
of all varieties and dimensions from the gohanna
downwards, with the result that, though they go to
by-bye for a time, they invariably recover.

“Dick K.”: All previous liars have over-
looked the most truly friendly bush animal. It is a
hairy, wiry biped, of the mammalian order, wearing
a hump on its back and carrying a tin-can in its
forepaw. It generally wears drab monotones, and
has a thirst for beer and a craving for tobacco. It
is a nomadic animal, whose chief habitat is the
bush, though it is sometimes found in city parks.
During the wool season it gathers in large numbers
near shearing-sheds. Farmers detest the animal,
except during harvest-time, when it can sometimes
be induced to work, and its presence in large num-
bers has a tendency to lower wages. It is a pecu-
liarity of the species that the specimens caught are
almost always males, the female being but rarely
seen. I don’t know its name.
* * * *
“Only Me” : Hindruarsh Island (S.A.) now
knows more than it used to about pre-natal influence.
A common or back-yard cat had the misfortune to
have her tail in the way of a door that was closing
with violence, with the result that she lost it. In
the course of time an event happened, and the off-
spring were as tailless as mother. The demand for
tailless cats immediately became great, and exceeded
the supply. Orders were booked ahead, and the
neighbors waited patiently. But when the next
family arrived, tails were the fashion, though they
were nothing for a cat to boast about. The third
lot have just come to hand, again with no tails at
all; and a tote is now being run on future results.

“Can-Teen”: I want “Nitellub’s” (B.
27/7/Tl) blood. Having got in holts with a “san-
terpee,” I streaked for the spirits of salts bottle,
put my finger in, and applied the remedy. It cured
the bite all right; that is, it left an excavation where
the bite was. But “Nitellub” never said it should
be the other lunatic’s finger that you dipped in the
acid. The nail is now dropping off, and my tongue
has a small hole in it. The doctor said it served
me right for taking a fool’s advice.

“Geofred”: Concerning brilliant snakes.
A party of us, prospecting up at Farmers Creek, in
the Lithgow district (N.S.W.) some years ago, dis-
turbed a chap about sft. long, and colored a bril-
liant blood-red. As he knelt down to quench his
Wowseristic thirst, and the sun shone on his gaudy
back, we were almost convinced that he was a
glorious pink. Unlike the yellow reptiles seen by
“K.P.,” etc., this red snake was quite friendly, and
allowed us to break his back with a stick. He was
bottled and presented to a pub in Lithgow, and he’s
there yet for all I know.
* * * *
“Jeroi”: Which of the Australian bird 3
has the longest food list? Tallawahgs will eat flesh,
fruit, grain, insects and grubs. As to the animal
food, they make a meal of it, whether it has been
dead a day or a fortnight, though I think they
prefer it fresh. They will attack maize crops while
the grain is soft, but are hardly bad enough in this
respect to be called a pest. They also have a
greater number of calls —mimicry excepted—than
any other Australian bird that I know, except per-
haps the oofbird, though none of their notes are
sweetly musical.
* * * *
“Jack Lizard” : For the keenest sense of
smell, I’ll back the fox every time. When Reynard
wants a feed he doesn’t chase the rabbits about or
try to enter the abode by the front door. He selects
a one-room residence, sniffs along till the terminus
is reached, and then burrows down, dropping right
on to the young family, in some cases digging
two and three feet—kittens are never any deeper.
If any Aboliar can produce a sniffer to pull a scent
3ft. below the ground to the surface (must be a
live scent), then new-chum liars are no good on this
page.

“Metropolitan Cat Proprietor”: I don’t
see why this Abo. column should be used solely as
a Bush Liar’s Refuge. Here! lemme in and gimme
room to swing a pen! I want to know (1) what
will make a house cat attend to its job of catching
rats and mice, and (2) is a cat utterly degraded
and useless when it greedily devours the cold, stark
corpses of the vermin captured in the trap set by
its disgusted proprietor? Some months ago a friend
burdened my household with a cat which is a nuis-
ance to me in about 75 different ways. It strews
itself in my path, invites friends round to sing after
hours, secretes itself at locking-up time, and has to
be searched for and thrown out; kills birds; has
vulgar squabbles with the brute next door, and (last
straw!) yells at me in the most insulting way for
donations of milk and meat! I would gladly write
off its sins if it would only catch rats, but it won’t.
Is there any way of curing that animal of being use-
less, or do I just have to treat it with the old re-
liable mixture of brick, rope and river?

“Duffer”: Re the Bananaland drover
Broadhurst taking 2065 fat bullocks from Roslyn sta-
tion to Gladstone meat works without losing a single
beast (B. 10/8/’ll). That’s nothing. I knew a
drover who started from a station in the Gulf with
1200 mixed cattle, and arrived at Charleville five
months later with 1235 and a calf. He didn’t buy
any meat, alive or dead, en route either. But he
was one of the old hands, and knew his business.
