“Pick”: I cannot say at what rate the
death-adder dyes his clothes to match his new sur-
roundings, but, for a lightning-change artist, I can
recommend the jungar or devil-fish. If chased off a
sand bank, whereon his garments will be found the
straw hue of the sand, to the muddy bottom of the
lake, his coat will adopt the greeny-black of the
ooze, with a speed and precision that would do
credit to a Wadeite when his chief fires the gun.
“Mallarapoonie”: That wearisome old
bogey about the ill-treatment of natives in the nor’-
west of W.A. is threatening to bob up again. That
the niggers out there get a fairly rough time goes
without saying; but the saving feature is they are
a mighty tough race. As a general rule, they get a
fairly square deal, and any extra kind of rough time
they may hit against mostly arises out of white
fellows’ horse-play. Race hatred is practically non-
existent. Of course, the white man is severe when
he takes the law into his hand in cases of misdeeds
by natives, and the evildoer is mostly made to wish
himself temporarily dead; but the severity is mostly
tempered by justice. j.n fact, the of justice
meted out to his black brother by a rough but
decent class of white man in North Australia is
rather astonishing when one comes to recognise the
brand of it. It is the'justice of primitive man—the
horse-sense brand that obtained before hair wigs and
silk gowns were invented. As regards a remedy for
trouble with natives in North Australia, I arise to
remark that there isn’t any—none, that is, beyond
the ordinary course of Nature, which points to the
extinction, sooner or later, of the black race. In
the meantime, justice tempered with severity needs
to be handed out to both sides —especially the
white, and it is time the lower-class white got his
share. For a start, miscegenation (combo-ism)
should be declared a crime throughout the North of
Australia, and white men handed penal servitude for
harboring native women. That reform alone would
go a long way towards bettering the status of races
in the North. The usual misdemeanors (such as
cattle killing on one side, and ill-treatment of
natives on the other) can be dealt with as usual, if
usual is looked on in a common-sense light. Other-
wise, seeing that reservations for natives and- other
weird proposals are ’ foredoomed to failure, there is
nothing for it but to wait the aforementioned course
of Nature; but waiting isn’t much of a game unless
one has tucker and blankets with, him.
“K.V.8.” (from Bisjohn’s jarrah coun-
try) : Can any Aboliar tell me how to get rid of
the small red ant which abounds here in millions?
Recently we made jam, cleaned a wooden safe
thoroughly with strong lysol, stood the four legs in
tins of water, and stirred the confectionery. In the
morning the ants were in it—even into the jam
under the paper covers. A Mrs. Aboliar assured me
that they carry straws and float them on the water,
and thus get into the safe, but I haven’t found any
straws, and a million of them couldn’t eat a straw
in order to destroy the clue to their crime. They
seem to come from nowhere —suddenly loom into
existence like “Liberal” ladies at afternoon tea-time.
» * * *
“Eryandei-” : To “X-Burner” (B. 3/8/’ll).
The so-called “pine-adder” is not venomous, and the
effects mentioned were probably due to blood-poison-
ing, which may supervene upon the neglected bite
of any animal—man included. The only venomous
lizard known to herpetology is the Mexican helo-
derma, a repulsive-looking creature, which reaches
about a foot in length. It has a dirty brown-and-
yellow, warty skin, and, unlike the snakes, injects
its venom through an under-jaw fang. Also, the
gecko is a true lizard, and is consequently not
“allied more or less to the newt,” which belongs to
the Urodela, or tailed batrachians—the tailess ditto
being the frogs and toads. The salamander is an-
other tailed batrachian, and takes its name from the
fabulous creature that, according to the classic (not
American-Indian) myth, lived in fire, and died when
withdrawn from the flames.
“Billy T.”: I am old enough to have
learned that all the conventional rules for the judg-
ment of human character are practically valueless, and
would like believers to find a category for Mander-
son. I saw him blubber for 20 minutes when a fire-
bar at the Hand and Band burnt through his boot,
and singed a blister on his heel. But when Jacker,
the engine-driver, derided' him for the blubbering,
he waded into Jacker, and fought him nine violent
rounds. In fact, Manderson was as ready to fight
as he was to weep. He would cry dolorously while
he fought, but he wouldn’t stop fighting. He was
afraid of the dark, and when on night-shift he
walked half a mile round Pinnacle Hill to dodge
the Winda cemetery. He had a mortal aversion to
cats, and ran a wire-net about his bed because of
his belief that a large black cat, with yellow eyes,
would come some night and “suck away his breath”
while he slept; and he fainted like a woman on
discovering a tarantula on his wrist. He beat his
father, aged 78, with a weapon ingeniously devised
out of a cow’s tail loaded with battery sand ; yet he
fought Elliott’s bull with a pick to save a broken-
legged fox-terrier. And when the drive caved on
Bittern’s shift at the Hand and Band, he was first
of the rescue party into the black, broken workings,
and last to leave. He forsook Winda stealthily by
night to avoid a fight with a little fellow named
Hacket, who had kicked him halfway through the
township on the preceding evening ; and 18 months
later we heard of him receiving the Humane So-
ciety’s medal for extinguishing a charge in a mine
at Eaglehawk, and saving life. Where, then, is
Manderson’s niche in the popular gallery of human
sorts ?

“Mallarapoonie” meets the Dago under-
ground :
Antonio Superfluous (native of that delightful
Macaroni country which finds such a warm place in
the hearts of Randolph Bedford and others) slowly
finished rolling a cigarette, lit it from his candle,
stabbed the point of the “spider” into the Oregon
bulkhead that supported part of No. 1 plat in
the Great Fingall mine at Day Dawn (W.A.), and
spoke.
“Sefen year I come des Dubbla-u A. country
—fife year I wor-r-k des Fingall mine. Des Dubbla-u
A. country he goodt for-r weges; he no goodt for-r
live. Eetalee man he no stopa des countree for-r
long. Fife, seex, sefen year he worka da mine; he
safe da fife, seex, sefen huntre’ poun’. He go
Eetallee nefer come back.
“Nong, des countree lan’ no goodt for-r settle, no
goodt for-r live. Sefen huntre’ poun’ een Eetalee
ver’ goodt; growa da ongeh, da olive, da grape—
getta da wine shop een Meelan, no more work. Nex’
year I go Eetalee, nefer come back.
“Nong, des companee, da Bewick More, he no lika
da Austrelia-man for-r wor-rk. Eetalee man more
betta. Austrelia-man too much he talka da back-
ansa. By gorry, no more I hava da Austrelia-man
worka for-r me.; Las’ year I getta da beeg contrac’
for-r sheeft da battery san’- —ten tousan’ ton. Da
companee lenda da horse for-r pusha da truck.
Eetalee man he no much goodt for-r drive da horse.
Boss say, ‘Putta da Breetish man for-r drive da
horse.’ By gorry, I trya heem. He no goodt for-r
me. I getta da contrac’ ; I wanta da Eetalee man
for-r wor-rk. My countree man I tella heem pusha
da truck, he pusha da truck. Da Breetish man I
tella heem pusha da truck; he say, ‘Gout yer bleety
Ski ; you pusha da truck youseluf.’
“Nong, mister-r, des Breetish man no goodt for-r
me.”

“Jack Jay”: Another coincidence. Jor-
kins, after long and wordy koreros, had agreed with
Henare to buy the latter’s bit of earth. The day for
completing the sale came round, but Henare died that
morning. Jorkins, however, behaved so well at the
tangi that he had little difficulty in practically com-
pleting the bargain with Henare’s heir. But on the
morning of the final negotiations Henare’s heir was
also dead. In view of Maori superstition, Jorkins is
afraid to mention the matter again, lest he should be
the next link in the chain.
BAKEHOUSE ENGLISH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Why does the baker knead the dough?”
Enquired my son, aged six or sough,
And I replied in accents lough:
“Because we knead the bread, you knough!”
“But then, why do we need the bread?”
The same enquiring youngster sead. ’
“Because small boys have to be fead,”
Quoth I. “Now, off you go to bead!”
K.s.w.

PARLIAMENT.
Speaker Jenkin Coles (S.A.) is still so ill
that it again' seems unlikely that he will ever
go back to the chair of the Assembly.
Agent-General Kirkpatrick (S.A.), who
was due to retire in March next, has been in-
vited to keep going for another 12 months,
and has agreed to do it. “Kirk,” who was
one of the first Labor members elected to the
S.A. Parliament, was Chief Secretary in the
Price Government. For many years he was
a compositor on the Adelaide Advertiser.
Lewis Cohen has decided to drop out of
the mayoralty of Adelaide, having held it for
(in all) seven years, which beats E. T.
Smith and everyone else on the local record.
John Baker, who was moving up into the
mayoralty as a matter of course when Cohen
made a sudden reappearance in municipal
affairs two years ago, is in England, and the
position seems likely to go without opposi-
tion to Lavington Bonython, a young aider-
man, and son of Langdon Bonython, of the
Advertiser.
Died a week or so back, Charles T. New-
man, Australia’s wealthiest Methodist par-
son. Or, rather, it was the parson’s wife
who was wealthy; she was a daughter of
Sydney’s preposterously wealthy merchant
and coal-mine owner, Ebenezer Vickery—
the old gentleman who once writted The
Bulletin for saying he was going to be
married again in his old age, and then did
remarry within three months. Deceased
son-in-law Newman married his monied lady
in his old age, too, after a pretty strenuous
financial struggle; for the Methodist parson
has to cut things fine to pull through, and
Newman never had the “money sense.” Still
he was a nice, comfortable-looking old gen-
tleman, and, though he had a horror of his
singer-son going on the stage, he didn’t
wowse to any serious extent.
“Adelaide”: “Vagabond’s” pleasant idea
(B. 24/8/T1) of making church popular by
having refreshments in it, recalls a member
of one of S.A.’s best known squatting families
of a generation back. In summer-time, when
a party staying in the house had been driven
over to their country church, the youth’s
habit was to stroll out of his pew during
the hymn before the sermon, and hand a
hatful of peaches round among his friends,
to fortify them against the approaching
trial.
J. Maddern, now living in Adelaide, was
resident at Swan Hill at the time Burke
and Wills crossed the Murray. In the ful-
ness of their enthusiasm the residents a year
or two later decided to build an obelisk to
the memory of the dead explorers. Mad-
dern was instructed to build it, but when
part of the work had been completed, a stop-
page occurred for lack of funds. The monu-
ment was never finished, and the con-
tractor is still waiting tor his pay.
Joseph Vardon, Senator and Saint, has
just returned to Adelaide from the Crown-
ing, with an expression of Christian charity
on his face, and a deadly hatred of the
Labor party in his soul. But Australia made
a mistake when it thought that he was not
representing it as lie ought to do, for Sena-
-lor Vardon, when he was in England, visited
the London City Council, and, as lie says
in an awed voice to the press interviewers,
“sat between two carls!"
A Swedish scientific party of four, now
“doing” Australia, has put in nearly a year
in the north-west corner, and has just come
east. Dr. Mjoberg, the youthful leader, is
paying half the cost, the rest being found by
his Government. He says that European
museums know next to nothing of Australian
flora and fauna, and to help clear up the
ignorance he and his three companions
have secured about 1500 important speci-
mens—many of them quite new to him—and
thousands of smaller beasts. The West-
ralian Government has been very helpful,
and the grateful Swedes are going to see
that Perth Museum profits accordingly.
Sir Herbert Tree (who has dropped the
Beerbohm), speaking of his brother actor-
manager Alexander as “Sir George,” re-
marked in ecstatic parenthesis: “How fit-
ting was the honor the King has done him!”
It was fitting. “Knighthood” nowadays
should be conferred only upon people who
can wear it like a tin crown, and carry it
with artistic stage effect. The serious actors
—such as Tree and Alexander —have a right
to protest against its being given to mere
political performers of the George Reid
and late Tom Bent grade, who wear it like
a circus clown’s cap in association with a
low-comedy circumference and a waddle.
The late J. G. Hornblower, of South
Yarra (Vic.), left behind him a treasure
equal in interest to the old Geelong “Direc-
tory,” picked up lately by J. F. Hogan (B.
17/8/T1), which contained an advt. of
Henry Parkes’s ivory and bone-turning busi-
ness. It was a directory of Birmingham of
1830, in which was a business announcement
of John Holding, of Moseley-street, bone and
ivory turner, in the margin was a note
stating: “Henry Parkes was apprenticed to
this gentleman.” The note was probably
written by Hornblower himself, as he was a
friend 9!' Parkes’s in his boyhood. A letter
from him to Annie T. Parkes is printed in
one of arkes’s books.
“Lex” on a great battle of Costs:—
The Coal Vend case has been, amongst other
things, the triumph of B. R. Wise. His onening ad-
dress, which inspired Professor MacCallum to remariv
that the Sydney K.C. was “among the greatest living
masters of the English language,” looked an easy
feat on the surface, by reason of its clearness anil
simplicity of arrangement. It drew from Anti-Sosh,
which nowadays is none too partial to Wise, the re-
mark that everyone knew B.R.W. to be a brilliant
speaker, and that the effort proved no more than
that. His reply, which lasted 14 days, gave these
carpers their answer. Ten thousand documents were
analysed with meticulous care, and their contents
laid before the Court in the tersest, plainest terms
—an extraordinary feat of industry and physical
strength. Whichever way judgment goes, the Vend
case leaves Wise intellectually the undisputed
leader of the N.S.W. Bar.
J. F. Hogan, from London (16/7/T1) :
Cardinal Moran, at 80, can turn out “copy” with
the facility and smartness of some of the youngest of
us. To the new volume of that colossal Yankee
enterprise, “The Catholic Encyclopaedia,” he con-
tributes articles on St. Palladius and St. Patrick.
The latter would fill six or eight pages of Tiie
Bulletin. Of course the Cardinal has St. Pat’s life
at his finger-tips; still it is a notable achievement
for a man of his age. He says many people suppose
that St. Patrick’s conversion of the Irish to Chris-
tianity was “a series of peaceful triumphs, whereas
it was quite the reverse.” The Irish of 1500 years
ago were just as fond of a fight as they are to-day,
and St. Pat had a dozen fierce scrimmages with the
Druids and other Hibernian pagans of his time. But
he had more courage and determination than Palla-
dius, and he won in the final round. Palladius seems
to have been a cowardly sort of chap, who cleared
out of Ireland at the first sight of danger, and why
he was made a saint is a bit of a conundrum.

M.L.A. Hampson, in Victoria, told another
honorable member the other evening that
when the hon. member was in the South
African war he was court-martialled for
“pinching a bridle. The hon. member in-
dignantly cried “No!” and Peacock inter-
jected that a bridle was of no account in
those days of loot. But what was the story?
Hampson should have told a little more.
THE MA STATE'S NEW SPEAKER ,
HENRY WILLIS, WITH HIS EYE
ON THE OPPOSITION.
“Jimmy” _ Carroll, who has been acting-
Prime Minister of Maoriland while lousepli
Ward crept stealthily after the elusive
baronetcy, turned into the 55th lap on
Time’s track the other day. lie has been
a member of the Reps, continuously since
1887 and a Minister of the Crown since
1894, and he is so well-liked by palceha and
Maori alike that even his recent acceptance
of the cheap title which is thrown to Gaud
Mayors and flag-wagging linen drapers may
be overlooked in time.
Melbourne Argus recently made a splash
with the old story of the theft of the steamer
Ferret in 1881, basing the article on a book
published lately in America dealing with the
adventures of one Captain George B. Boyn-
ton. This book alleged that Boynton smug-
gled Chows into N. S. Wales, and received
150dols. a head from Quong Tart, of Syd-
ney, for them. The indignant denial which
this has drawn from Mrs. Margaret Tart,
Quong’s widow, concludes with the interest-
ing remark that the lady is preparing a bio-
graphy of the founder of the house of Tart,
which will appear in a few weeks.
All sorts of things may happen to a State
Premier in Australia. Verran, of S. Aus.,
with Treasurer Vaughan for a mate, was re-
cently exploring the sandhills of Eyre’s Pen-
insula, when the motor-car ran into a patch
with an unusually strong embrace, and re-
fused to budge. The chauffeur cursed,
Vaughan dug, and Verran pushed till the
perspiration dropped from him like truth
from an “Abo.” writer. But it was no good,
and finally the driver had to tramp 20 odd
miles for a horse, while the parliamentarians
put in the night in the scrub.
Widow Duerdin has just died, aged 93.
Her husband was not the very first Vic-
torian attorney, though he began in 1839,
and so wasn’t beaten by much. Sixty years
ago he built a mansion, with brick wall and
large garden, on the corner of Flinders and
Exhibition streets. There he had a fine
cellar; but he stocked it with law books in-
stead of wine. He and Clark were the rival
prosperous solicitors in young Melbourne:
the well-known firm to which he belonged
became Duerdin, Bronckhorst and Smale.
The fine solid mansion which he built is in
these days a “hostel,” which is Toorak for
hashhouse.
“Jimmy” Chamberlain, the best-known press-
man in Adelaide, has forsaken journalism,
and taken a job in the Tourist Bureau. Too
long ago to be remembered with comfort, he
started his inky career on the Advertiser,
and threatened to grow into its oldest in-
habitant ; but when the Labor Herald
turned into a daily, he sprang a surprise by
joining the staff as night sub. An enthusi-
astic bohemian, with a tenor voice like
cream buns, Chamberlain is pretty certain
to be a success in taking the fleeting visitor
gently by the ear and singing to him about
the wonderful things he’ll miss if he doesn’t
put in at least a month in S. Aus.
The late “Dinny” Keogh, member for Rose-
wood in the Queensland Parliament, was
best known to his intimates as “Shin Fane,”
his usual toast when observing the great
Queensland custom. Though a thoroughly
unreliable politician, “Dinny” was a good
storyteller, and many of his yarns were told
against himself. He was shingling a roof,
and _ had assisting him a newchum com-
patriot. The newchum was carrying up the
shingles. As he placed a bundle handv for
the just-elected member, Dinny said: “Pat,
you’d be a long time in Ireland before you'd
be handing up shingles to a member of Par-
liament.” “Faith, an’ I would,” was the
answer; “but, Mr. Keogh, you’d be a long-
time in Ireland before you’d be a member of
Parliament.”
Melbourne has three more “silks” at the
Bar—Leon, Gurner and Woinarski. And
M . C. Guest, the Commissioner of Titles, is
put up to the same dignity.
At Sotheby's, in London, the other day, a
Mr. Sabin gave £451 for the stray leaf of
the journal of Captain Cook’s first voyage,
m which the navigator mentions that he
gave the place “the name of Botanist Bay.”
George Long, the new Anglican Bishop
of Bathurst (N.S.W.), is a mere boy, as
bishops go. He is but 36 years of age", and
lias been only 10 years in orders; and seven
years of even that brief time have been
spent as headmaster of Trinity Grammar
School, Melbourne.
“B” recalls another case:—
Now that we know all about it, I think
Prime Minister Fisher got oft very well in his inter-
view with “Julia” Stead. Don’t you remember
Stead's description of his interview with Dr. Cook
on that worthy’s return from the North Pole? It
was in Copenhagen, and the crowd was full of
joyous welcome, and pressed close to the explorer.
“I was immediately behind him,” wrote Stead (or
Julia, as the case may be) ; “1 flung my arms round
him under his armpit. ... My face was close
against Dr. Cook’s left ear, and as we swayed back-
wards and forwards, 1 began a series of ‘Talks with
Dr. Cook,’ which I shall embody in this character-
sketch. I was close to him in all conscience. If I
did not hug him to my heart, 1 had a good hearty
grip of him round the chest.” Fisher explains that
Stead pushed into his bedroom for only seven
minutes (Julia stayed outside on the mat, I hope) ;
and as he doesn’t talk of having been gripped or hugged
or talked to through the left ear, he may consider
himself lucky.
The Oldest Inhabitant—still among the
Tombs —
In reply to “Tomaso” (B. 24/8/'ll), let me sav
that I never had the impression that Margaret Catch-
pole and Mary Haydock—afterwards Mrs. Raby (the
original spelling)—were one and the same. The
mere fact that the Launceston lteibeys (new spell-
ing) objected to the “Margaret Catehpole” play, did
not indicate that they were the descendants of Mar-
garet Catehpole. Forty years ago yellow-back edi-
tions of Cobbold’s “Catehpole” book were in circu-
lation, and book-hawkers used to say, “If you take
that book to Councillor I , he’ll give you half a
crown for it.” The imputation was that the learned
Councillor, being a grandson of Mrs. Reibey, was
anxious to buy up the book. But that was a mere
trick of the trade. Mary Haydock was an educated
girl when she came to Sydney ; Margaret Catehpole
was an uneducated woman, verging on middle-age.
There are some students of our history who say that
the woman buried by Parson Fulton, in 1819, was
bribed to personate Margaret, and that the parson
was unaware of the fact. The late George Matcham
Pitt, shortly before his death, at a good age, remem-
bered that his mother often spoke of Margaret Catch-
pole, and told how, when nursing one of the Pitt
household, she caught the infection and died. The
popular error in connecting the two women lies in
the fact that one came from Bury, in Lancashire,
and the other from Bury, in Suffolk, and that both
visited Australia for a somewhat similar trans-
gression. To-day it would be called “horse-sweat-
ing” or “illegally using.” There was certainly a
difference between the two transgressions: Margaret
tried to sell her horse; Mary threw the bridle over
the pony’s head and sent him home.
DR. HAM, CHAIRMAN OF THE
BOARD OF HEALTH (VIC.).
Labor Government or not, the legend in
N. S. Wales is still, “No Australians need
apply,” so far as the Education Department
is concerned. Despite the comparative
failure of the imported Mackie as Principal
of the Training College, and the Imported
Tearne as musical director, the Department
is chancing its luck with W. J. Elliott, M.A.,
Cambridge. This gentleman is to fill a
new position as inspector of secondary
schools; the ordinary inspector of State
schools, being merely Australian, is not
“class” enough. And then, when the good
billets are passed out to imported persons
with poorer qualifications than dozens of
local men possess, the Department wonders
whv it can’t get recruits for the service.
THE OLDEST INHABITANT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Professor Barry, of Melbourne, informs us that,
according to an eminent German anthropological
authority, Dr. Otto Schoetensack, Australia is the
only garden of Eden science can recognise. This
country must have been the cradle of the human
race, since it offered primitive man the conditions in
which a weakly, unarmed, comparatively-brainless
human could face the struggle for existence in the
absence of dangerous rivals such as would have beset
him in pliocene times on other parts of the earth’s
surface.
When Adam delved and Eve span,
Declares one Otto Schoetensack,
The couple were Australi-aw.
Within our groves the infant man
Tramped out the first known human track
With baby feet. Here wicked Cain
Put brother Abel out of pain,
And Adam ate that apple snack.
Here man possessed the perfect place,
Until his silly wife went wrong,
Imposing by her conduct base
The sad discomfort and disgrace
Of trousers on the human throng.
When Cain went journeying to Nod—
A course that’s been considered odd—
He humped his bluey to Geelong!
And East—in Eden—says the Book,
A splendid garden did He set,
And from this garden flowed a brook,
For which perchance ’tis vain to look
Where now the dusty yabby strays.
But from these proofs we do unfold
The fact that Havilah of old
Is called Yass-Berra nowadays.
VICTORIA.

The co. was floated in 1902 ; and, as Sydney had just
previously been making the Duke o’ York the excuse
for many extra drinks, the figures were so big that
when the two Tooheys asked £85,000 for the good-
will, the public simply rushed the brokers. Soon, how-
ever, the State’s beer began to lose some of its sparkle ;
and then it was realised that properties and other
assets had been valued on a wrong basis. As the
table shows, some very solid writing-down followed.
The Toohey estates came out of the reconstruction
with much credit; they voluntarily returned 50,000
of their £1 shares, and that and the writing-down
of all the other shares from' 20s. to 165., allowed of
the writing-down of the goodwill to £22,500 ; and
other assets were also reduced substantially. Even
then things were on the turn in Sydney, and in the
past five years the pot of prosperity has been grow-
ing bigger and bigger, and the froth on the top of it
has foamed higher and higher. Hence, after writing-
off £4OOO for depreciation of plant, the 6 p.c. div.
is nowadays very comfortably earned; in fact, the
past 12 months’ net profit was rather more than 10J
p.c. on the capital. Certainly, the Wowser is in the
land, marching up and down to see which pub he
will devour next; but, though he may succeed in
closing a pub here and there, he doesn’t seem able to
reduce the public’s appetite for beer. Whereas the
gross profits from brewings, etc., were only £31,629
in the first half of 1906, they were £52,921 in the
past six months—which doesn’t say much for the
Wowser’s success. Nevertheless, the Wowser has to
be reckoned with: and not only the confirmed
Wowser, but the man who is incline'd to vote for pub-
reduction on general principles. In the first place, it
costs the brewing co. a heap of money to fight for
“the trade” ; in the second place, this and other
brewing cos. own many pub-premises which owe more
than half their value to their licenses. It is impos-
sible to tell from the accounts how much of this
pub property the Toohey co. owns, because the man-
agement has the lumping habit, and piles the pubs
on top of the breweries, and buries the plant and the
rolling-stock in the heap. For the same reason it
isn’t possible to even get a hint as to whether a fair
amount is being written-off to cover depreciation.
The comparison probably isn’t worth much ; but this,
anyhow, shows how various items have moved: —
July, Jan., July,
1906. 1911. 1911.
Liabilities, less cash .. .. £55,569 £25,248 £44,596

Book debts are yery heavy; but the growth isn’t out
of proportion to the growth of the turnover. The
brewery business is very profitable—has been so for
very many years, and is likely to continue so for very
many more years. The only doubt of much conse-
quence is as to the probable future value of
hotel properties. If they slump, then, of course, big
bites come out of the profit from the sale of beer.
But, as before mentioned, there is no evidence as to
the quantity of this shrinkable variety of property
which the co. has in its possession.

Like almost every other concern in Queensland, this
one came a cropper over the boom of the early ’9o’s.
The State didn’t get much change out of that boom
except experience, and it is beginning to be doubt-
ful whether the experience did it much good. Any-
way, it is doing now many of the things that it
did then. Fortunately, so far the banks appear to
be keeping their heads, otherwise one would expect
that shortly there would happen just such another
disaster as put the State into sackcloth and ashes
in 1893, and kept it there for about a dozen years.
In fact, some of the old suit is still being worn —
by the Q. N. Bank, for instance, which, in spite of
the State’s prosperity and its own big business, is
still not in a position to pay a dividend. This
brewery co.’s share of the ashes was the writing-off
of one-half of the capital as lost. That cleaning-up
was done in 1902-3, whereupon the co. crept back
on to the div. list with a modest 5 p.c. ; and it
hasn’t felt justified in going beyond that since.
Still, good progress seems to have been made:—

Thus, in the past nine years (1902 was the last
year before the axe was used on the capital) an im-
provement of £30,447 has taken place in what may
be called the cash position, though certainly stock
and book-debts are hardly cash. It may, of course,
be that the co. has sold some freehold properties
or other non-liquid assets ; but there is no reason to
believe that it is so. “Freehold properties, build-
ings, machinery, plant, goodwill and rolling stock,
etc.,” have come down in book-value from £88,907
to £83,196 in the nine years; but that reduction
probably represents part of the amount written-off
against depreciation. The other part is represented
by new purchases. Assuming that the book-debts are
as good now as they were in 1902 and the stock as
conservatively valued, it seems, therefore, safe to say
that the co. has improved its position by at least
the £30,447 in the nine years, after paying the
moderate dividends. A shrinkage in profits is shown
for the past year as compared with its predecessor.
This is no doubt due to the fact that the three
Brisbane brewery birds haven’t been able to agree
in their little nest, and have been pecking one an-
other in an absurd manner.

The Maobiland State Fike Insukance Office,
which began regular business early in January, 1905
(the deficit up to then represents preliminary ex-
penses) :

1905.. £13,136 £2,418 6,738 £3,980 2,954
1906.. 20,991 10,312 7,215 3,464 6,418

1910.. 41,788 18,914 11,478 11,396 23,267
This office had a tough fight, which it didn’t go into
with much daring, but it appears to be winning
through. It began with an actual paid capital of
£2OOO, the amount being advanced by the State;
but, being a State business, every policy it wrote
was backed by the whole taxable wealth of the coun-
try. Probably never before was a policy issued with
such a guarantee behind it. The State had been
driven to take a hand in the business by complaints
of the excessive rates charged by the insurance cos.,
and consequently the excessive profits made out of
what nowadays is really an everyday necessity. As
soon, howeyer, as the State office showed its nose,
the rates were hauled down hand over fist; therefore,
the benefit conferred by this department on the
general body of insurers is enormously greater than
one would judge by merely looking at the office’s
own figures. According to the general-manager,
the reductions “ranged up to 33J p.c. on the greater
portion of the fire premiums for the Dominion.” It
was, of course, expected that that would lay the
babv-office in its little coffin. But the plan mis-
carried ; and the increasing success of the office
shows that very handsome profits can be made out
of the much-reduced premiums—which is the best
of evidence that the old rates were more than a fair
thing. Certainly it can’t be professed that in six
years the office has made a clear profit of £23,267
on a capital of £2OOO, after paying interest on that
capital. As a matter of fact, the greater part of
the £23,267 is necessary to cover unexpired risks.
And, anyway, a fire office —a small one especially—
can’t hope to strike an average of losses every year;
it may have a good run, and then a conflagration
may mop up not onlv that year’s premiums, but the
savings of a dozen others. Nevertheless, though the
office l is by no means in a strong position yet, and
though it ought to have reserves of at least £60,000
to fairly balance the risks it is taking, it is pushing
along famously. This is largely due to its ex-
tremely modest expense rate of £27 9s. 4d. p.c. of
the total income—a rate which shows an improve-
ment of £2 4s. p.c. on the previous year’s, and one
which, considering the size of the business, may be
regarded as extra good.

Some of those reserves are not true reserves, though,
and that applies particularly to the Depreciation
Fund. Steamships, tugs, city freehold, works, etc.,
are shown at their cost to this co. ; and these things
can’t be used without losing some of their value in
the process. This paper doesn’t know what the
depreciation amounts to. If, however, there was
even a 21 p.c. depreciation all round, then this
composite item lost £5857 of its value during the
half-year; and the true profit was £16,978, less
£5857, or £11,121. But, again, this paper doesn’t
know whether 21 p.c. is a fair thing to deduct. No
doubt the value of steamships, tugs, engineering
works, and some of the other assets shrink more
than 5 p.c. p.a. ; but, then, city freehold is
thrown into the heap, and city freehold doesn’t
waste to that extent. Then there is the Insurance
Fund, which is really an accumulation of premiums
to cover a risk that hasn’t expired. So that isn’t
the best of reserves either. Still, making a very
liberal allowance on that account, the net profit is
very handsome. The position can be put this way:—

In the second half of 1908, £20,000 of new capital
was got in. The excess of liquid assets over lia-
bilities three years ago was £22,994 ; it is now
£38,335, or £15,341 more. Meantime, £52,131
has been added to the non-liquid assets ; .that repre-

cost. Thus we get this: —

That is 3 years’ record —in addition to the divi-
dends paid, and anything that has been spent on
maintenance, renewals and repairs. And £47,472,
or £15,824 a year, seems a most ample provision
for wear and tear and contingencies and everything
else —ample and more than ample.
The co.’s original capital was £75,000; at that
figure the assets taken over from three shipping con-
cerns were capitalised in 1895. Subsequently the
inter-State shipping business looked up wonderfully
partly the result of Federation, and partly the result
of one of the good old honorable understandings—
and in 1904, on a revaluation being made it was
found possible to put 100 p.c. of water into the
stock. This represented undivided profit, and no doubt
some writing up of assets that had previously been
written down too hard. Subsequently £2O 000 of
new capital was got in at par, making the present
capital £l. 0,000. In the table the rate of divi-
dend is computed on £95,000 ; if £170,000 is taken
instead, the past year’s 16 p.c. is reduced to 9 p.c.

If a punter at the pony meetings produced a list ot
his annual net winnings, it could scarcely be more
bumpy than that column which shows this co.’s pro-
fits. The big variations appear to be mainly due to
unavoidable speculations in raw material. Thus, in
1904, for instance, there was a sharp rise in the
price of wool. Usually a rise of that kind is passed
on to the consumer, which is perfectly legitimate;
but unfortunately the Maoriland manufacturer of
woollens is up against very keen foreign competition
all the time. Moreover, a lot of those competitors
deal so dargely in shoddy that a rise in wool isn’t of
very tremendous importance to them. So, as a rule,
the local manufacturer can’t put up his price—any-
way, not to make good a sudden jump at the wool
sales. No doubt it cuts both ways to some extent.
Presently, for instance, after the prices of woollen
goods have been gradually shoved up, there may be a
slump in the sheep’s overcoat; and then it usually
takes some time for the price of woollen goods to
slide back again, and while the gradual slide is in
progress the co. is in clover. These circumstances
no doubt mainly account for the remarkable un-
evenness of the profits.
In taking a general view of the profit column, it
must also be remembered that this co. has been
gradually increasing its capital. This shows the
total at various dates (including calls paid in ad-
vance) :
1906 .. £80,240 I 1909'.. £116,515
,1907 . . 94,145 1910 . . 123,204
1908 . . 103,726 I 1911 . . 122,901
This £42,000 or so of new capital has enabled the
co. to reduce its outside liabilities a trifle. In 1906
they totalled £62,724 ; they now stand at £42,543.
The outlook for trade is said to be satisfactory ; for,
though the Customs protection isn’t what it ought
to be, more and more people are patronising home-
made goods as a matter of principle and patriotism,
and that spirit will no doubt go on spreading.
Therefore, the co. feels justified in launching out still
further: over £lO,OOO has been spent in extensions
of buildings and plant during the year. One insect
in the ointment is the reaction which is almost cer-
tain to hit Maoriland and disorganise its trade
when the present borrowing boom stops—especially
if it stops suddenly; and that’s how borrowing
booms mostly do stop, just as Billjim’s jamboree
stops with a jerk when Bung shuts down on the
supply of snakes and other liquor.

This co. issued 25,000 new shares at par last year,
instead of getting a small premium for them, as it
should have done; and some of the money thus
raised has been used in reducing the mortgage,
which, nevertheless, still stands at £45,000. A
mortgage is a very good thing to get rid of; but as
the co. issued the new shares at par, and as it has
now again sprung the dividend, it means that it is
paying 8& p.c. to get rid of debt which was costing
something no doubt under 5 p.c. The raising of the
new capital isn’t, therefore, likely to hasten the
mortgage off the premises. The simplest way to
hurry the incubus off would be to keep the dividend
modest, and apply surplus profits to the purpose;
but the co. has evidently a thirst for dividends, and
for the fourth time in four years the rate has been
bumped up. As a matter of fact, this isn’t a con-
cern from which big dividends ought to be expected.
The capital is £125,000, and £103,625 of that is
represented by real estate. Certainly there is the
previously-mentioned mortgage resting on that estate;
but even if you deduct the mortgage, there is
still just upon half of the capital represented by city
property; and when you’ve got your money out on
the gilt-edged security of city property, you can’t
reasonably expect to get a high return for it. This
paper, therefore, regrets that the dividend has been
again increased. The co. may be able to earn it
with a fair amount of comfort to-day; but these are
abnormally prosperous times, and when the times of
the other sort arrive there is always a disinclination
to pull the rate of dividend down again, with the
result that depreciation is neglected and a crop of
trouble is planted. The amount shown as written-oif
for the past year is the balance of the money spent
some little time ago on “additions and improve-
ments,” and the writing-off has been a most proper
thing unless the outlay meant the actual production
of increased revenue. Apart from the mortgage, the
co. owes £22,330, mainly trade debts; and its own
book debts run to £30,028.

The paid capital now stands at £78,125, made up
of 75,000 shares fully paid to £l, and a recent lot
of 12,500, issued to shareholders at a premium of
2s. 6d., and on which 2s. 6d. has so far been called
up. The co.’s profits have been steadily climbing up
the ladder, and, assuming that full provision is made
for bad debts and depreciation, the present dividend
is very comfortably earned. Further, though the
co.’s profit is so handsome, there is very little of
other people’s money in the business; total liabili-
ties, including a few deposits, are only £9626.
The risks are, therefore, considerably less than they
would be if the co. borrowed heavily to lend out
again. Of course there are risks even if the co. only
uses its own money ; for the eo. may be making- ad-
vances too liberally. And that is the uncertain quan-
tity when you come to consider this as an investment
Here is a co. with a capital of £78,125, and reserved
of £33,927 ; and £100,625 —or practically all the
capital and reserves—is represented by “advances on
mortgages, pledges and other securities and other
assets ; and you simply have to take the manage-
ment s word that the exceedingly mixed lot of assets
are worth the money. To add to the difficulties of
valuation, there are branches in Victoria, Queensland
and Westralia. Still, the co. didn’t go into the busi-
ness yesterday or the day before, and if it doesn’t
know how much the family jewels are worth, then
the chances are that the knowledge is utterly lost.

total amount written-off in the past half-year,
£ooo is applied to an extension of works account
and that might possibly be better added to the re-
serves. The total to the credit of this account, and
not included with the reserves, is now £2OOO. Ex-
tension of works is, however, so far as most gas cos.
are concerned, merely another way of providing for
depreciation. A co. has (say) 10 miles of mains,
which are undergoing the inevitable process of de-
cay ; and instead of dropping some revenue into a
stocking to presently renew that length of pipe, it
uses the money to lay another half-mile of line.
Whether this co.’s extension of works fund is that
kind of depreciation reserve, isn’t certain ; but, to be
on the safe side, the amount is herein written off. The
other £lOOO written-off in the past six months goes
towards reducing the book value of the co.’s pre-
mises. Besides the gas business the co. does a little
—a very little—in the way of electric lighting; but
it professes that the latter isn’t profitable. Possibly it
doesn’t want it to be. Nevertheless, somebody else is
evidently going to try it; for, according to the latest
reports, the capital is assured for the big scheme for
harnessing up the Great Lake’s water power and
turning it into current for the use of Hobart and
other places. This will give the eo. such a com-
petitor as it never before had in its life. Its financial
position isn’t particularly strong. Seven years ago
it had a few hundreds with its bankers; to-day it
has an overdraft of £5219. Thus, all of the revenue
earned in excess of the dividends paid has gone into
plant, etc., and about £6OOO besides. Still, there
certainly is something to show for the money, inas-
much as the sales of gas reached £14,266 last half-
year, and of electric current £3546, whereas seven
years ago the figures were £11,209 and £lB2l.
Albury Brewing and Malting Co., Ltd.
(N.S.W.), whose profits have been going from bad to
worse, hopes that it has got out of its difficulties
by amalgamating with the Beechworth (Vic.) Brew-
ery. The new concern is to be known as the Border
United Co-operative Breweries, Ltd.
* * * *
For the first half of this year Australia
exported to Europe 156,115 tons of lead ore and
concentrates, and 253,889 tons of zinc ore and con-
centrates, a total of 410,004 tons —mostly concen-
trates—representing in all more than a million tons
of crude ore out of the mines. The local producer
received at the rate of £6 3s. 2d. per ton of lead
concentrates and £2 13s. sd. per ton for the zincif-
erous stuff. The 414,004 tons returned Australia
£1,640,200 ( £962,023 for the lead and £678,177
for the zinc) and it is quite safe to say that the
net profit derived from the half-year’s shipment of
zinc alone amounted to £250,000“ Still, the money
Australia got for the goods was little compared with
the extractable value of the metals. As to the zinc
material, at least three times as much would be got
out of the concentrates as Australia received. The
balance goes to foreign smelters’ profit, and in paying
heavy freight on metal and on the dirt that is asso-
ciated with it, and for foreign fuel and foreign
smelter labor, etc. It represents a prodigious waste
of a national _ opportunity; and the sooner some
method is devised of keeping the highly profitable
job of metal-extraction in Australia the better. Just
at present we are merely supplying putative enemies
with the sinews of war, because hardly any of this
stuff goes to J. Bull.
* * * *
Placer tin mine, Germanton (N.S.W.),
had buyers in Melbourne at 12s. the other week,
but was soon knocked back to Bs. or thereabouts.
Yet the original syndicate is professedly confident
that the first big clean-up—-next month —will estab-
lish the character of the property. But the big holders
of Placer scrip are not experienced in the ways of
tin fields, so it may be that their calculations are
based on dreamy assumptions. They have added
another block or two to the original property, and
are issuing new contributing shares, which move is
not likely to inspire public confidence in their con-
fidence.
About the most cheerful remark in the
chairman’s address at the Broken Hill Prop, meeting
related to the expected surpluses from zinc de-
liveries: “If the present high values of metals are
maintained until the close of this year, the adjust-
ment of the old contracts for the sale of zinc con-
centrates—the last deliveries of which were com-
pleted in December, 1910 —should yield us a hand-
some surnlus, as the terms of such contracts provide
for settlement on the basis of the average prices for
the year following that in which delivery of the
concentrates was given.”
The contract made by the Broken Hill
South for the sale of its zinc tailings to the De
Bavay Co. is panning out all right in view of the
fine rise in spelter. When the agreement was made,
4s. a ton was reckoned a very fair thing as a flat
rate; but the co. stipulated for an equal division
of profits after the De Bavay people had pocketed
an amount equal to the flat rate. As the result of
that stipulation, it is estimated that the South’s
tailings are now worth to it from Bs. 6d. to 9s. per
ton.
Broken Hill Block 14 has found a N.S.W.
mine at last. When it struck blanks in its own
deeper levels, it set out to look for properties else-
where, mostly with the eyes of J. H. Fawcett; but
one after the other the options have been turned
down. Finally, the Torrington wolfram and bis-
muth mine, near Deepwater, satisfied the co.’s en-
gineers ; and the co. is paying for it £ 5000 cash
and 27,000 shares in a 150,000 share co. The
agreement stipulates for £20,000 at least of work-
ing capital, and this and the purchase-money are to
be provided by declaring a bonus of 2s. 7d. per
share. If Block 14 shareholders don’t want one
share in the subsidiary co. for every two in the old
one, they can take the cash. The Torrington has
68,000 tons proved, and the engineers agree that
the bulk of the stuff can be mined and milled for
6s. per ton. It is confidently expected that there
are good profits ahead, though few figures are yet
available under that heading. The co. will hold
23,000 shares in reserve.
Though things haven’t been quite so good
lately, the Jembaicumbene continues one of the Ma
State’s consistent dredgers. On a paid capital of
£13,200, the past year’s profits (to June 30) was
£3835, after paying £476 for machinery repairs

The most tnoderh, simple and effective Vacuum
Slimes Table is the “Wilfley.” It is and
Consists of a revolving drum which is partly submerged
in » tank of slimes or material to be filtered, The
drum is covered with a filter cloth supported of)
grooves on the drum, which are connected by means
of holes to the interior *td allow the liquid to be
withdrawn. Through the trunnions at the ends of
the drum the vacuum pipe passes to the interior.
This pipe connects with the interior of the drum,
through which the liquid and air are withdrawn by
means of a vacuum pump. The filtered slime is re-
moved by a simple apparatus placed at or below the
level of the pulp in the tank. So that directly the
cake is removed, the surface of the filter cloth as it
revolves is immediately re-immersed in the pulp
ready to receive a fresh cake, thereby preventing the
sucking in of air, and consequent loss of vacuum
between the time the cake is removed and a fresh
one is forming. The residual liquid in the slime
cake is removed by distributing water over the sur-
face of the semi-dried cake. Owing to the fact that
the filter is circular in form, it will be seen that it
is continuous in operation, and has no complication
of valves to get out of order; furthermore, work is
being done continuously during the whole revolution
of the filter drum. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company, of South Melbourne, have the sole manu-
facturing rights of this filter.* [2

* * * *
When P. W. Tewksbury looked afield for
something to put his dredging experience into, he
and his friend J. C. Watson had hopes of a S'Afri-
can proposition, and the Mynpachts Gold-dredging
Co. was formed. The proposition, however, wouldn’t
stand the acid; so S’Africa was abandoned, and an
arrangement made with an Araluen (N.S.W.) co.
to secure some ground. The new co. that was
formed—the Victorian Araluen Dredging Co.—has
got to work with the dredge intended for the Myn-
pachts Co., and the first nine weeks’ work showed
gold worth £2546. The V.A.D. Co.’s accounts were
made up to June 30, after five weeks’ operations,
and that run showed a working account profit of
£854. The present position is that of the 30,000
V.A.D. Co.’s shares the Mynpachts Co. owns 20,000,
and when the divs. on these have wiped out a
debt of £6547, the V.A.D. shares will be divided
among the Mynpachts’ shareholders, and the old
co. be wound up.
Bull Ant (W.A.) shareholders, meeting
in London, eagerly assented to liquidation and dis-
tribution of assets. Doolette, the elder, said that
the Board had considered the Wisdom of getting
hold of another property, but had ultimately decided
to barrack for winding up. Lionel Robinson fol-
lowed, and said that the acceptance of the offer of
Bullfinch shares in exchange for Bull Ants, made by
Doolette, jun., and himself, was purely optional, and
he wanted it clearly understood that there was no
compulsion about the matter. Neither he nor the
other fellow recommended Bull Ant holders to take
Bullfinches. At the Bullfinch East meeting, held two
days later, it was announced that at least 6s. per
share would be available for distribution by the
liquidator. Although winding up, the co. does not
intend to abandon its Bullfinch holding for the pre-
sent. Arrangements are being made to have it
worked on tribute, and if the tributors find anything
worth having, the first chance of subscribing more
capital for the purpose of again working the show
is to be offered to existing shareholders.
Cheerful item of interest to Bullfinch
shareholders, received by the last London mail:
“The fully-paid shares so far issued number 458,000,
including 8000 of the option shares ‘called’ and paid
up in full. Another 12,000 shares have been ‘called’
and partly paid, and a further 8000 to 10,000 are
likely to be ‘called’ very shortly. The option-holders
agreed to exercise portion of their options in order
to put the co. in possession of funds for development
and plant. If they had not agreed to do this, it is
probable that debentures would have been issued, or
possibly even a reconstruction called for; and that
would have given the public a worse ‘sickener’ than
it has suffered from alreadv.”
* * * *
Gwalia Proprietary (W.A.) has been at
it again. The directors’ report for the past twe
years shows that the amount spent on development
stands at about one-fifth of the sum subscribed at
the last reconstruction. No estimate is given of the
ore developed as a result of such outlay, nor is any-
thing further said about the “large body of ore in
the northern workings,” which some time ago was
reported to “average 525. per ton.” At the last
meeting shareholders were filled up with the old
yarn about the great value of the property; and,
given the money asked for, the directors seemed pre-
pared to take their oath that things would turn out
much better in the future than they had in the past.
About £25,000 was then put up, and the principal
items of expenditure in the accounts now to hand
are: Directors, £1116; offices (two) and expenses
therewith, £1480; consulting engineer and manager,
£1166; development, £4465; shaft sinking, £606.
The question arises, Is it worth while going on ?
Large sums of money have already been lost here in
attempts to carry on the business of gold production
at a profit, and if, after all this expenditure, the co.
cannot do any better than it is doing, it seems like
a waste of money to go further.
Ida PI. (W.A.) has completed its im-
provements above and below, and expects to resume
crushing and mining on a more economical scale
almost immediately. Nothing is yet known to the
public as to what quantity of ore is to be put through
the mill in future, but in all probability a smaller
tonnage will be treated and a compensating increase
made in the grade. Some borehole prospecting was
recently started in this mine, but as no information
has since been published, it may be taken for granted
that the drill hasn’t so far struck anything worth
blowing about.
Oroya Exploration, Ltd. (W.A.), has
issued a circular containing a list of its belongings.
From this it is gleaned that the co. has 150,000
yuanmi, 90,000 Mountain Queen, 60,000 Oroya-
Leonesa, and 50,000 Oroya-Black Range shares; also
it owns the Queen of the Hills and Transvaal
(Westralia), the Babylonia (Nicaragua), and several
other mining odds and ends in other parts of the
world. The directors don’t guess how much the co.
is going to make out of its big share interests, but
in the case of the Queen of the Hills the profit in
sight is computed at £102,000, that in the Baby-
lonia at £60,000, and that in the Transvaal at
£40,000. These three shows will either be equipped
and worked by the co. later on, or floated off as sub-
sidiaries. According to the present price of shares,
the valuation put upon the co.’s miscellaneous lot
of real and personal estate is £215,625. It would
probably be much higher but for the fact that the
co. is heavily weighted down with financial respon-
sibilities, and may want further help at any time.
At last something is being done to see if
Norseman (W.A.) is a goldfield at depth. Hitherto
a deeply-rooted impression has prevailed that it isn’t,
on account of an unfavorable change of country cut-
ting out everything of value at a few hundred ver-
tical feet from the surface. The Mararoa and
Princess Royal cos., however, think otherwise, and
have started drilling to determine whether they are
right or not.
“M’poonie” : The big mining cos. of West-
tralia are laying up trouble for themselves by stack-
ing the newly-arrived Dago into mines in contra-
vention of the law, and to the injury of the home-
gTOwn miner. Presently, when the Australian
miner can’t get a job in Westralian mines, the
A.W.A. will wake up and make the biggest indus-
trial row heard in this State. One of the worst
transgressors is the big B. M. Co., which is absorb-
ing every available Italian or Austrian immigrant.
The firm’s mines are full of Dagoes, and its newest
acquisition, the big Youanme, is being almost en-
tirely worked by Italians. It is a matter of common
knowledge, too, that some Westralian mine officials
are in the habit of abusing their authority and the
rules of common decency by accepting backsheesh
from Dago workers; and that howls in the home-
grown miner’s ear that he isn’t likely to get a fair
deal from those bosses.
* * * *
The same old thing is happening in North
Queensland. The Chillagoe Co. having been em-
powered to buiid the Etheridge railway, and pouch
the profits, direct and indirect, is now pushing ardund
a petition imploring the Government to spend
£15,000 on unproductive roads leading to the
various stations and sidings. The idea is that low-
grade or refractory mines might be opened up with
the help of traction engines. And no doubt some-
thing of the kind will have to be done. The incident
proves over again that the authority which owns
the unproductive roads should also own the produc-
tive rails.

hasn’t been yarded by the Mount Elliott Co. yet; A

After prospecting poorly for 136 ft. of
driving, Hampden’s Duchess level, at 550 ft., has
entered a shoot of good ore. The last 21ft., from
136 ft. to 157 ft. north, gave an average assay of 24
p.C., and the face shows even higher grade ore for
a vVidth of 48in. This is a splendid development;
but Hahipden needs it, for the co. is spending a pot
of money On prospecting and development work, and
is also making costly additions to smelting plant.
Last month’s return of 612 tons of matte, contain-
ing 2io tons Copper, 2040 z. gold, and 28450 z., silver,
would Certainly not pieet all the expenses. It may
be easy to issue another hatch of debentures, but all
this borrowing will prove a heaVy burden on future
production. Assuming that the Various mines Con-
tain 20,000 tons of Copper, a profit of over £2O per
ton is required to equal present market Value, plus
the debenture debt.
* * * *
Charters Towers still feels pretty gloomy.
Day Dawn crushings from Mills’ United, Hew Bril-
liant Freehold and East Mexican are all good; but
this alone will not hold the market —reserves of pay-
able stone are essential too. Mills’ United is cross-
cutting from its two deep winzes below No. 14 level.
This prospecting is 300 ft. deeper than the present
stopes. The crosscuts are in diorite, and if any
mineral stone is struck, the market will come to life
again. Mills’ crushing for the fortnight Was 1012
tons for £3163, and its dividend of Is. per share
costs £16,000. The Freehold, next door, crushed
832 tons for £3lOB. There is no important new de-
velopment work there.

Mungana (Q.) has got its Girofla mitle
ready for stoping a few thousand tons of silver-lead
sulphides. It expects to be able to supply Chillagoe
furnaces with 70,000 tons, and, in anticipation,
Chillagoe is adopting the Dwight Lloyd process for
desulphurising and sintering the crudes, preparatory
to smelting.

No. 1 North Victory enlivens Gympie(Q.)
with a crushing of 211 tons for 9680 z. gold. This
good return is followed by a dividend of Is. 3d. per
share. No. 2S. Great Eastern is not in so much
favor, as it is doubtful how the Inglewood formation
will turn out. Trial crushings have been poor ; still,
there is a good deal to be done in opening up the
1750 ft. level, and driving another crosscut at 2000 ft.,
before a definite opinion can be formed about the
future of the show.
* * * *
Blair Athol, Central Queensland’s biggest
coal producer, whoops in a circular that it is going
to do bigger things on its own account, now that
the southerners have turned it down. Electrical coal-
cutters have been put in, and an endless wire-rope has
been installed for haulage. The co.’s present book-
ings are equal to 8300 tons a month, so it looks for-
ward cheerfully to a monthly output of 9000 tons
when odds and ends are counted in.
* * * *
Local opinion in Stawell (Vic.) is rapidly
inclining to the view that the old place is 'going to
have another life by working the enormous bodies of
low-grade oxidised ores on modern lines. Although on
paper these deposits may appear hardly worth inves-
tigating, yet shillings here go as far as dwts. on
some other fields ; and, altogether, the conditions in
regard to mining, etc., are so favorable that the
record for economical working of low-grade ore may
eventually be held by Stawell. In fact, as far back
as the early ’Bo’s, ore, ranging from sdwt. down to
2dwt. (and more of it approaching the 2-weight mark
than the five), was mined at Stawell, carted a mile
or so to a public battery, and crushed with profit to
the mines. This ore was raised, moreover, by parties
of working miners, in most cases without a penny
of capital to assist them, and neither the dray that
carted the stone to the battery, nor the battery that
did the crushing, was the property of the men who
raised the stone. In many cases also the miners had
to pay tribute out of their crushings. _ Only one
plant is now dealing with this or*, but it is demon-
strating that a payable extraction can be obtained
by a combination of the battery and cyanide pro-
cess; and, though the problem has been tackled in
only a primitive, experimental way, there is good
reason for the belief that an up-to-date _ plant,
thoroughly equipped with the best labor-saving de-
vices from mine to mill, will yet demonstrate that
Stawell possesses a valuable asset in its huge de-
posits of oxidised, un-complex ore.
* * * *
There are a few little Victorian claims
that have a way of piling up profits. The Ajax,
Davlesford, set out about three years ago on what
seemed to be a weary way, but, hitting good stone,
up s:oes a record of £197,866 of gold won. This is,
roughly, 50,000 oz.; and as dividends have piled up
to £90,750, £1 16s. out of every oz. won has
found its way into the nocket of the shareholder.
The wages account is now up to £72,259, so that
it isn’t the Fat miner on this occasion who has
swallowed up profits. Directors’ fees look even worse
than wages, as they tot up to only £780; but then
Ballarat Boards float lightly when a good market is
about. All this has been got on a paid-up capital
of £BBOO. Therefore, the money sunk at the out-
set has come back 10 times over. What’s more, the
Board won’t confess to exhaustion. The average of
the yields has come down, certainly; but Phil Lewis,
the manager, holds out that he is going to strike
something in the No. 7 level crosscut east and on the
west lode at the two bottom levels and the No. 4
Speaking generally, Lewis is hustling along well,
and doing his pioneering without fuss.

Bright Star (Vic.) has still the big-
boulder disease ; but that wouldn’t matter so much
if the dirt in between was richer. The half-year s
gold was worth £1929, while exes, ran to £1784,
plus £235 spent in altering the dredge. One div.
was paid during the half-year, and another just
after it closed ; but these things obviously were not
done out of the half-year’s crop. Moreover, on July
31 the co. had only a hundred or so of cash in hand
after providing for outstanding accounts. The co.
has chewed up 17 acres altogether, and has 106
acres still on its plate.

* * * *
Hercules (Tas.) is shipping larger quan-
tities of lead sulphides to Zeehan, but no doubt the
Is. dividend is in consequence of a favorable adjust-
ment of the zinc ore account with European buyers.
A rise of £5 in spelter is Is. per unit, so 30 p.c.
zinc ore nominally increases 30s. in value. Simi-
larly, Broken Hill’s 45 p.c. stuff increases 455. in
nominal value when spelter rises from £22 to £27.
But the smelter only pays for extractable metal, and,
moreover, makes liberal deductions against metal
losses and other things, so the actual zinc-produCef
doesn’t land anything like an extra shilling a unit
from the recent rise.

* * * *
Not oil from shale —soft* Soothing oil— —
would Taslnanians apply to De BaVay, Monty Coheri’s
cheniist. They would scarify him and rub in vitriol,
biting, burning, destroying. For De Bavay has
kicked their Latrobe oil-drum so hard as to about
burst it, Cohen, De Bavay and their pals put down
£5OOO before they threw up their option ; so they
evidently meant business. They first had De Bavay
out to spy the land and take samples. The next big
gun was D, Currie, Of Briseis, who arranged the
sampling, and they then went in for boring. This
programme convinced them, first, that there Was
more coal than shale on some of the blocks; next,
that there was less money in the treatment of the
oil than in flirting with zinc products at Broken
Hill. So as they are not out to-day to be coal barons,
but to be oil kings, they throw Latrobe On the
scrap-heap.
'The Australian-owned Tongkah (Siam)
produced 1294 tons of black tin last month, making
a total of 4054 tons for the last three months. There
were poorer yields in April and May; nevertheless,
it seems reasonable to expect a total of 750 tons for
the current half-year to September 30. But nothing
is known about the price that has been realised; and
the dredging of tin ore from ? salt water harbor is
such an unusual proposition that it certainly isn’t
safe to simply assume the stuff to be worth 70 p.c.
of metal.
* * * *
The success of the Tongkah Co.’s dredg-
ing in Siam sent numerous prospectors and option-
hunters on the chase over the Malay Peninsula and
the East Indies; but they have had mighty poor
success. In those few cases Where the prospects
were any good, the Wily Chinaman usually had his
finger in the pie, and when terms were mentioned,
John opened his mouth so wide that he could have
swallowed an alligator, let alone a poor, unsophisti-
eated Australian option-hunter. Among the con-
cessions that did look to have a hope was a big
mineral and timber area in Borneo; but the people
who got it, the Kendawangan syndicate, _ turned it
down after sending two people to probe it; and as
subsequent inquiries about other alleged El Doradoes
gave no better prospects, the shareholders got a
little liquidation dividend the other day.
* * * *
A hullabaloo is being raised in the innei’
circle of the Sydney Stock Exchange, because certain
Melbourne firms have cut into sundry preserves.
Sydney abhors the advertising broker: Melbourne
doesn’t care a kick about advertising, but it tears
its hair over “recommending.” But when it comes
to partners opening up in Sydney, and doing biz. on
the barest of corns, allowable —well, that, isn’t either
“advertising” or “recommending.” It is just nip-
ping biz. at cheap rates from the old ’uns. Two can
play at most games, and it would be easy for (say)
Joseph Palmer to send young Joe to Melbourne.
Still, that wouldn’t remove the canker, because the
cutting would merely extend. Then the gentle
public, having once tasted cheap
Wouldn’t want the old scale. And it is the old
scale that keeps up the motor-car.
* * * *
Said the Man from Tanami: “Yes, there’s
gold there all right, and payable, too—though one
must take all the stories of jewellers’ shops with
plenty of sauce. Water ? Tons of it! Beautiful,
clear, cold water, too; just as if it had come
through limestone beds. In fact, I think it has.
There are two bores, and both strike water at about
176 ft. They are sunk between the reefs —which run
east and west, with a dip westward. There area ,
many people there; it’s about the Dead Centre. But
it’ll come, it’ll come. I’m going back there next
week, via Port Darwin.”
* * * *
Burma Mines, Ltd., in which a heap of
Lake View money is sunk, again thrusts itself on
public notice. In six years all the subscribed capi-
tal has vanished, and, in addition, there are liabili-
ties on debentures amounting, with interest, to over
£214,000, besides other weighty indebtedness. Last
year’s revenue from sales of lead totalled £l6l 1,v, 9.
Costs absorbed £149,000, exclusive of Lm-
ciation. Addressing shareholders at uie annual
meeting, F. A. Govett said that a new o i body,
containing silver, copper, zinc, lead, etc.,
had been intersected in the old workings.
As to the financial position, he was pleased
to state that this year the co. had a credit
balance, whereas during the previous years
it had to borrow from its bankers. But he might
just as well have added, while he was about it,
that the co. owed just as much as ever, and that its
credit was bv no means unlimited. When the co.
took over the show in 1905 it had 150,000 tons of
prehistoric slags, a maze of burrowed workings, and
the off-chance of finding the leavings of a payable
lead mine underneath the ancient workings. Ol
course, as The Bulletin remarked on a previous
occasion, the hifalutin prospectus and the inspired
newspaper puffs said something different; but
well, the public had to be got in.
* * * *
Investor: (1) Prospects good. (2) A
gamble. #

there last year, being the largest record for the
last seven years, and that the average value, £145
per ton, was, with one exception, the highest that
has ruled for the past ,20 years. The quantity of
bOche-de-mer exported, however, is less than that
of anv of the last five years, but the average value
was £ll per ton higher. Some of it brought £l9O
