There’s not anything in the
world so refreshing and at the
same time so harmless as a cup
of pure tea, and nobody knows
it better than a nurse who has to
sit up night after night.
Of course, there are some
teas nicer than others, and some
teas better for you than others,
but "Robur” is the tea you
should get, it is nice and it is
refreshing, and above all you can
depend upon it at all times being
absolutely pure. Besides, it is
cleanly and carefully packed,
and it is very saving. As a
matter of fact, it is the best all-
round and most reliable tea
procurable and that’s saying a
lot, but it’s true.
The Nurse
QUALITY—BUCHANAN WHISKY
“BLACK & WHITE.”
A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, September 4, 1011.
My Dear Arini, —
When Melba opened the grand opera sea-
son here as Violetta in “La Traviata,” she
flattered on to the stage like an elegant
balloon, puffed out with the broad acreage
of flounces - that bulged through the Traviata
period. Verdi seized Dumas’s “La Dame
aux Camelias” by her white throat, and
wrung her neck in a swan song. Dumas fils
may have built Camille on the frail struc-
ture of a notorious Frenchwoman; most
likely, as George Eliot said, with violence,
a thousand persons unconsciously sat for
one immortal portrait.
As Violetta, Melba stuck to every detail
of the Greater Clothes-bag epoch in dress,
and wore her hair smacked down on her
head. To modern eyes, “unbecoming” is a
(ame swear-word for this kill-joy coiffure.
The vast circumference of the green skirt,
with a shimmer of gauze and a mile or two
of lace flouncing—Violetta’s first aeroplane
appearance in the great banquet revel —
showed the tragic white camellia caught
with diamond dewdrops, on a front selection
of flounce. The wide, lace mantel-drape on
the corsage was illuminated by a 2000-
candle-power diamond necklet. In this
magnificent first act, Melba’s skylark tx-ills
worked an agitated, herring-barrel audience
into a state of mild frenzy. A few dozen
yards of lace over daffodil silk, and a volum-
inous oyster satin frou-frou, with sparks of
light shooting over its bodyguard of scintil-
lating drape, accompanied Violetta’s silvery
song through the second and third acts.
And, finally, she died with distinction in a
white foam of chiffon, with a cloud of the
same frothy lingerie, edged with darker
velvet, flung over her shoulders.
Tenor John McCormack threw his en-
chanting voice over Violetta’s corsage during
the greater part of Traviata. He’s a charm-
ing stage lover after the Marcus Stone style
—rather more beefy about the waist, per-
haps—and has an instinct for color both in
song and in dress. His swallow-tails —-the
tint of a purple plum—with white lingerie
at the wrists, help the gorgeous color-scheme
in the scene in which Violetta’s friends eat
no supper because Mr. Angelini expects
them to sins- in chorus.
As Flora, in this tearful opera, Australian
Rosina Buckmann has her first big chance;
and she seizes it with a bold, dramatic
grip.
Pitt-street, in the neighborhood of Hex-
Majesty’s seethed with excitement all day
Saturday. The gallery, which paid from
ss. to 7s. 6d. apiece for its roost, tumbled
up to its sky-line early in the afternoon.
Before “Traviata” turned down the lights,
some portion of this enthusiasm got a large
bouquet off its chest, per wire rope, from
gallery rail to manager’s box. The fragrant
vegetables were aimed at Mrs. Grace Miller
Ward. Hugh Ward examined its address
and handed it to the lady, whose bow to the
ceiling was an immediate Act of Grace.
In an opposite box the musical McQuades
sucked up sweet sounds from Melba’s honey
notes, while the hoop of diamonds round
Mrs. McQuade’s black hair flashed a fixed
white light. The Chisliolm-Marks party
was just below, the sleek beauty of Mrs.
Herbert Marks garbed in raven plumage.
An adjacent lady in white was considerably
undressed about the chest, with a film of
glitter supporting the situation. Mrs. An-
thony Hordern’s graceful head reared a nest
of brown curls over a fluff of white ostrich
feather boa, and with her Mrs. Pat
Osborne clothed in expensive elegance,
showed an opera cloak that was some kind
iff modern miracle in white lace and cobweb
gauze. Mrs. Ernest Raleigh wore Queen of
Sheba earrings, long, luminous slabs of
jewel, and other good-looking attire. Mr.
and Mrs. George Tallis were comfortably
wedged into the manager’s eyrie; and dress
circle and stalls looked like slices of female
Resurrection turning its bare bosoms to the
Recording Angel.
John Lemmone made his first public at-
tempt to pipe his lay on the same occasion
that saw Melba’s debut in Melbourne, when
the fledgling songbird sang “Ah fors e lui”
from “Traviata.” It was at a benefit con-
cert given by the Liedertafel to its then con-
ductor Elssaser, who had been ill. Elssaser
has been tuning up his harp for years in a
place where benefit concerts are not adver-
tised. Melba sang “Ah fors e lui,” on Satur-
day night triumphantly; and Lemmone, too
excited to sit still for more than five min-
utes, doubtless scratched the “little doors of
memory” for a dusty glimpse of that other
first night, when waggon-loads of flowex-s
did not pass up over the conductor's back
hair to tlie lady singer’s feet.
Mr. Charlie Mitchell, the Skylark’s best-
known brother, and son George Armstrong
came from Melbourne for the first night,
and scampered back on Sunday.
Tenor Giulio Ciccolini, of the Melba Co.,
is young and beautiful, and was almost in-
nocent of the English language till Sunday
afternoon, when some wild city swans took
him a-motoring. As he neither drinks nor
smokes, they could do no more as hosts
than fill him up with strange sounds, pro-
fane and otherwise. Then they brought the
guileless Ciccolini back to the Australia,
oozing with excited English, which he
worked off on serious-minded citizens. “Does
whisky and soda make you muzzy?” and
“I’m a bosker” are mentioned as now being
in his repertoire.
An ancient inhabitant tells me that the
gallery concert at Her Majesty’s on Satur-
day afternoon was on all-fours with the
efforts made to sneed the hours while Sydney
waited for the first high kick of the Leslie-
Farren co. The recent frivol before “Travi-
ata” sent the curtain up, was an organised
business; a president was elected, and the
incidental piano, which had been hauled up
to the gallery, was exploited with a good
programme. Bert Fahy had memorised the
scores of “Merry Widow” and “Madame
Butterfly,” and did his little bit on the ivory
keys; Hilda Aiken, Elsie Peerless and Syd
McDonald lifted up their voices in song.
Elsie was the last gallery warbler, and her
lilt, with a final E flat, caught the ear of
Melba, who wafted the girl a flattering com-
ment on the range and purity of her music-
box.
H. N. Southwell was handed a Melba pre-
sentation matchbox on Saturday, so he lias
lost any excuse he ever had for carrying
wax vestas loose in his pockets. With the
Melba gold box dangling from one portion
of his person, and a heavy gold case from
Paderewski (also for the necessary match)
climbing down his waistcoat buttons, the
resourceful Southwell is laying up a useful
store of bullion.
KOROLOWICZ- WAYDA, Dramatic Soprano,
Melba Grand Opera Co.
Photo, by Rembrandt , Berlin, Charlttbg.
Sydney’s authority on decoration sends
me this:—
Melba’s colleague, Cisneros, whom you pictured
last week, is the finest woman at present perambu-
lating our continent. She is not wholly culpable.
Art has conspired with Nature to make her so. I
saw her on the day of her arrival in Sydney, and she
reminded me of the Goddess of Liberty, Pallas
Athene, Diana of the Ephesians, and the Spirit of
the Australian Commonwealth. Surely some artist
in frock-structure builds her garbs—this phrase is
just right; “frock,” “costume,” and “ladies’ tailor-
ing” fail to describe the robing of her majestic sym-
metry. She wore a dark picture hat and a fawn-
colored over-garment which reached to her heels, bor-
dered by a broad band of crimson silk. The nine-
inch cuffs on the long sleeves, and the pointed turn-
over collar were of the same material. She did not
walk, she progressed; and the crucial test of her
magnificent ensemble is that I do not remember
what her face was like.
The wattle tossed its golden spray all over
this city on September 1. In honor of the
event the unemotional motor broke out in a
new place, and flaunted nodding plumes near
its front window; the serious cart-horse
waved a bloom that scratched his winkers;
and the Girl got into a lingerie blouse to meet
the first warm breath of early summer, and
perspired gently behind a yellow haze. In
the evening, Acting-Premier Holman exploded
at the King’s Hall in eloquent defence of
national sentiment. Curator Maiden, one of
the fathers of the Wattle Day junketing,
took the chair. Hon. Sec. Mrs. Agnes
Kettlewell, who mothered the Wattle Infant
from its first squeak; Mrs. David, Chief
Hatter Storey, Bruce Smith and other mag-
nates and minors took a hand in the King’s
Hall floral riot.
Carrie Lanceley’s concert to help five hos-
pitals got into the shadow of grand opera;
nevertheless, the Town Hall was fairly well
filled, and the Girl did her best in clear
soprano tones to make an artistic success of
it. The string orchestra, which accompanied
Carrie’s first two numbers, Elgar’s “Plead-
ing” and Amy Woodford-Finden’s “Re-
quest,” gets off with a caution for its inci-
dental efforts to swallow up the Girl’s softer
notes by too-vigorous fiddling.
St. Cecilia Choir (feminine gender) is
young in spots, and fairly fresh in its voices
—also in spots. Some of it has a wilted air,
and seems too tired to open its mouth wide
enough to let the sound escape. One back
row of the choristers at the Lanceley concert
sang through its nostrils, with a slight auto-
matic movement of its upper teeth. The
choir wants whisking up—it’s too flat on top.
At the same concert Percy Herford’s aggres-
sive phrasing of Gounod’s “She Alone
Charmetli My Sadness” had a fist in it; and
Pearl Palmer got through her attack on the
piano keyboard with considerable success.
The Admiralty lady, who lent her ground-
floor to the D.A.D.E. (Depot of Arts and
Domestic Economy) discussion the other
day, met the visitors with a soft smile on
her lips, and a bunch of violets in her hand.
The little daughter hung about Lady King-
Hall’s dove-grey drapery as juvenile under-
study. The problem to be discussed was:
“What are the aims and methods, etc., of the
new Sydney Club?” and the pot-plants near
the dais tottered under the arguments. The
Acting-Gov.’s lady took the chair. Mrs.
Bassett, president of the D.A.D.E., tossed a
long white feather in her black millinery;
Mrs. Moseley dilated on the duty of the
“Working Gentlewoman” towards button-
holes and other tribulations connected with
the home-made stitch; the Calico Knight’s
lady inserted a wedge of praise for the new
Depot scheme; and Mrs. Brown-Eyed Mac-
kinnon put on the pace in the matter of
pickles, hams and other domestic virtues as
practised by the pioneers of New England
(N.S.W.).
The Society of Artists had a mild revel in
students’ work last week. Souter’s cats will
have to earn their living catching mice in-
stead of licking their chops on dados, etc.,
if one girl-student goes on specialising in fur,
whiskers and claws; Miss Josephson’s way
with pussy’s tail is getting up alongside the
expert Souter’s. Miss Lovett, who took Sid
Long’s place, has sold her little lot of painted
ware to an appreciative public. A tall blue
vase in the French style shows a Nymph’s
Saturday night out; another handsome piece
of crockery by this artist is a clever grouping
of attractive damsels taking a sun bath. Mrs.
Ashton exhibits some china of charm, and the
studio walls bristle with ideas in oil, char-
coal, and other mundane mediums. Julian
Ashton, still fighting, has his ears pricked
to catch the clink of the hammers that may
some day get to work on the foundation-stones
of the Beeby Art Palace.
Lieut.-Gov. Cullen was in charge when the
Prisoners’ Aid Association wagged its tongues
in council the other day. The Town Hall
vestibule was the scene of this annual record
of the Society’s efforts to help the bird with
the broken wing to hop out of its cage with
an olive leaf in its beak.
Cards to view a meeting called “The Syd-
ney General Poor Relief Fund” lately flut-
tered across the city. Then a hurried denial
from Lieut.-Gov. Cullen of the statement that
he was “Patron” dropped in to the morning
papers. Gov. Chelmsford is “patron,” and the
Acting-Gov. isn’t going to steal this perquisite
behind his Ex.’s back. An icy postscript now
chases the card-to-view —“Meeting can-
celled.” Sydney General Poor Relief Fund
seems to be suffering from an effusion of ink-
pots to its head.
The National Council of Women nobly
gave the Women’s Conference another chance
to get some of its excess language off its
chest (it had been restrained by a tiine-limit
bell at the regular meeting), by gathering it
together one evening in the Royal Society’s
rooms. Refreshments and remarks were
served up at the same time.
Various Australians with a Scottish Sab-
bath in their parentage have recently
strayed through Edinburgh’s best street in
search of ancient Holyrood. George and
Mary have been temporarily in the presen-
tation business there. Probably Holyrood,
with electric light turned on its moth-eaten
history, had Rizzio’s “blood-stain” spring-
cleaned for the occasion. Having person-
ally inspected this splodge of red paint, I
know it only wants a new lick about once
in 10 years or so, a coat of dust being
rather helpful than otherwise to its occupa-
tion as a Rizzio relic.
The late Chief Justice Darley’s daughter
Sylvia, whose fair thatch was prominent at
Government House when the unattached
Gov. Beauchamp was shepherded by sister
Mary, was among those present at the Holv-
rood pilgrimage. 1 fancy that Sylvia’s first
husband, a Baronet Anstruther, was search-
ing in the Jenolan Caves, or thereabouts,
for longer-life microbes, when he chanced
across the Darley family. He was Heredi-
tary Carver to the King; but nis speedy
demise passed the royal joint, as well as the
family fortune, on to his son Frederick. It
is alleged that when this infant trekked
across Holyrood, George asked him if he
really could carve. Widow Sylvia lost her
title by dropping into matrimony for the
second time, and is now Mrs. Algernon H.
Tracy.
Two ancient Australian commercial
firms will presently break out into a
joint orange-blossoming. Major Richard
George Bright, a son of C. E. Bright,
C.M.G., and the Hon. Mrs. Bright,
of Elstree, Hertfordshire is to be
the bridegroom, and Muriel, a daughter of
baronet Robert Lucas-Tooth, the bride. Mrs.
Bright, the bridegroom’s mother, is a daugh-
ter of Viscount Canterbury, who was Gov-
ernor of Victoria when the Duke of Edin-
burgh was gaining experience of divers sorts
in Australia in tli6 late ’GO’s. She married
C E. Bright, of Bright Brothers, at St.
John s, Toorak in 18G6.
Maoriland is collecting its spare bawbees
for the purpose of giving a new musical find
a chance to make a name for herself in
Europe. This is Carreno’s opinion on the
subject: “All over the Avorld people come to
me and ask that I should hear them play.
I hear them. I say, ‘Ah, yes, your playing
is very fine, very good, all that. I compli-
ment you.’ But I do not say, ‘You have
soul. You have a future. Come home to
Berlin and study with me.’ But that is
what I say to Miss Renee Queree.” And
Miss Renee Queree is naturally feeling very
good about it.
From an old hand in London :
Another vanishing source of revenue for the singe-
ami instrumentalist. is the decay of the “musical
evening in fashionable houses—dry-rotted bv
“motor-cars and the growing habit of week-ending
in the country. So aspiring and ambitious Aus-
tralian maidens who fancy they have unrecognised
Melba voices had better think more than ever be-
fore migrating’ to the modern Babylon.
Lamberti, the impersonator who finished
his season at the Tivoli this week, carries as
a charm a bullet which his sister picked up
on the battle-field of Magersfontein during
the enraged argument between the British
and Boers. The lady, who is Dr. Barrie
Lambert, was the only fully-qualified doctor
on the nursing staff in S.A. during the war,
and anybody who wants to write in appre-
ciation of her services will find her practis-
ing in Maida Vale, London. Inci-
dentally, she is the only lady lecturer em-
ployed by the London County Council, and,
altogether, earns about half as much money
as her brother. If she earned only a quarter
sue could keep pace with the 'fashion in
motor-cars.
Mr. and Mrs. Charles Marsden (she was
a daughter of the late Mrs. Mary Thorne, of
Prestonville, Potts Point) and familv left
by the Osterley for Foaland. The Mars-
den eldest son has developed wireless tele-
graphy bumps, dabbles in mechanics and in-
vention, and will inquire into the habits of
modern machinery in the Old World.
St. Vincent’s Hospital ball will occur in
the Town Hall in October race week. The
Denman Excellencies have been borrowed, and
a gilded Government House retinue will
clank its heels through the official Lancers.
Will Shakspeare’s characters have been
snared to give a tone to the fancy-dress sets.
(Shakspeare, by the way, only emerged for
use after several scratchmittee gather-
ings had severely jumped on his corpse.)
Non-fancy attire may also, however, waltz
across the ballroom. Mrs. G. Lane-Mullins,
Mrs. T. H. Kelly, Mrs. Kenna, and Mrs.
John Hughes are helping to push the results
into a fat cheque.
Concord Ball, which is heavily gilded with
vice-regal and Admiralty House patronage,
will take place at Cabarita on September 12.
The Western Suburbs’ Cottage Hospital is
the cause of this outbreak. Hon. Sec. S. A.
Kemp and Hon. Treas. J. J. Shipley are
shoving the ball into the public eye.
Surf-bathing clubs’ vice-presidents sprout
like mushrooms all over sea-side resorts;
and the Bondi Club battled with the growth
in a general meeting last week. It had 110
vice-presidents, and wanted to slay a score
or two, for the annual guinea which is sup-
posed to be a vice-president’s thanks-offering
doesn’t always roll in, and, when it does, it
isn’t compensation enough for the regiment
of hon. officials. After the slaughter, 98
vice-presidents were amongst the missing.
Twelve apostles remain to preach the gospel
of the Bondi breakers; amongst these sur-
vivors are Macarthur Onslow, M.L.A., and
Mr. Oakes.
Yours, with a broad nib,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, September 4, 1911.
My Bear Yarrie, —
MisS “Auntie” Phipps, of State Govern-
ment House, received a mild shock during a
recent vice-regal visit to a country town.
She and a devoted aide wandered off to see
the sights, and went into a small shop to
make a purchase. The lady behind the
counter was inclined to be friendly, and
chatted away as she tied up the package.
“You’re from England, aren’t you?” she in-
quired. Miss Phipps admitted it. “I thought
you were,” said Mrs. Shopkeeper. “I picked
you by your accent. You speak just like
Mr. Thompson, the new man at the butcher’s
opposite. He’s an immigrant, too.”
The Fuller nursery contingent has not
been seen at any public function for some
time now. At the beginning of the reign,
never less than three of the quiverful were
trotted out at a time, with Auntie Phipps or
Aide Awdry as guide. Now it is Auntie
and Aide minus the minors. In many a
suburban household there is a mutual feel-
ing of unrest and a strong yearning to lead
the Fuller life.
Lord Charles Scott, who died the other
day, full of years and medals, created a stir
in our Sassiety circles in the ’Bo’s by marry-
ing an Australian girl—Ada, daughter of
Colonel Ryan. The Ryans had a lovely
country house at Macedon, with a Garden of
Eden attached. Of late years, the blossoms
thereof have been rented to a florist—which
must be rather sad for the garden-makers.
On one memorable occasion, the Ryans gener-
ously gave the flowers of one week to char-
ity, and realised thereby the sum of £5OO.
A distinguished member of the family is
Mrs. Rowan, the flower painter. In her
maiden days she lovingly tended the Eden
and acquired a knowledge of botany.
Major Quilter, the big Grenadier, has
marched across the border and gone on to
Sydney to make ready the way for the Den-
mans, who go north after they have escaped
from the maze of machinery and animals
which is the Royal Agricultural Show.
Last week’s “Chatter” alleged that a top-
paling had to be shifted from the way of the
G.-G. lady in the hunting field. Hunting
may have been made easy for some other
Diana of the chase, but for Lady Denman —
never! The G.-G.’s lady is an accomplished
rider, with an intrepid soul, and if anybody
slipped a top rail off it was a wicked asper-
sion on her horsemanship.
Ethel Irving and her hubby, Gil-
bert Porteous, were invited to tear food
at the Melbourne residence of the G.-G. Den-
mans. Former Governors rarely unbent so
far as to sit at meat with the most cul-
tured or clever on stage or platform, though
one Excellency was not so averse to theatri-
cal or other amusing company under —well,
favorable circumstances. On one occasion
he asked a leading lady to enjoy the pink
lights and privacy of supper a deux. At
another time he smote a visiting singer
with a polished request to come to lunch.
Both invitations were declined —the second,
it was said, in the haughty terms of an in-
jured lady.
Dolores, in a golden gown and a seraphic
smile, sang to a delighted Town Hall audi-
ence on Saturday night. “I am always
eager for new songs,” says the gentle wo-
man. “So much that is exquisite is buried
in old scripts; I sigh because I cannot learn
them all.” However, she has learnt so many
that her repertoire, to which she is continu-
ally adding, is more extensive than any
other singers. She is always searching, and
her happiest way of “resting” is to burrow
into dustheaps for song-gems which have
been forgotten for years. She arranged a
beautiful string for Saturday’s recital, and
told them off in a setting of spring flowers
—pink plum petals, violets and primroses.
Suburban Sassiety has been worrying to
discover the identity of the elegant young
man with a gold-rimmed monocle whose
photo, lately appeared profusely in the pic-
ure papers. The talked-of stranger is Nor-
man Raphael, son of a prodigiously wealthy
Raphael, whose money moves in the highest
English circles. Young Norman is touring
the universe on an allowance of £lO,OOO a
year or so, with his cousin (male sex) for
pal-de-voyage. He is a horsey swell —the
pater is noted for expensive steedflesh —and
some of the time when he isn’t lounging at
Melbourne Club he whiles away in riding
along Alexandra Avenue, which reminds him
faintly of deah old Rotten Row. in his way,
the charmingly-outfitted Norman Raphael is
most impressive.
Herbert Wertheim, son of Hugo, is matri-
monially engaged to Miss Daisy Lucas,
daughter of the late Charles Lucas and sister
of lawyer Hugh Lucas, of this city. She is
a white-capped sister of the Children’s Hos-
pital, which will lose one of its most capable
nurses when she says good-bye to the wards.
Another engagement. The contractors this
time are Dr. Deravin, brother of Dr. Arthur
Deravin, the golfer who figured prominently
in the recent tournament, and Bella Illing-
worth, a big, blond*, country girl, who is by
way of being an heireC?. She was the ac-
knowledged belle of Fleet Week, and dangled
from her belt the scalps of many of Uncle
Samuel’s star-spangled officers.
Two sprigs of Sassiety contrived to wed
last week without any advertisement what-
ever. Ethel Graham, daughter of politician
George, was one conspirator; the other was
a scion of the Hope-Campbell clan. Only
the nearest relatives of the pair got within
shoe-throwing distance. The guests had to
be few or many, as the family connections of
the bride’s people extend to regiments of
Grahams, Wards, Coles and McCraes. The
newly-weds are off to Westralia, where the
bridegroom has urgent business to look after.
Ruby Glover, one of Melbourne’s prettiest
girls, has said “Yes” to Mr. Godfrey Gar-
rick, of Taviuni, Fiji.
Even more imposing than the wedding
march down the aisle is the Block parade by
the bride when she returns from the honey-
mooning. The most recent Toorak wife
promenades, proudly wearing a beautiful
frock, a lace butterfly in her hat, and the
superior air of the newly-wed. As a rule,
she has a double escort, a leisurely brother
sauntering at her disengaged elbow, and
helping her Indian captain-husband to show
her around.
Lieutenant-Colonel C. R. Gaunt, brother
of the two naval Gaunt captains, has been
given command of the Fourth (Royal Irish)
Dragoon Guards. As a family, the Gaunts
are giving Bull valuable help to rule the
seas and govern the land.
The other day, at Buchan, where a Bush
Nurse has just commenced work, a com-
mittee-woman was riding, to a meeting, when
her horse threw her heavily. Nurse Hurnall
hastened to the injured lady and skilfully
patched her. The patient, who is now doing-
some of the mending herself, fervently
thanks her stars that she helped to get the
scheme extended to her district.
The Staughton dame threw open St.
Neot’s to the Swastika Literary and Dram-
atic Circle t’other Monday night, for the
Club’s initial performance. For the occa-
sion the acre or so of ballroom was dis-
guised as a tropical jungle, with palms and
smaller vegetables. Agnes Jansen had
charge of the musical portion of the pro-
ceedings, and sang impressively. Unie
Russell and Beatrice Cox played the French
scene from “Henry V.”; and Mr. A. O.
Nicholson sang some weird love songs, one
of them by Henry VIII., composed, pre-
sumably, during one of his searches for a
new Mrs. VIII. I listened attentively for
the thud of the headsman’s axe, but couldn’t
catch any souad resembling it. Mrs. Jan-
sen evidently does not believe in Sousa
methods; John Philip would have introduced
a sort of wood-chopping contest somewhere.
Another ditty was by Charles No. 1, and it
sounded just like what might have been ex-
pected from that sad-faced Tory.
A cotton-wool snowstorm in a state of
suspended animation hung over the slippery
floor of the Glassy Harem on Thursday
night, when the only carnival of the season
took place. All Sassiety that retains the use
of its limbs struggled into fancy dress, and
glid over the pepper-and-salt surface. The
Frank Madden girls, wearing Dutch caps,
drew a daffodil car after them; Guido Bar-
acchi had the Watson heiress, Marie Hall,
and two other pretty girls on a blue string;
and John Madden’s Beatrice, in an emerald
green dress, and with an old woollen shawl
round her head, looked the real colleen.
The duetting Gregor-Woods and Miss
Hume Black, with her ’cello, gave a pleasant
recital in Beale’s Rooms, on Thursday after-
noon. R. F. Fox tore accompaniments
out of the piano, and, in the interval, the
cheerful clink of teacups and saucers was
heard.
The Victorian Artists’ Mask Ball, to be
held on September 15, promises to be the
most hilarious function on record. An iron
rule has been made that revellers must come
in fancy dress and remain masked until a
late hour. The artists have designed them-
selves alluring posters, which catch the sob-
erest citizen’s eye, and set him yearning for
gaiety. These works of art are to be sold
at the dance by a brisk auctioneer. The grate-
ful Marshall Hall, for whose Conservatorium
the hop is being will supply the
music, and it will be plentiful enough to fill
the vast Town Hall. The Gaud Mayor has
lent the whole pile for the occasion, and the
picturesque balcony will be fixed up with red
livhts and awnings, which will shelter, yet
not conceal, the masqueraders from the curi-
ous eye of the man in the street.
Dutch-Australian painter Scheltema has
a mixed exhibition of paintings at Tnckett
Chambers. The artist’s old, likeable arrange-
ments of cattle and Australian bush are in-
terspersed with some reverential bits of
British scenery. There are pictures of Arun-
del and Stirling Castles, and the beech-bor-
dered walks on the Duke of Argyll’s Inver-
aray estate. The one Dutch item shows
some yards of canal supported by a sky of
lead and scrambled egg. A solitary figure-
subject displays a young lady who looks too
bored to worry over the way she is painted.
The gilded frames of the pictures are very
successful, and four of the Scawtch subjects,
also several of the cow pictures, found pur-
chasers right away. Scheltema did well to
choose Show Week for his display, and, as
an exponent of commercial instinct in art,
he deserves a playful dig in the ribs.
The grey-towered house which frowns over
the Yarra was the scene of a polite battle
last week when the Widow Staughton, who
had secured the Denman lady as chief
patroness for her Try Boys’ dance, arranged
her forces for the first committee meeting in
one of the sacred drawing-rooms. Brand-
new aides and secretaries hovered about, and
longed for a glance or two at an up-to-date
“Who’s Who?” The ha/tted women, ranged
in neat rows, waited and conversed in
hushed whispers till the place seemed like a
prayer-meeting, and the pamphlet-burdened
aides had the air of hymn-book distributors.
At last two tall purple-and-silver circum-
stances strode softly into the room, carrying
a Chippendale table, which they placed near
the fire. Two more purple decorations fol-
lowed with silver ink-bottles and one pen.
Then the leading lady slipped quietly in,
looking slender and anxious in a powder-
blue house dress.
“President’s Day” brought the tourna-
ment of last week at the Royal Golf Club
Links to a sociable end. As a compliment
to the womenfolk, there were mixed four-
somes and doubles, and the daffodil-decked
clubhouse re-echoed the gossipy clatter of
cups and saucers. The soda syphons were
not absolutely silent; but they hissed in a
properly subdued way. Women in garden-
party rig overran the place, while a sprin-
kling in the knitted coat and cap of the
female player sat around and discussed the
games. Scobie McKinnon, big and florid
under a verra Scotch cap, filled the part of
host. Alister Clark, a keen man with the
sticks, and the donor of the decorative blooms,
was also in evidence, ready and eager to
name tne beautiful products of his wilder-
ness-garden for admiring visitors. Depart-
ing motor parties bore off armfuls of daffo-
dils and wild flowers gathered on the links;
hut the floral harvesting is only permitted
by the male hosts on ladies’ day. The Royal
is purely a man’s preserve, and is so closely
barricaded that an outsider could not get in
with a battering ram. Also, it is so well
.stocked with members that after the plea-
sure of being proposed is over, the golfer
has to wait on the mat for about five years
before room can be found for him on the
greens. Its most distinguished member is
the Hon. Michael Scott, who just failed to
land the Irish championship last week. Scott
should be a crack player, for he pitched his
villa at the very gates and golfed regularly
as long as the light lasted. Other members
have done well “abroad.” Recently T. H.
Fitzgerald left his sheep long enough to go
to Cannes in the south of France, and wrest
a gold bangle, donated by the Grand Duke
Michael of Russia, from a herd of other
goffing competitors.
The Stuart Murrays have planted a new
house within sight of the little “Royal” flags.
It is a big, blank-faced, red cottage which
stares from the rising ground fronting the
club’s domain. The owners motor round to
the clubhouse in a new car, which bears
some resemblance to an icecream waggon; it
is pale butter color, and has a double-striped
awming. Dr. Leary is another enthusiast
who cannot live more Fnan a coo-ee away. In
his recreative hours, the clubhouse ’phone is
the likeliest instrument to find him with. The
ancient game never seems to lose its hold on
its votaries. After playing all day, Mrs.
Stanley Riddell, an old golfer, was once dis-
covered playing by herself in the dusk. The
sun had long departed; but the keen-eyed
enthusiast was waiting until night abso-
lutely dropped the curtain.
Those of us who have been praising George
Dancey’s panels at Her Majesty’s may well
spare a word to mention Albert Enes, who
painted the female figures adorning the roof.
Enes is a local man ; with a fine faculty for
handling greys in his decorations.
Minnie Grant Bruce, who wrote “Little
Bush Maid,” is about again, looking very
white and wan after a spell of illness. The
“Maid” has gone off very well, and is still
moving at a hand-gallop. A new book by
the same authoress will be on sale shortly.
On September I (Wattle Day), Mel-
bourne Writers’ Club farewelled Rose Bol-
drewood, authoress of “The Complications
at Collaroi.” “Rolfe’s” youngest daughter
is shortly to sail for South Africa (where
the fashionable novel grows nowadays). She
will live in a farmhouse and gather material
for a book in which all the popular literary
props will be served up attractively. Rose
looks like being the only member of the
family upon whom the ink-stained mantle of
the father may hang without a wrinkle.
Dr. Leary, the young golfing medico of
Sandringham, has gone over to Sydney for
the Medical Congress with a learned paper
in his kitbag. The suburb by the sea re-
cently Suffered a serious epidemic of diph-
theria, and he had the opportunity of study-
ing it closely. It seems that anti-toxin,
though it cures the trouble, does not destroy
the germ, and the convalescent is a source
of danger to others. Leary reckons he has
found a way to destroy the germ, and he
will set out to try to prove it when his turn
comes to speak. Medicine is the family fail-
ing of the Leary family; his father, three
brothers and two brothers-in-law all live
behind brass plates.
The Old Exchange, from which spot The
Bulletin looks at Melbourne, was put up
to auction last week; but the highest bid
was a paltry £90,000, so the propputy was
withdrawn. The Money Millers are the
owners, and they have enough spare cash to
be able to look at £90,000 without getting
excited.
My Adelaide correspondent writes: —
Steeplechase Day on Saturday was a dream of
Spring frocks. Lady and Miss Bosanquet made their
first public appearance after their Queensland trip,
and both looked well. Lady Frederick provided the
sensation of the day, by paying a dividend of £79
10s. in the first race (after which half the women on
the course won.dered why they hadn’t backed her—
“because of the' play, you know”), and The Greek,
for A. P. BfTVvman, and Tadanga, for “Dick” Hawker,
landed some more big stakes where they weren’t par-
ticularly needed.
The Adelaide Hunt Club had its run in the morn-
ing, to leave sporting members free for the races after
lunch. One of the fences brought disaster to Mr. Lang-
don, of Melbourne. Rumor at first made the case as bad
as might be, but later reports speak of “slight” im-
provement.
By the way, the S.P.C.A.’s new purchase, the horse
ambulance, made its initial appearance at Victoria
Park. Its first official act was to remove the slain
Killamey.
Our seven-times Mayor (Lewis Cohen) turned the
gaunt Exhibition building into a “Moorish palace”
on Thursday night, and asked 4000 people to dance
in it. Miles of red art muslin and over 5000 electric
lights (amber, red and green) were requisitioned,
and I was assured, in a hushed whisper, that 100
roast turkeys and 1000 lettuces were to be had for
the eating.
Mrs. Cohen wore her Court dress, shorn of its
train. It was of white satin and chiffon, with crystal
fringe and hand-worked embroidery. Melbourne’s
Mayoress appeared in a dress of oyster satin and
wonderful net embroideries that turned half the
women in the room green with envy.
Mayor Cohen’s “Moorish palace” was again on
view on Friday night, when a public promenade
concert occurred. A fair number attended, despite
the rain. Amongst the crowd were the corporation
employees and their families, and nurses from the
public hospitals.
Philanthropists and “Sunbeams” are having a wild
and woolly week feting Sir John Kirk, who’s devot-
ing his time to urging that more care be taken of
the all-important rising generation, and to visiting
those institutions which keep a paternal eye on the
young. The ungrateful youngster possibly prefers a
visit from some vice-regal, because that generally
means a half-holiday.
The Rev. E. 11. Miller, who died recently, is re-
ported to have paid £29 ss. into the Clergy Annuity
Fund, and to have received from it £3576. And
yet the clergy would deny us a sporting chance in
Tatt.’s!
Wattle Day was all wrong from a weather point of
view, but all right as regards the celebration. The
city looked as if it were waving a giant Chinese
flag. Tram cars sported a spray of the bloom at
each comer, and some of the engines rose to heights
of decoration which did their drivers proud. Bunches
of the emblem were sent to hospitals, etc., and lead-
ing officials were presented with a spray, so they’d
no excuse for not being patriotic! In the afternoon
the committee and its friends met at the May Club
rooms, at the invitation of Vice-President Sowden,
and made speeches and drank tea. Miss Boothby
rose to the occasion with wattle decorations and a
“yellow” table. The guests included our own Mayor
and Mayoress, as well as the visiting Daveys, who
seemed impressed with the way the notion has caught
on here.
Clytie Hine, the pretty soprano song-bird who is
studying at London Royal College of Music, has
caught another scholarship, the third in less than
three years. Present triumph is particularly desir-
able, as it not only pays the damsel’s fees for a year,
but provides for her maintenance in the smoky
village. Clytie is to take the leading soprano r6le
in thfe college opera again this year.
A bush nursing order, to be called the
King’s Nurses, is projected in the Apple
Isle. Mrs. Curly Keating is secretary of the
scheme. At Launceston, a magnificent ball
was recently rolled into shape to help the
bank account, and all the Very Best Fly-
speckers attended. Governor Barron and
his lady danced a set of lancers, and after-
wards fed at a table decorated with local
Beauty’s wreathed smiles. The Boy Scouts
were retained to cheer the vice-regal en-
trances and exits, and generally play the
part enacted by the stage villagers. The
net result of these heroic exertions was a
solid sum, which will not be eaten into by
expenses, the City Council and others hav-
ing loaned and donated pretty well every-
thing needful.
The County Court was filled the other
m °i' ni Dg with agitated members of the Aus-
tral Salon. Mrs. Quinnell, the popular
secretary of the club, was the plaintiff, and
appeared pale but composed under a hat of
pink heather. A mass of correspondence
which made the Court rustle excitedly, was
read aloud by a hard-voiced lawyer; after
which harrowing performance, his Honor
postponed the hearing for a month. If, in
the interim, the other lady in the case wilt
agree to take back all she w T rote, all will be
well. Most of the women interested had
dragged unwilling husbands with them, and
a good many clutched smelling salts as if
they were lifebuoys. Also, every female pre-
sent breathed a silent vow never to touch
pen and ink again except in the w 7 ay of kind-
ness. To hear one’s confidential communica-
tions read in a cold, level voice in open
court, with a dog howling round the corner
and a remote cornet puffing “What’s the
Matter with Father?” is enough to frighten
anyone off the volcanic inkbottle.
Sincerely yours,

WATSON'S
NO. to WHISKY
About Rheumatism
Treating the Complaint in a
Common-sense Way*
There’s nothing like a little common-sense
in treating Rheumatism. This is the way
to look at it. There is something that
causes rheumatism. The pains, aches and
swollen joints are only symptoms of the
cause. The complaint cannot be cured
until the cause is driven out of the system.
The pains and aches and swellings disappear
when there is nothing to cause them. Many
medical authorities agree that Rheumatism
is in the blood in the form of acid, and this
is why Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills have been
so successful in curing the complaint. They
are a blood medicine, actually making new
blood and toning the system to a point that
enables it to throw off the rheumatic poison
through the channels of the body. There’s
another great point in their favor. Nature
always makes a big fight to drive out. any
complaint. The better the health the bigger
effort nature makes. As a general tonic
there is nothing better than Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills to put the body in a sound,
healthy condition. This is how Mrs. Sarah
Nichols, 278 Charles Street, Launceston,
-sured her Rheumatism, and her experience
should be of interest to other sufferers.
“I was only 32 when I was struck down
with Rheumatism,” said Mrs. Nichols. “It
attacked me in all my joints. The pains
were something dreadful, and I suffered the
greatest agony. I could not bear the bed-
clothes to touch me. I got very little sleep,
and lost the use of my left arm from the
elbow to the shoulder. I called in one of
the leading medical men. He gave me stuff
to rub in and also medicine, but neither
did me any good, only easing me for a time.
I suffered the greatest agony for about 12
months. My left arm was paralysed; I
could not move it from my side. Having
read in the daily papers of the wonderful
cures effected by Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills,
I made up my mind to give them a trial,
but had very little hope. I got a box, and,
after taking half, I felt my arm better.
When I had taken a box I could move my
arm from my side. I improved every day.
and when I had taken three boxes I was
completely cured, and was able to work as
well as ever. In fact, it was wonderful
what Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills did for me
in such a short time.” .
When you want Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
don’t be put off with anything else said to
be just as good. If you have trouble in get-
ting them, send direct to the Dr. Williams
Medicine Co. of Australasia Ltd., Sydney,
who will send them by return, post free,
2s. 9d. for one box, six boxes 15s. 3d.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The Argus becomes more snobbish as it
grows older. It has just discovered that
“the itruth is that the Labor party is a new
party, and, therefore, without those tradi-
tions that give stability and dignity to
parties with a history behind them.” The
Argus, of course, uses as a standard that
extremely stable party which calls itself
Conservative to-day, and Liberal to-morrow,
and [Reform the day after, with a few odd
things in between. And it was, no doubt,
thinking also of the tremendous dignity of
Tommy Bent (“guts to a bear”) of Victoria,
and of Wood and Fitzparrot of N. S. Wales.
“Cross” : What about protecting our ship-
ping against the foreigner, as he protects his
against us? A vessel may go to one German
port only and discharge there, but may not
call from port to port for the purpose of
picking up or setting down freight and pas-
sengers. The Germans reserve their coastal
and oversea port-to-port delivery trade for
themselves ; we in our dunderheadedness al-
low foreign vessels to pick up and set down
at all our ports. And then we wonder at
the rapid growth of the armed German
marine, compared with the slow progress of
our Own —which, by the way, isn’t armed !
The failure of any attempt at law to upset
the new Speaker’s election in N. S. Wales
appears inevitable. C. G. Wade is the man
who suggested the appeal.
From the Age of a recent date: —
In the recent engineering trade trouble there had
been a Wages Board sitting leisurely once a week, in-
vestigating matters which were vital to the parties
concerned. The employees had lost patience, because
every week’s delay meant to them a loss of wages,
which the event showed was equitably due to them.
And just as the “law’s delay” often means the rum
of tlie suitor, so these exasperating delays in a
Wages Board tribunal may be the real cause of
trouble.
And the Age which now groans about the
Board’s “exasperating delays’’ is the journal
which, during the Referenda campaign last
March, fluted about the “simple, rapid and
effective mechanism” of those same organis-
ations, and called upon the State to save
itself from the slow horrors of Federal Arbi-
tration. No wonder Andrew Fisher thinks
that if Australia were given the chance to
speak again it would thunder out a “xes-
Yes!”
The Labor party in Maoriland is begin-
ning to view with belated suspicion the old,
threadbare assurance that the objects of the
“Lib-Lab” party are similar in character to
those of the Australian La.bor party, a.nd, in
order to obtain some definite information on
the subject, McLaren, M.H.IL, recently
tabled the following few questions for the
consideration of the Loanly Government:
Would the said Government take steps—
(l) To establish a State note issue and a State
bank ; , ,
(2) A State factory for the production of harness
and saddlery required in connection with the mili-
tary defence scheme ;
(3) A State clothing factory to provide uniforms,

public services ; .

THE PROTESTING TIGER.
A tiger for the Sydney Zoological Gardens arrived
in Port Jackson on Sunday from the Malay States
by the steamer Mataram.
THE TIGER (to absent-minded carrier): “ No,
you don't! No, you don’t! I signed on
for the Zoo! No State Parliaments [?]
me!”
It is proposed that the Speaker in the
House of Reps, shall dispense with all his
“symbols of authority.” Yet where is the
democratic reasonableness of abolishing the
Mace symbol from a Commonwealth which
is tinselled o'er with knighthoods and C.M.G.
distinctions? The abolition of one set of
gauds shrieks to Consistency to come and
lend a hand to abolish the other.
The latest scheme of Sydney D. T. is to
divide its pen down the middle; and by thus
providing two versions simultaneously, ac-
curacy is almost within the old party’s
reach. As an instance, the attitude of
Speaker Willis (N.S.W.) is reviewed on
August 30. In one leading article we learn
that:—
Members on the Government side of the House and
their friends in the galleries laughed with glee
when members of the Opposition, zealous to defend
the rights and privileges of Parliament, were arbi-
trarily ordered to their seats or made the victim of
the attentions of the Sergeant-at-Arms.
On the same page the bewildered reader is
informed that far from “defending the rights
and privileges of Parliament,” on the occa-
sion referred to, the Opposition had been
offensive to the Speaker ; but: —
The calm indifference with which Mr. Willis was
able to treat that futile display of ill-temper made
him the meretricious hero of the episode. And so it
will be with all such attempts to badger and bait
the Speaker by members whom he can, and under
the circumstances should, immediately order outside
until they learn to behave themselves in a manner
more worthy of a deliberative Assembly.
Still, the Telegraph would save its readers
time if it printed its bricks in black ink and
its bouquets in red. Then “Paterfamilias.”
desiring bouquets, wouldn’t have to stumble
over a column of bricks.
The best argument for compulsory defence
service that lias been used so far in Maoriland
comes from the Christadelphians, who are
lighting in alliance with the rump of the red-
flaggers on the side of general helplessness.
In an article urging its readers to “defend
their rights” on the question, the party s
official organ shouts :
Let us not be caught disorganised, and so be mal-
treated.
Which shows that even a Christadelphian
has his lucid moments.

The Fisher Government is mustering up
courage to frame new Standing Orders for
the House of Successive Gov-
ernments have been too tired and too con-
servative to sweep out the old anachron-
isms, and the Usher of the Blue Cod, the
Mace, the Major-domo or Sergeant-at-Arms,
and all the other flummery have been al-
lowed to prance round without even a dead
marine being thrown at them. Nqw, it is
proposed to sack a few of them, and Mace,
who isn’t related to the celebrated Jem, has
been given a hint that he is likely to be the
first to go.
DOCTORS BAFFLED. ' ~~
All aged people should read this remarkable letter
from Mrs. Patience Thomas, of, Belmont Road, Gee-
long, Vic. “Last winter I got a frightful cold on
the lungs, and, being 81 years of age, the doctors
refused to prescribe for me, saying that at my age
medicine would be dangerous; but, knowing that
Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy bears the Government
analysis, showing that it contains no poisons or
narcotics, I tried it; and three bottles were the
means of giving me back my natural health. I am
so old now that I cannot use my pen, so am getting
my niece, Mrs. E. Wylie, to sign this.”*
With the recent English Revolution fresh
in the newspapers files, Solemn Lee asked
the N.S.W. Gasembly last week what was
the use of our industrial laws, seeing that
they had failed to prevent the Lithgow
trouble. Yes, certainly ! And what’s the use
of the police, seeing that there was a burglary
last week. And Solemn Lee might also ex-
plain what’s the use of parsons, seeing that
somebody was recently supposed to have
gone to Gehenna.
Willy Watt, Victoria’s Acting-
Premier, probably never realised
what q meal of ashes there is on his
plate until he considered the con-
stitution of the “Liberal” crowd
which turned tail on him over the
Preferential Voting Bill. The
nine Ministerialists who exercised
the usual “Liberal” privilege of
selling their friends, were mostly
the remnants of the Cabinet which
rode to ruin along with T. Bent.
They included Argyle, who was
Bent’s Whip ; Bowser, who was his
amiably incompetent Railways-and-
Education figurehead ; Mackey, the
“educated Minister,” who failed in
department after department;
McLeod, the drifting Minister for
Mines; “Andy” Robertson, the
honorary Minister who represented
the squatters in Bent’s Cabinet;
old-man Langdon, Who was a joke
in the Ministry until Bent un-
kindly shied him out; Gray, a
noted sorehead ; and Alec Peacock-
of-the-Laugh, who fled from the
Bent Ministry amid unforgettable
odors. Added to these was Norman
Bayles, the foolish person who re-
presents Toorak, and had his leg
pulled by the astute old hands. Of
such is the “Liberal” party—a
party of fag-ends that never looks quite so
disreputable as when it is exposing itself in
its own sordid quarrels.
If the Law had any sense it would long
ago have declared, once and for all, that the
man who uses any animal or vehicle that isn’t
his, without the owner’s leave, is liable to
punishment; but, instead, it went into de-
tails, and mentioned the particular things
that were not to be used. Hence, in these
days of the motor-car, the cheerful Victorian
youth who goes joy-riding, can snap his
fingers at the owner of the car. Even now,
when Nawman Bayles proposes to put a
patch on the law, he merely particularises
motor-cars, and in a few years probably
another animal or vehicle will be invented,
and then some other Norman will have to go
forth to conquer the new difficulty. It is
all so futile and untidy.
RULE I.—AN ALLEGORY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Our ridiculous Party System of Government insists
on the constituencies returning a majority for one
side or another, if Parliament is to be workable. —
B. 31/8/’ll.
For years the Pure Merino Club
In peace and order ran.
The Secretary’s name was Brown;
The Steward’s, James McCann.
James died —and in a little while
Sheer Anarchy began.
The trouble that convulsed this club
Of old and fair renown
Arose because the members had,
In searching through the town
For someone to replace McCann,
Engaged a man named Brown.
For by Rule I.—so senseless ’tis,
I mention it with shame —
Unless the Steward’s moniker
(That is to say, his name)
Was different from his colleague’s, all
Their powers were the same.
The Jeremiahs of the club
Had oft, in language hot,
Predicted trouble from Rule 1.,
And d—d it for a Blot:
Their Tory friends denounced such views
As “visionary rot.”
And now the members had a chance
To see Rule I. in use;
And, shortly, all the club was filled
With faces purple, puce
And black with rage; oaths hummed like bees
And blasphemous abuse.
Each time the Steward told a man
Or maid to sweep the hall,
Or dust a chair, or fetch a drink,
Or answer someone’s call,
The Secretary cried, “Desist!
Cease working, one and all.”
The Steward, not to be outdone,
Rejected everything
The Secretary ordered. Steaks
And poultry he would fling
Upon the rubbish-tip, and cheese,
And beer and bream and ling.
The servants, though they rarely worked,
Were in no wise content:
'iV.e Steward drew their wages-cheques—
But ev’ry time they went
To cash the same they found the Sec.
Had “stopped” each document.
The tradesmen fumed to see their goods
Cast scornfully aside ;
They fumed still more when to secure
The cash they vainly tried:
The Secretary wished to pay ;
That boon he was denied.
At last, when Ruin hovered like
A vulture o’er the place,
And ev’ry member saw himself
Involved in black disgrace,
The Pure Merinos took Rule 1.,
And stamped upon its face.
Envoi.
Not Clubs alone, but modern States
Have mad old Rules or Laws.
Whereby Progression’s checked and held
As though with iron claws —
A foolishness designed to give
'•The thinking voter pause.
N.S.tV.
M. SAFRAN.
THE VICTIM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The mud through which Parliament is dragged
when a Labor Ministry is struggling to preserve its
existence to the last possible minute is knee-deep.—
Argus, 23/8/’ll.
The saints of old—who were martyrs, too —-
They stuck to their creeds till all was blue,
And went, whenever their time was due,
Through fire and boiling water;
And the “Liberal” lamb, as white as snow,
Through oceans of slush is booked to go
(A sacrifice of a sort, you know)
To the Progress-yards, for slaughter.
Through horrible Labor slush, alas!
The “Liberal” lamb to his doom must pass,
No more to nibble the bright green grass
In the sunshine, sometimes skipping
With the joy of life that the seasons bring.
Early or late, the poor young thing
On the skinning-gallows must gently swing
With its dear neck drip.. .drip.. .dripping !
llow sad that the lamb for the slaughter must
Go walloping on in the slush, with just
No chance of raising a decent dust
Along with its bleating neighbor!
But the “Liberal” lamb has got to go,
Whether the poor thing likes it or no,
To the dreadful garden where lamb-chops grow—
Black shame to the butcher, Labor!
VICTORIA.
FRANK HENTY.
