SPORTING
NOTIONS
Thus the Mclnstoush, who reached Syd-
ney last Tuesday: “If there is a White Hope he’ll
have to be found in Australia, and I’m giving you
an honest opinion when I tell you Bill Lang is a
good hope. When Burns cabled me that he could
not meet Langford in London, Lang was the only
white man who was game to go into the ring with
the black. He had a good chance, too, right up to
the time he was d’squalified—rightly disqualified,
mind you—but the fight between the two in the
Sydney Stadium won’t, end in a disqualification, or
I’m much mistaken. Johnson will fight Langford,
McVea and Lang. If he beats the three he will retire,
after a tour through Japan, China, India, and a few
other round-about places. He wants to fight McVea
first. That McVea is good goods all right; and if
Lang does not get the belt he (McVea) will be the
next champion. He has fought Johnson three times —
once going up to the 20th round with him —and
he fought a draw with Langford in Paris.”
The Melnsplosh doesn’t take the coming Johnson-
Bombardier Wells fight seriously. The syndicate
which is handling the match approached Mac. before
he left London, with the view of matching Wells
with one of the big niggers under his contract, and
Mac. put it to them that he was in a hurry, but that
he would bet a good stout wad that McVea would
stop Wells in two rounds. So, unless the picture
rights get in the way, Massa Johnson shouldn’t be
working overtime the day he meets the ex-soldier.
* * * *
“0.K.” : The overwhelming Mclntosh, who
talks casually of a forthcoming outlay of £50,000 on
the importation of fighters, black and otherwise, seems
to give the game away when he asserts that “Bill Lang
is certainly the best white man living.” If Aus-
tralia is still burning with desire to see the stousli
championship wrested from a colored pusson, why
engage two or three more colored pussons, beside
Johnson, to give performances at enormous salaries?
Is Australia to be the Tom Tiddler’s Ground of all
de greatest niggahs and second-best white men in
the boxing world ? Is this gullible, sport-loving
community going to pay some tens of thousands of
pounds for the joy of beholding white men bite the
sawdust at the feet of their dusky conquerors, or the
ecstasy of witnessing the passing of the champion-
ship from one niggah to another? Public worship
of such hollow gods as Bill Squires (“the Hurricane
Fighter”), and Tahmmy Burns (“the Irresistible
Kidney Puncher”) was saddening, but the assurance
of the astute Mclntosh that Johnspn is to receive
£lB,OOO for revisiting Australia, whilst his colored
friend McVea will be “made quite a hero of,” on
account of his being a nice fellow- - this is worse
than saddening. It’s maddening.
Meanwhile, there is the bare chance that Johnson
will lose his championship to young man Bombardier
Wells, who presumably has at least a good sound
heavy-weight punch to recommend him,. Wells,
thus far, has been so very young' And* 'fresh at the
game that his public form seems less worth consid-
ering than the fact of his having j)eqn! guaranteed
25 per cent, of an £BOOO purse. To a.big 24-year-
old athlete, with some skill in boxing, a lot of rapid
improvement has been possible, what time a lot of
rapid disimprovement was possible to J. Johnson.
The Wells experiment has, the charm of audacity. If
tiie young man goes into the ring with strong nerves
strung up to concert pitch, he, will have one great
qualification which Tahmmy Burns and Jim Jeffries
Incited entirely. They went scared to the slaughter.
Johnson, by this time, must have almost forgotten
how to fight a man who isn’t frightened of him ; and
if, perchance, Wells be such a man, he may even
succeed in removing the golden smile from Jack’s
restless face.
*•* * •
The rough-up at Sydney Sports Ground
last week, between Bill Turner, the heavy-weight
champion of Tassy, and Jack Howard, of N. S.
Wales, ended after 2J rounds. Turner was the
woodened, and his chances of getting a battle with
Lang are up in the clouds. It was hard slugging
from the gong, but the man from Slowbart was not
in the same class as Howard, who is improving
every fight. Next to Lang, Howard is the best of
the Australian talent in the heavy division—which
is, of course, nothing to write a poem about.
* » * *
The Lang-Lester assault and battery en-
engagement, which was billed for Saturday last at
the Stadium, did not reach finality. In fact, it
didn’t start, owing to Billang handing in a certificate
that he had influenza or something. And now not
even the Inclement Wragge could prognosticate when
the fight will take place. The Stadium people have
been unlucky with their fights so far this season.
The first scrap was held up by bad weather for two
or three weeks; the Hill-Elliott event was post-
poned so often, for one cause and another, that it
appears to have been forgotten altogether, or thrown
overboard as a Jonah; and now the affair between
Griffin and Picato (the Yankee I-talian) has been
declared off.
* * * *
Kid McCoy, the clever young lightweight
of Melbourne, will have his first fight in Sydney
against Dick Cullen, an American boy, on October 7.
* * * *
Scanlon versus Carrie was a recent fight
of some consequence at Melbourne Athletic Club’s
chapel. Carrie is the ex-amateur champion welter-
weight of this State, who, since taking to profession-
alism, has not been beaten. Fie was not much put
to it in his scrap with Scanlon, although the latter
had half a stone to the good. In round 1 a violent
right swing connected with the larger man’s left
optic, and put a culvert over it. For the rest of the
mill Scanlon had to get along with one sidelight.
In the third Carrie’s in-fighting flummoxed Scanlon,
who took the worst half of a burst of punishment,
and was put down with a nice hook. On resuming
the erect attitude, he was not allowed time to for-
mulate a policy. When the process of rising and
falling grew wearisome to Scanlon’s corner, it
angrily hurled the serviette into the ring, and Carrie
had nicked another win.
» * * *
“Hacky” is now definitely “ex,” Frank
Gotch having beaten him in the “rastle” which took
place in America on Monday. Gotch won two
straight-out falls, the first in 7min., and the other in
14 min.
Dhobi’s win at Caulfield last Saturday,
with Bst. 131 b. up, was less remarkable in ttie
running than in the betting. He started at 20 to 1,
after trainer Jimmy Lynch and his friends had put
a few pounds on at 40’s and 50’s. They thought the
horse was almost certain to fail “for lack of condi-
tion,” in spite of his being so full of health. The
result suggests that his people have always made the
mistake of getting Dhobi too “fit” for his big en-
gagements. If Lynch can keep the nuggety animal in
steady work without taking any more flesh off him,
he should have a great chance at 7st. 51b. for the
Caulfield Cup, especially if the going happens to be
soft. Dhobi is always thought of as a non-stayer, but
two years ago he won the Caulfield Cup over 11 fur-
longs.
* * * *
Comedy King got beaten by 3-year-old
Flaith last Saturday, even as he was beaten by Eye
Glass at the same meeting last year. The black
crack can do with a little more work, no doubt, but
it is a question whether he also has not been some-
what overdone —like Dhobi —for some of his engage-
ments. When he won so handsomely at Randwick
last Spring, the condition of Comedy King was said
to be “capable of improvement,” yet he managed to
stay to the end of the Melbourne Cup without having
been improved at all.
* * » *
Broadsword's latest performance threw
no light on his Caulfield Cup prospects. In a 9-fur-
long race he led Flaith and Comedy King for a
while, but dropped away towards the finish. Broad-
sword was palpably above himself, but he might be
as fit as he was when he won the Doncaster, and still
want the extra stamina required for a 12-furlong
straggle.
* * * *
The totalisator once again rose to the
occasion at Adelaide, on Saturday, when Ivadlunga’s
win of Tattersall’s Handicap yielded a dividend of
£6l 7s. (with only 12 starters in the race) ; Peal’s
success in the Spring Handicap returned £l9 75.;
while Netherton’s second in the Juvenile Handicap
presented punters with £lO 6s. The continued
liberality of the machine is getting irksome; still, it
appears necessary to keep notifying a “welshed”
public that Moses is an unnecessary evil.
* * * *
After worrying over the trouble for three
weeks, the A.J.C. stipendiary stewards last week
disqualified trainer L. C. Want for five years for
tampering with the girth of racehorse Flavigny at
Rosehill on August 12. Prior to the start of the
August Handicap on that date, jockey H. Gray dis-
mounted and changed the girth, and the stewards
promptly seized it. According to the report, an ex-
amination of the girth presented “extraordinary
features,” and biffing was considered necessary.
Without being inhuman, the stewards might have
been much more severe in dealing with an offence
that might have ended in murder or mutilation.
Amid the cronkness the betting public suffered, since
Flavigny at one time was favorite; )>ut towards
starting time the long nose of Moses smelt a'rat and
the odds lengthened. While Want got the full brunt
of the biff, the stewards exonerated jockey and owner
from having a hand in the shocking business.
* * * *
“Bill” Marion Edwards, the she-male
whom the public only knows in man’s rig, is again
dabbling in “ponies” in Melbourne. The other day
she was called up before Jack Wren at the Fitzroy
meeting, to explain why her “boy” hadn’t been paid
the half-sov. for a losing mount. Billarion’s explana-
tion was that she had given it to a cobber, who had
been swallowed up in the crowd; but as cobber
and coin continued to be swallowed, the she-male
was called in again and fined £2. The lordly way
in which she spurned the Court when it announced
its decision suggests that Billarion is wasting her
time chasing shekels at pony meetings. If she could
turn down the villain’s shameful proposals like that,
there’s a fatter living for her in bellowdrama.
* * * *
The N.S.W. Rugby League first-grade
tussle between East Sydney and South Sydney, on
Saturday, was another advt. for Messenger. Before
a crowd of 12,000, Messenger recorded 20 points out
of his side’s 23, and at one time the 15 points
belonging to the club were all his. The first half
ended with East leading 6 to nil; then the tally was
lifted till play ended, amid fisticuffs, with East 23
ponts to South 10. Although referee McMahon didn’t
send any players off, too much yabber went on dur-
ing the game, and at times it got rough enough to
suggest that,the,referee.was losing the reins, especi-
ally towards the end, when Souths played the man
instead of the ball, and Messenger and others were
knocked about. East Sydney will tackle Glebe in
the final on Saturday next, and the League will act
wisely to notify the touch-line judges to keep an
eye on the things that drag the game into the
gutter.
* * * *
Eastern Suburbs v. Redfern provided a
clinking game in the qualifying round of the
League second-grade semi-final on Saturday, when
Easts just squeezed out of trouble by 3 points to 2.
It was East’s day out at Rugby League football;
and it is a coincidence that in the final matches
next Saturday, clubs from Glebe and Eastern Sub-
urbs will meet in both first and second grades.
* * * *
Newington College gathered in this Syd-
ney season’s Great Public School football premier-
ship, with nary a defeat in nine matches —seven being
won and two drawn. As it is 11 years since the
College previously handled the title, the success
wasn’t out of turn. Big school football this season
has been the best shown for many years; principally
the result of Rugby Union coaches lending a hand.
* * * *
The Sydney Rugby Union premiership final,
on Saturday, proved a spirited tussle, during which
Newtown trampled University down by 27 points to
10. The teams had previously played three games
during the season, and as ’Varsity had won two with
good margins, it was expected that the Blues would
go down again. A vigorous first half saw Newtown
leading by 8 points to 3, which wasn’t much to
crow about; but in the second section, ’Varsity
crumpled badly. Newtown scored seven tries (three
converted) against one try, a field and a penalty
goal by University—which about represents the differ-
ence in the clubs on the day. Newtown therefore
retain the premiership after a hard season.
* * * *
In the Sydney premiership semi-final (Aus-
tralian rules) on Saturday, East Sydney surprised
Sydney by handing it a defeat —63 points to 51.
The game was an excellent exhibition, and the see-
sawing of the scores provided barrackers with plenty
of reason to stretch their lungs. Y.M.C.A. and East
Sydney will supply the menu for the coming Satur-
day. and if the Good Young Men come out on top,
and also account for Sydney in the challenge round,
the Rawson Cup will get a final resting-place in
their pantry, and a new utensil will be necessary for
future years.
* * * *
The Gardiner Cup is the only Sydney Soccer
trophy whose ownership is disputed. Balmain has
the other two, and has a team in the final for the
Gardiner. In fact, its two teams have to play off to
see which shall meet Powerful, last year’s winners.
Yarraside football enthusiasts crowded
into the little East Melbourne ground on Saturday,
to witness the collision of the two leading League
teams—Essendon and South. Nothing depended on
the result; not even the positions of the “final four”
could be altered. Yet everyone anticipated a strenu-
ous, exciting struggle, and got it. The “marking”
of the two sides was about equal; but the ’Dons were
swifter, cleaner and more systematic, and by lemon-
time they had 62 points to South’s 38. In" the last
term, however, Skinner’s Hirelings, by sheer bogging -
in, added 20 more points, while ’Don’s put on only
one point. So the end was: South 58 to Don 63.
On Saturday’s showing—though, of course, nothing
in football is certain —’Wood should beat South, and
Carlton should do the same for the ’Dons. The other
games were mere practice matches, ’Wood downing
Saints by 101 to 44 ; ’Roy outing Richmond by 57
to 34 ; Carlton upending Melbourne by 62 to 54, and
Geelong dittoing the Students by 107 to 46.
These same Students, by the way, are occasioning
the League much thought. They battled heftily
during their first three seasons; but in this, the
fourth, they have taken little interest in the busi-
ness, and have won but a single lonely game—against
St. lvilda. They have no ground of their own ; and,
on top of this, they have but a meagre following
of “supporters.” Unless the brilliance of their play
can draw the great unattached public—which belongs
to no club, but pays to see the best ’ball —they are
no good to the League. The “gates” in some of their
matches this season haven’t paid the wages of the
attendants (against St. lvilda, f’rinstance, they were
£lO, and against Melbourne £l2 155.) ; but (and
this is where the shoe pinches the League) they
divide the semi-final and final “gates”—worth some
£5OOO or £6OO0 —with the other nine clubs.
Years agone, when they belonged to the Metropolitan
Ass., they overshadowed all competitors; but their
junior team, which now battles in that organisation,
is almost at the bottom of the list, and lately
they couldn’t raise a side to play Caulfield. So the
League will probably pass them a strong hint to do
something vigorous or retire.
* * * *
The only Ass. disturbance was the semi-final
between North and Brunswick on the North Melbourne
sand pit. (The Ass., by the way, has got in early
by applying to the M.C.C. for the big Metropolitan
ground for its finals in 1912, which application the
incoming M.C.C. committee will no doubt presently
find some means of shelving.) This game was spoilt
as a spectacle of over-anxiety on both sides, so that
at lemon-time the scores were: ’Wick 14 points,
North 16. North continued flabbergasted to the end,
but in the last quarter ’Wick recovered its nerve,
piled up another 26 points, and, with a total of 40
to North’s 25, ran into the final. These two clubs
battled out the premiership last year, when North
won, with the aid of five purchased Carlton stars.
Though most of these are still in the team, North’s
dash is done, and it is financially on the rocks, so,
apparently, the purchase of stars is a bad business.
* * * *
During the week the law upheld the appeal
of Carlton ’bailer Gotz, who was fined a “tenner” away
back in May for jobbing Trood, of ’Varsity, the evi-
dence being contradictory. The Law likewise acquitted
Martin, of Essendon, of all evil intent in punching
Holden, of ’Roy, in July, so he saves a “fiver” by
the appeal. Meanwhile the League, which has up
to now postponed dealing with Gotz’s case, has fired
Martin out for the rest of the season, and last week
suspended Holden till October, 1912, for jobbing an
Essendon man the Satuiday before. The tears and
triumphs of these ’bailers are of no great moment
to ordinary citizens, but some persons set great
store by them, like the St. Kilda man, who, when
asked his excuse for uproarious and insulting be-
havior on a recent Sunday evening, told the P.M.
he “had belonged to a football club for three years,
and that they were premiers.” Even then he was
fined 40s.
After duly anointing all creditors, the Aus-
tralian Rulers’ recent football kick-up at Adelaide
(S.A.) yielded a profit of £7O to each of the
States that lent a toe. The S. Aus. League did the
thing well, and the visiting brethren are saying their
thanks in fervent letters.
* * * *
Something like a record football match
was played at Knox Park, on the Tweed (N.S.W.),
the other day. In the first 40 minutes one crowd
(Blues) scored 77 points to the other’s nil; and as
the others (Wanderers) were already utterly ex-
hausted, the umpire stopped the play. A few weeks
previously the t’irates had scored 83 to the same
crowd’s nil. The Wanderers came from a Murwill-
umbah cow-centre; and the fact that the team was
exhausted at half-time seems to suggest that cow-
slavery is getting in its deadly work on the young
Australian.
* * * *
Stevenson turned the tables on Lindrum
in his second match, and, when the game was
ended, the scores stood —Stevenson, 18,000 ; Lin-
drum (ree. 4000), 13,702. The English champion
was at his best throughout, but the youngster was
not in his usual form at any part of the game.
Stevenson leaves for England on the 16th inst. ;
Lindrum, who has now taken on A. S. Williams
in Melbourne, follows a week later.
Hardwick, the N.S.W. swimmer, has been
having a mixed time in Europe. At Gladback he
met Oscar Schiele in a 400 metres race, and was
beaten; but at Leipzig he defeated the same Schiele
in a 300 metres race, accomplishing the distance
(with a standing start in the water) in 4min. 9sec.,
which beat Beaurepaire’s record by 2sec. and the
German record by 3sec. By all accounts, however,
he is finding things pretty dull.*
Warner’s English XI will open their
shoulders at Adelaide Oval on November 10, with
the customary match against S. Aus. The Board of
Control hasn’t thus far fixed up the balance of the
job of sandwiching the “picnics” between the
“tests” and State matches; but it is certain that
the coming band will not have to tackle the heavy
programme which knocked the stuffing out of pre-
vious teams. A request from Maoriland for an ex-
tension of the tour has been refused by the Board of
Control, and the English team will, therefore, roam
through Australia only.
South Aus. loses W. .J. Whitty during the
coming season, and N.S.W. will again have the cham-
pion Australian trundler. Whitty’s inclusion is
likely to make N.S.W. exceptionally hard to beat in
this season’s cricket.
Last cricket year there was trouble over
the selection of Ma’s inter-State team, and the local
Cricket Ass. has already grappled with the problem
for the coming season. The proposed solution will
also include the settlement of the captaincy diffi-
culty. Although the trouble was nominally on
matters of principle, it really centred round Trumper,
who showed a disposition to assume the rBle of
cricket I AM vacated by Noble. Trumper reckoned
that he was Ma’s captain by right of succession or
Divine authority, or something, and should be con-
sulted by the Ass. as to the selection and manage-
ment of the team. The Ass. had a bone to pick with
Victor, and would not bow the knee to him. The
difficulty is to be solved by nominating two selectors,
who are to choose a captain, and the three will then
pick the team. The two selectors will, of course,
choose Trumper ; but then the Ass. won’t have chosen
him. It recalls the time when Britain was a Com-
monwealth. Parliament wouldn’t give Cromwell’s
son right of succession, but it allowed Pa Cromwell
to choose his successor.
A petrol-consumption test, held in Sydney
recently, shows that a big advance has been made
lately in the manufacture of this liquid combustible
and the appliances for utilising it. The winner
drove a four-cylinder 16-h.p. car 16 miles 960vds.
on half a gallon of the goods, equal to over 33 miles
per gallon. The weight of the car with passengers
was 29cwt. The furthest distance recorded was by
a 7-8-h.p. twin-cylinder car (weight 15icwt.),
which covered 18 miles 1573yds. on half a gallon,
equal to nearly 3*B miles per gallon.
An Italian co. is placing a new motor on
the market, with a variable stroke, actuated from
the steering wheel, the same as the throttle lever,
and by which the stroke of the pistons can be
shortened or lengthened, thus affecting the volume
of explosive mixture and the speed of the engine.
The chief advantage claimed is flexibility; for, as
with a steam engine, the power can be increased at
will, thus doing away with all gear changing, the only
gear needed being a reverse. The change speed-bos
is an expensive part of a car, and its elimination
would be a great advance towards the cheap and
perfect machine.
Entries for the Warrnambool-Melbourne
cycle road race close with the Dunlop Co. on Oc-
tober 14. Last year there were 525 entrants and 355
starters, and this year there promises to be still
more.
Dunlop individual road championship—
the tallest honor of the N.S.W. Cyclists’ Union—
was wheeled off on the Ilomebush (Sydney) roadway
on Saturday, when 17 people raised clouds of dust
and pushed the rest of the traffic on to the footpath.
A strong westerly punctured most of the riders, and
only six saw the journey through. The rush to the
tape provided an excellent finish., and S. Edworthv,
in a slashing sprint, won by half a length from D.
Kirk (who also ran second last year), with C. M.
Cutcliffe (last year’s champion) a few inches away,
third. Time, lhr. 47min. 31sec., which, although
smin. slower than the record, was a good effort
under adverse conditions.
The old Victorian League of Wheelmen
has swallowed the unregistered Victorian Cyclists’
Union, which has been a spike on the track for 15
months, and the title will be changed to the Vic-
torian Cyclists’ Club. Trouble may, however, arise
when the other States are asked to confirm the
brotherly attitude of the cyclists down south with
regard to the registration of disqualified riders.
On Brighton Beach, N’Yark, on Satur-
day, Burman, a Yankee driver, smashed the two-
miles world’s motor record. His time was lmin.
37.875ec5., which is about 74 miles per hour.
* * * *
Definite word comes from Anthony F.
Wilding that Australasia will have to battle without
the smashing Maorilander in the coming Davis Cup
tennis struggle. Brookes will, therefore, have to find
another mate.
Irrespective of side-wagers, bets and other
diseases, the contestants in the recent 440yds. cham-
pionship at Sydney were compensated with a share
in the gate by the N.S.W. Rugby League. Yankee
C. E. Holway thus received £74 ss. lid., while
Maorilander W. F. Trembath gathered £49 10s. Bd.
The effort by the ladies on the same day was re-
warded with open orders for £7 and £3. There
seems to be still a little boodle in footracing when
conducted with safe management.
Died at Melbourne, last week, Walter
Bullock, an early bird who supplied the sauce for
the horsey-man’s breakfast, by contributing the
“work” for many Victorian papers. Bullock had
only a short illness ; lockjaw followed the mutilation
of a foot.
N.S.W. amateur champion golfer, O. H.
O’Brien, migrated to North Queensland last week,
and gathered in the championship of that section.
Tippet took 12 strokes more over the trouble, with
Bettington (N.S.W.) third, and R. J. Pope (who
gathered in the title last year) fourth. It was a
long trio to pick up a small bauble ; but possibly it
had a lump of glory sticking to it somewhere or
other. And, anyway, they are good fellows to meet,
those Northerners. t
The remaining Sydney baseball match to
be played is Leichhardt v. Paddington, and, provided
Leichhardt gathers it in, the premiership falls on
that doorstep ; if not, a final match with Lane Cove
will be necessary.
Sydney lacrosse premiership finals on Sat-
urday were productive of good matches. Petersham
defeated Stanmore in a dust-up, which called for
many “cautions” from referee Robards; and Sydney
just accounted for Mosman by 10 goals to 8. The
recent invasion of inter-Staters seems to have put
vim into the game, and the final match between
Sydney and Petersham should produce a fine tussle.
Botany harriers won the N.S.W. 10-mile
amateur teams championship at Victoria Park on
Saturday. The struggle for the individual title
wi jn a surprise win for R. J. Fitzsimmons
(South Sydney),

“Mashtun” : Quietly entering the Common-
wealth, in the guise of immigrants, are numbers of
peds. ..of proven pace, who will come to light in rich
Sheffields and sprint events in due course. Watch
the entry list for the next Stawell Easter Gift, and
you’ll probably find a good sprinkle of “imported
nominations. A Scotch batch which struck West-
ralia recently, included one Dave Lawrance, the
runner-up in the rich Powderhall (annual) Sheffield
at Edinburgh three seasons ago. And there are
others.
SICK HEADACHE.
WOMEN SUBJECT TO FREQUENT
THROBBING, BURSTING HEADS.
There’s many a cause. Men are not often
troubled by headaches; when they are, it is
generally due to Biliousness or Indigestion.
But women have headaches which seem
peculiar to their sex, frequent throbbing
headaches. Does it not seem that such head-
aches peculiar to women must be at once
related to womanly disease'? Women who
suffer with diseases peculiar to the sex do
not realise the drain of vital strength and
nerve force they undergo as a consequence
of disease. It is this that causes the head-
aches of sick women. In other cases these
pains and disagreeable feelings are generally
symptoms of some other complaint, such as
Indigestion, Biliousness, and Constipation,
all of which are caused by corrupt, noxious
matter clogging the circulations; hence a
stream or rush of blood to the head, and by
the excitement a great pressure on the
brain. “I was,” writes Mrs. Sarah Ourzon,
of 26 Broughton Street, North Sydney, at
one time subject _to severe Headaches and
Neuralgic pains in the head, which would
cause much distraction, nearly driving me
mad, the shooting and throbbing across the
temples being very distressing, and I seemed
to be completely run down and nervously
prostrated. I could scarcely do any work,
and tried many remedies ; but could not hnd
any to give me the relief that I have re-
ceived on taking your valuable remedy, Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills. I rarely ever
suffer now, but if indisposed at any time, 1
take a few of these pills, and they not only
ward off the attack, but make me feel quite
well. I think they are splendid, and am
pleased to be able to give you a small testi-
mony as to their healing qualities, whicii
you may use if you so desire.”

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, whether sent by post or handed
inj and no MS. will be returned unless stamps
(of any State) accompany.

to be demolished and built again.... Zoe: You make
heavy weather of it....D.D.: There is a breath of
country air in the verse, but the tide gauge says it
is not up to print level. .. .Jack H.: Smoothly done,
but there is nothing new in it. First, everything
goes wrong; then everything goes right....J. Rudd:
Regret unsuitable Cher: Shock not powerful
enough.... Bellerive: We will revisit the old place:
LINES ON AN OLD HOMESTEAD.
While strolling down a quiet street
An old Homestead met my gaze,
Though years had passed I recognised
The Home of my childhood’s Days ;
Where flowers once in beauty bloomed
Wild weeds did meet my gaze;
Dilapidated by neglect and time
Was the Home of my childhood’s Days;
Yet it mirrored the familiar picture
Of happy bygone years,
Stirred the ashes of the past
And dimmed my eye with Tears ;
Wintry nights when the winds did waileth
Like the shrill voice of the foam,
I’d nestle before the ruddy fire
Within the dear old Home.

THE DISMISSED BARMAID.
The barmaid took a glass and smiled
And then put on her cuffs,
And looking round the bar she said:
“I’m leaving all you toughs.
I’m going now ; I won’t come back;
I wouldn’t if I could.”
But she’d got the sack
And couldn’t if she would.

Room for a new poet and prophet:—
Church Militant and Triumphant in grand Moran
blended
With the fight that never will be ended.
As a little lad, Uncle Cullen took him on his knee
And asked the fond mother, “What is he to be?”
Says she, “Like you, he’ll climb to the Red Hat,
And prove himself the foremost Pat.”
For this he had to leave beloved home
And go at twelve years old to Rome.
A quarter of a century he remained
And much Ecclesiology he gained.
Then Kilkenny claimed him as a prelate
Further on, superfluous to relate;
And if you a prophet are
Say, the Cardinal’s hat will go to Carr.
....S.P.L. (and 27 or so others): All right; the
Australian flag is put in its proper place at some
schools, in spite of what The Bulletin said the
other day. The question, however, is, What is its
right place? “Rathmines” (and 14 others) yell
that it isn’t in its right place on Empire Day, what-
ever it may be on the common or garden variety of
occasions. So this paper cuts off the hopeless contro-
versy. .. .E. J. Fitz: Sydney pays 3s. 9d. per thou-
sand, feet (private monopoly price) ; Bathurst 2s. 6d.
per thousand feet (municipal price) ... .John V. F.:
Th» Colonel is not worth your heavy artillery.
Politically he is a mere Boy Scout...H. D. McPhee:
Eighteen months or so ago, when you wrote com-
menting on a Bulletin article, we asked whether
the joke was on you for being too stupid to under-
stand, or on us for being too obscure to be under-
stood. Considering that your letter in reply has
just turned up, after being 16 months on the road,
the joke is now on both of us... .W.R.W.: (1) The
fact that Australia, under the voluntary army system,
sent some foolish volunteers to South Africa and the
Soudan is the silliest argument against universal
military training that has turned up this week. ;2)
Supposing “the future army is ordered to put down
those who are striking against an enemy far more
dangerous than the Chow, the Jap or the nigger”—
well, the army is much less likely to shoot in a bad
cause if it is a citizen force than if it is a hired or
mercenary force like Britain’s, and like the one
which Australia has had up to date. (3) How do
you know that the enemy you speak of is “far more
dangerous?” Did you ever see a country that had
been trodden on by the Asiatic ? If you had you
might not talk through your hat so gaily. (4) Why-
all this irrelevance about “conscription”? Nobody
proposes conscription. (5) Your argument, put
short, is that the people can’t trust themselves with
arms for their own defence, lest they should order
the Parliament (which is themselves) to command
them to shoot each other or to fire on inoffensive
strangers. And you back up your theory with tales
of despotically ruled countries where the people have
no voice in the matter. What right have you to
assume that the Australians are the utter fools or
complete blackguards that you imply? (6) The
White Australia policy is a direct challenge to the
adjacent Asiatic States, and there is every probabil-
ity that they will take up the challenge as soon as
they see an opportunity. There are just two classes
in Australia now —the pro-niggers and the advocates
of compulsory military service.
TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC)
CONTRIBUTORS.
B. F. Adelaide: Same idea has been used too
often ; and the drawing is scratchy and hopeless.
Labor member Senior (S.A.) has thumped
a pulpit cushion for over 30 years as a
Methody lay brother. Before that he was a
full-fledged parson. In the Assembly he can
make great play with a book. He balances
it deftly in one hand, while he whacks it with
the other to emphasise his quotations. He
pierces a poor Opposition victim with his
eye as he metaphorically hurls a chunk from
a forgotten Hansakd at his head. He is
“rocky” in the matter of aspirates, and the
other day, with great deliberation, demanded
to know, “’ow the Hopposition can marvel
when the Labor party ’urls liobjurgations at
hour hawful Hupper ’Ouse, which ’as hun-
done so much huseful legislation.” The
sentiment was all right, anyhow.

The Inky Way.
“The Oldest Inhabitant” : The first action for libel
against a Melbourne newspaper was tried in 1843.
The libel arose out of the heat of a municipal elec-
tion. It was a violently scurrilous article in the
Herald, denouncing the candidature of Anthony
Hordern for a seat in the Town Council on personal
grounds. The newspaper soon found out that it had
made a great mistake and had committed an act of
the grossest injustice. A most unqualified apology was
tendered, and all the available copies of the paper
containing the libel were destroyed. A warrant had,
however, been taken out against Mr. Clarke, the
registered printer and publisher of the Herald. He
was committed by the Police Court for trial, but
upon Mr. George Cavanagh, the proprietor, offering
to change places, Clarke was released and Cavanagh
bound over to answer the charge at the criminal
sessions. Mr. Stawell (afterwards Chief Justice)
appeared for the prosecution, and Mr. E. E. Williams
(afterwards a Judge of the Supreme Court, and
father of retired Justice Hartley Williams) was for
Cavanagh, who pleaded guilty, and was admitted to
bail, self in £200 and two sureties in £100 each,
to come up for judgment when called upon. Cav-
anagh had been misled by “swarms of rumors”
during the heat of the elections. In December he
appeared to receive sentence. Stawell prayed judg-
ment. Williams read affidavits, in which respectable
witnesses averred that they had heard and believed
the reports. Thomas Jennings expressed the belief
that the story originated with a meddlesome, mis-
chief-making attorney named Carrington. Jeffcott,
the Judge, took time to consider, and then sentenced
Cavanagh to pay a £60 fine and do three months’
imprisonment. Hordern at once asked that the im-
prisonment part should be cancelled, and this being
agreed to, Cavanagh walked out free.

EPITAPH.
(For The Bulletin.]
Bombala, Wednesday.—-The Bombala Herald,
about the oldest newsnaper in Southern Monaro, was
published for the last time on Tuesday, after run-
ning 45 years as a Freetrade and Liberal organ.
The award of the Country Printers’ Wages Board is
given as the reason for winding up.— Doleful news
item.
Here lies the Herald beneath the sward.
Figures and facts it long defied ;
But at the sight of the Printers’ Board
And decent wages, it sank and died.
Weep Freetraders and Liberals gnash!
Order the mourning from far Peru!
The Myth explodes when the terms are cash.
Time has dishonored our I O U.
N.s.w. CLAUDE CRAPE.
“Scribe”: The London Tavern, next to the old
Age office in Elizabeth-street (Melb.), has just been
knocked down. It almost died of grief when the Age
went to Collins-street. In those days it kept Wolf
Isaacs, who seemed to be almost on the staff. Reporters,
of those liquidacious times, saw that the pump was
well oiled. But not the comps. ; they must walk
about a mile, to gather at Charley Benson’s pub,
corner of Bourke and Russell streets. But what has
the Press come to? Reporters of the Argus, Age
and Herald went lately to view a new brewery.
After copious note-taking the boss said, “Now, now
for the XXXX.” All were teetotal! “Then is
Doomsday near ! ” sighed the brewer.
* * * *
Guy Innes, sub-editor of the Melbourne Argus,
having become news editor of the Melbourne Herald,
vice Jack Nish, deceased, a shuffle of portfolios has
taken place in the conservative office. Harry Mad-
dison becomes sub-editor, with R. W. Jones as his
chief accomplice; and A. L. Brient, of a well-known
family of inky wayfarers, becomes cable editor, vice
Maddison.
“Joe”: Women are pushing into the professions
all right. I went up thiee flights of stairs to interview
the editor of a stock and farm paper, t’other day,
about an article I wanted published. The girl who was
in charge of the editorial department pushed back
her tousled hair with an inky fist, and inquired my
business. I explained as briefly as possible. She
fixed me up in a jiffy, remarking that the editor
was out. “Haven’t you got a sub-editor or a re-
porter?” I asked. “Oh yes,” she chirruped; “I’m
them! ”
“Adelaide”: Melbourne Booklover makes the
amazing statement that for an Australian writer to
get his living, “there are but two ways open to him—
the life of the freelance, which ends necessarily in
‘longsleevers’ and a handsome memorial, and the
ordinary journalist’s work, than which nothing could
be more corroding to the finer self. If you want a
proof of this statement, look around you!” Well, I
have tried both “ways” mentioned; and I have
known many who have pursued one or the other.
For myself, I have never drunk more than was good
for me, or qualified for a tombstone, or had my
finer self (if any) corroded to any discoverable ex-
tent. Nor have the others —with a few exceptions
in the longsleever direction, and that weakness is
not confined to journalists. The whole statement is,
in fact, a foolish libel on a capable body of good
citizens.
“Locust”: The descriptive writer on the Sydney
daily press is overdoing his new way of beginning an
article. Suppose he is writing about a fire. He
begins with a line (indented one em, and with a
note of admiration) like this: —
“Ding-a-ding-a-ding! ”
Then follows a description of the rush and roar of
the fire engines, the slosh of the water and so on.
A funeral is prefaced with the impressive word:—
“Dong!”
That opens up the question of church bells, their
muffled grandeur, the body on the catafalque, the
awful instability of human greatness, and the pro-
minent public men who attended the funeral. Or a
political situation begins in this way:—
“Was Mr. Willis duped?”
And we pass along to a consideration of the wicked-
ness of the Labor party; the nobility of Mr. Wade,
V.C. ; the necessity for a dissolution, and the views
of Mr. David Storey. It is reminiscent of the evan-
gel-actor style of the Chapzander people. The best
known instance was the sermon of Spurgeon, of the
Tabernacle, London, beginning—
“Oh, H—11!”
or words to that effect, followed at a long interval
of startled silence, by an explanation that he heard
a man say that just'now; leading up to a disserta-
tion on the evils of loose language and the immin-
ence of ll—l for those who cuss unnecessarily.
OVERTAKEN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
George Reid confided to a young Australian in
London: “A man can’t get away from his past.
Mine has followed me round the earth. A York-
shirerrtan asked me at a provincial meeting the
other day: ‘What did Australia send you here for?
Haven’t we got Freetraders of our own to get rid
of?’ And, would you believe it, my only youthful
indiscretion, those Sydney poems, were thrown up at
me in Glasgow?”
The speaker in electric glow
Insanely wagged his arms;
He voiced the nation’s needs, you know,
And spoke of grave alarms,
Because of someone else’s view,
And things the other chaps might do.
He’d reached his highest flight, when, whack,
A missile struck his head ;
He lay upon his good, broad back—
They thought that he was dead.
The weapon was a volume fat
Of verses thin, and poor at that.
In bed he scanned the pages wide,
Then heaved a hollow moan,
And turned him to the wall and died —•
The verses were his own!
The moral’s plain: Though you be fast,
Your sins will catch you up at last.
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON

LIMITED,
The Big BlocK,
BRISBANE.
IN
BANANALAND
Captain Cloherty, who has been appointed
Harbor Master at Brisbane, has been knock-
ing about Moreton Bay for nearly 30 years,
first as master of the pilot boat and then as
pilot. During the last four years he has
been deputy harbor master and senior pilot.
Passed out, in Mackay Hospital the other
day, Captain John Henry Schluter, one of
the most expert divers of the North. He was
the first to go below and have a look at the
Quetta, after she had sailed to Davy Jones’s
locker and taken her hundred odd passengers
and crew with her.
Although only six months removed from
an inspectorship, and but five years away
from a comfortable pension, Toomey, Bris-
bane’s best-known detective —the only one
of any note —has left the force, in order to
man the beer-pump. He has probably been
inspired by the success of Peter Murphy.
M.L.C., one-time policeman and later pub-
lican, who is now nearly first on Brisbane’s
list of heavy income-tax payers.
Dinny Keogh, of Rosewood, was not a
statesman, but on one critical occasion he
achieved a fame which lasted for a whole
day. It was in December, 1899, when the
Dickson Government was in power. Dickson
brought in a Bill to create a standing rail-
ways committee. The Labor party and the
Independents opposed it, and they were
joined by discontented Ministerialists, headed
by Joey Bell and Janny Leahy. A violent
debate took place, in which every member de-
clared himself but Keogh. Every effort to
induce him to speak on the Bill failed. _ It
was whispered privately that he had promised
Dickson to vote for the measure, and various
dodges were set afoot to smuggle him out of
the building. These failed. At a late hour
the division was taken. When the bells
rang Keogh was not to be seen; but just be-
fore the doors were locked he appeared inside
the bar. He was with a silence
inspired by suspense. Keogh hesitated,
walked down the gangway, and then stood
still, gazing first on one side and then on the
other. Then, after a moment’s wavering, he
sat on the side of the Government. There
was a yell of execration from the Laborites,
and a scream of triumph from the Dickson-
ites. The noise startled Keogh, and for one
moment it seemed that he was going to
change his mind. He raised himself on the
cushions, but sank back immediately. The
division gave Dickson a majority of one. He
resigned, and Andy Dawson reigned in his
stead —for one week.
A Ballad of Puritee.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Denham Government is proposing to deal
drastically with the liquor laws, with gambling', with
improper houses, with cattle-stealing, and wickedness
generally. —News item.
It was the State of Digbv D.
That took a fit of Puritee,
And in its fervor did decree
The death of all Iniquitee;
That, first and foremost, Drink should be
Abolished in totalitee ;
And ladies all of life too free
Should mend their manners instantly;
Or else each lax, improper she,
Must leave the land immediatelee,
That naught might soil the sanctitee
Of that sweet State of Digby I).
And wicked wights who will not see
Of sweet two-up the villainy,
The penalties of Law must dree
Like them who venture on the spree.
And they who linger lovingly
Around the spot where bookies be,
Must drop their shameless revelry
And join the cult of Toast and Tea
And spieling gents who prey with glee
On simple you and simple me,
The Law doth sternly now decree
Must drop their thimble and their pea.
And they whose language is too free —
Who fling around the frequent D—,
Who’re never known to bend the knee
(Except to kick a referee),
In one great horde, as we shall snn.
By big John Hops shall gathered be,
And driven fast and furiously
Outside the State of Digby D.
QUEENSLAND.
F. B.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, September 2, 1911.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
Fortune-tellers are not mentioned in
Queensland’s new Police Offences Bill, al-
though in Brisbane they do nearly as much
harm as the peroxided barmaids, at whom
the measure obviously shudders. Rumor sup-
plies as a reason for the silence the super-
stitious tendencies of one of the patriarchs
of the Cabinet.
Although Brisbane’s prosperity is a very
real thing for the moment, agents don’t find
it easy to let large private residences. Vari-
ous more or less historic houses have been
empty for many months, and social magnates
cling tenaciously to the seven-roomed villas
of Suburbia. The hauteur of housemaids
is a dreadful thihg to face.
A CONNOISSEUR.
HARASSED PRISONER : “Pray, great chief, why do you keep your servant tiet iup so long? ”
CANNIBAL KING : “ A thousand pardons, dear missionary; but we are waiting for thecon-
signment of paper bags from Sydney
The only Queensland charity named after
Lady Chelmsford is a charity no more. The
Lady Chelmsford Milk Institute, after much
ringing of christening bells and several years
of muddle and mismanagement, was finally
refused a Government endowment, and its
working plant has been sold to. a. milk supply
co. Actually very little free juice was ever
dispensed by the Institute; the bulk of the
daily supply was sold to adult faddists and
hypochondriacs.
By the Zealandia, which touched at Pin-
kenba to-day, came your Premier and Mrs.
McGowen, Mrs. Andrew Fisher and Mrs.
Batchelor; and all was hurry and bustle in
official circles. After telling the press inter-
viewers what a glorious nation old Free-
trade Britan is—as observed from. West-
minster Abbey, the Hotel Cecil and
a railway carriage—Premier McGowen
took the missus for a stroll, while
Wee Macgregor and Denham were ’phoning
frantically for a lost guest, who was wanted
at an official luncheon. That luncheon did
not come on. Mrs. Fisher and Mrs. Batche-
lor were, however, captured early, whirled to
One Tree Hill, fed by Mrs. Denham (who
had the rest of the Cabinet’s better half as
a bodyguard), brought back and escorted
round town per motor-car. Both ladies
are looking exceedingly well, but have little
to say of the trip, beyond that it was “mag-
nificent,” and ti.at the Presentation gowns
were very becoming. Verily, the Presenta-
tion gowns made more impression than the
Suffragette. Mrs. Fisher is among her own
folk here, and was met by an aunt and
cousin from Gympie.
The Mayoress has again been pursuing
hard cash in its accustomed haunts, this
time for the benefit of the Children’s Hospi-
tal; and once more she has shown her quali-
fications to hold a first-class certificate from
the Amalgamated Society of Inveterate Life
Insurance Agents, and the Do-Business-or-
Die Association of Sewing-machine Fiends.
When she opens her collection book to do
business, business has got to be done. Any-
how, the hospital deserves the bloated cheque
which will hit it in the treasury as a result
of the-collection and a young people’s dance.
At the latter* Lady Mac. and the Vision hap-
pened along by design, and smiled vice-regal
approval of the peeresses, poster maidens,
Scotch lassies, colleens and other unoriginal
attempts to make attractive the original
feminine.
Bowlers’ wives have no anxiety as to the
whereabouts of hubby these days, knowing
full well that he is battling on the shorn field
against the determined cannonaders from
Newcastle, and bringing all the tricks of his
deadly art to play in the defence of his jack-
ball. The games have been keen and close,
and, although I don’t know anything about
bowls, except that Drake refused to meet the
Armada till he had had a few, I like to
watch the contortions of the chiefs, or ser-
angs, or skips, or whatever they are. In
honor of the visitors, the Clayfield Club in-
vited not only its friends, but its wife and
mother-in-law, to a euchre and bridge party,
and other city clubs have made the New-
castle men sorry to have to go home.
Another loss to the steadily-dwindling
forlorn hope behind Denham is Dick Cottell,
M.L.A. for Toowong, who met Death in his
sleep, early on Wednesday morning, and went
away with him. Cottell was a follower of
Kidston until that shining statesman retired.
Also, he was the follower and good friend
of all out-door sport, and for some years was
secretary to the Queensland Lawn Tennis
Association. He leaves behind a wife and
three young children.
The redistribution of seats and the death
of two members mean a political shuffle
that will leave somebody in the pack.
Macartney, of the Lands, means to resign
North Brisbane, and again woo Toowong.
He will be opposed by one Lloyd, a foeman
worthy of his best platitudes.
Notwithstanding the fact that Spring
has elbowed the cold to one side, the private
dance continues with the regularity of the
Scotch Sawbath. Dr. and Mrs. Sanford
Jackson, in honor of niece Miss Ruth Jack-
son, of Geelong, gathered in a number of
men and maidens just emerging from their
fiufferdom, and set them chasing the flying
hours with flowing feet. The Jackson lean-
to includes a magnificent ball-room, and sev-
eral miles of verandah overlooking a beauti-
ful garden and the river. A daughter, who
combines looks with brains, assisted her
mother to make everybody wish midnight
would never strike.
Another dance was given by the A. 11.
Websters, at Whinstanes, in honor of
daughter Hilda, a pretty, fair-haired little
girl who has thoroughly enjoyed the delights
of her first season.
The sensation of the hour has been caused
by a pretty Society girl from the South, who
sojourned awhile with the spouse of one of
our future wool-sackers. After dancing one
night or morning till the hour when the
rooster and the milkman hit the dawn simul-
taneously, she breakfasted, wandered forth,
and wedded a small but persistent suitor,
dismissed him and returned home to lunch.
Some days later she apparently recalled
incident, and mentioned the fact of her mar-
riage quite casually. The resultant stir
would have gratified the most hardened
thrower of bombs.
A welcome innovation in the matter of en-
tertaining goes to the credit of Miss
Madge Woolcock, who has affixed the
usual stamp to the usual gilt-edged
card, and bidden the flutters and flap-
pers, both male and female, to a moonlight
garden party. The only thing that worries
the bidden is the correct garment for a moon-
light garden party. What does one wear at
an affair of the sort?
A smart tennis party was given the other
day by Mrs. Arthur Feez and dainty daughter
Myee, the athletic young person who is one
of our most ornamental chauffeurs or chauf-
feuses. Tennis parties are quite the thing.
The Vision gives them at regular intervals,
and on every lawn in Suburbia the habits
of sub-regality are faithfully copied.
The Dorothy Ryder boom is not yet on
the wane, the latest dispenser of _ free tea
in that young lady’s honor being Miss Emily
Beirne, whose own knot-tying to Mr. Tom
Hooper—a pillar of rowing and lacrosse
clubs —is not very far distant.
Yours, affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Dr. Row, medical superintendent of the
lazaret on Peel Island, is satisfied that there
are lots of Bananaland lepers who are never
segregated. All the same, to reward the
State for taking Ah Chow and Tommy Tan-
na to its bosom, there were 85 cases under
treatment at the lazaret last year. Of
these, 25 were whites, 53 discolored (ol
Kanakas, and 14 Binghis), and seven half-
castes.
Treasurer Barnes’s Budget speech was one
long scream of rejoicing about the magnifi-
cent prosperity Queensland has enjoyed dur-
ing the past financial year. Yet, according
to numberless Tory predictions, the past
year, being the first period under the -os.
per capita Commonwealth arrangement,
should have been hung all over with crape
and calamity. As a prophet the Tory is
even more of a failure than he is as a poli-
tician.

The Power of the Great Mogul
Though the power of the Great Mogul was tremendous, it furnished a striking
example of misplaced energy—misplaced by reason of it’s being directed where it did
the least amount of good.
The “1.H.C.” “Mogul” Gasolene Tractor has, like its namesake, enormous power,
but unlike him its, power is developed just where the greatest possible benefits accrue
from its energies, i.e., the Draw-bar.
That’s one great reason for the phenomenal and universal success of the “1.H.C.”
Tractor—it wastes less hauling energy than any other internal combustion engine in
the wide world.
No need to take our word for the above, because the “1.11. C.” has proved it
again and again in open competition against the world’s next best engines.
In the last competition at Winnipeg, Canada, its competitors were nine in number.
Three of these engines were withdrawn from Draw-bar test, and the remaining
six developed at the Draw-bar an average of 59.5% of their horse-power as against
75% developed by the “1.H.C.”
Not content with a 15% victory in the matter of Draw-bar power development,
the “1.H.C.” in the Fuel test at the same competition consumed 33% less fuel per
acre ploughed than the average amount consumed by its seven competitors.
As compared with other engines, buying an “LHC.” Tractor is therefore on a
par with buying several draught horses, that r.ot only haul more than any others,
but cost far less to feed.
Of all the internal combustion Tractors new in use in Australia, 4 out of 5 are
“IH.C.s”— very significant. ~

The Chalmers has been
recommended to me.”
h
Day after day, both in Sydney and Melbourne, intending purchasers come to our showrooms to
see the Chalmers—men from the North of Queensland, men from Tasmania, men from all over
Australia.
We take this condition to mean that the Chalmers car has not only now made a good reputa-
tion in our country, but also that the Chalmers Company has succeeded in building cars that
appeal to the careful buyers here.
Out of the 46 cars we have sold since Christmas, 35 of them were Chalmers cars; out of
these 35 purchasers, 12 were experienced motorists—men who had previously owned cars. Again,
there were four repeat orders from Chalmers owners.
From Sydney to Narromine, 300 miles, at an average of 33 miles per hour, and over 20 miles
to the gallon of fuel. From Warialda to Newcastle, 310 miles, in daylight. Seven thousand
miles in 10 weeks, and the front tyres still good. These records speak for themselves.

Forest Devil
This One Caps Them All!
“I have owned one of the ‘ Bunyip ’ Forest Devils
since 1909, and have used it in all classes of green stumps
and trees, large and small, and it has given every satis-
faction. I pulled two large trees in the township of
Marong, and plenty of the watchers stated that the
machine would not shift them. They were both pulled in
a very short time and with little trouble. I have pulled
large trees, one being 16ft. Bin. round the girth. The
machine, while in my use, never cost me one penny, and
is still in perfect order. I have used lots of other
machines, but this one caps them all.”
(Signed) E. J. OSBORN, Marong, via Bendigo.-

ALMOST everybody knows about my efforts
to make Cigar-smoking popular in Aus-
tralia.
My FREE CIGAR TRIAL is this:—l’ll send
you a box of too DIADEMAS for 22/6—equal, as
they are, to any 6d. Cigar on the market; really
worth £2 ss. per 100 —and you can smoke 5 or 6
of them. If they aren’t as good as any Cigar
you have ever tried, send the remainder back to
me, and I’ll refund your money in full, without
any charge for those you’ve smoked.
ħ
Now, I want to prove to
you how straight this offer is.
You might think—“ Why
doesn't he send the box up
without any money, and let
us send the money on when
we’ve tried the 5 or 6 and
found them as good as he
says, or else return the
balance then ?”
I tell you right off, I’d like
to do that. But that would
put all the trusting on my
side —and I've got to make a
living.
I know nearly every man-
jack ofyou would act straight
to me. if I did make this offer.
But you know as well as I
there are some who would
get the Cigars, and let me
whistle for the money.
I know there’d be only a
few who would do this; but
I sell my Cigars so close to
cost—cutting off all the ware-
houseman’s, traveller’s and
retailer’s profits—that these
few would cripple my scheme
altogether. That’s why I
can’t do it and give the same
Cigar value as I give to-day.
I know you’re trusting me
in sending down the money
before you get the Cigars.
But you’ll get the Cigars
right by return post, never
fear, You can smoke sor 6
of them, and if they’re not
as good as you expected,
you can send what are left
right back. I'll return your money every
penny of it, with no deduction whatever—
and even here you needn't only take my word
for it.
I have my banking account at the Com-
mercial Bank of Australia Limited, Melbourne.
The General Manager there knows all about
me and my way of doing business. He'll vouch
for me; and you can refer to him before you
send any order at all to me. That’s proof of my
squareness, isn’t it?
Sit down now and send me 22/6 to-day, and
I’ll forward you a box of 100 DIADF.MAS —
Cigars that I promise are equal to any 6d.
Cigar sold in Australia—made from clean, long,

Yagabond.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
"TT 'WAIT, among the rushes
That edge a salt lagoon,
- The coming of the wild duck
To, feed beneath the moon.
Where jovial day, departing,
Drinks deeply as he goes,
From out a golden goblet
Inlaid with pearl and rose;
Like feast-cloths splashed by spillage
Of precious wines, expressed
In vintage old, the clouds are stained
By lees of sunset west.
Each furred and feathered creature
Its ordered way betakes;
As daylight nature drowses,
Nocturnal nature wakes.
In ti-trees densely drooping
Dark shadows on the tide —
With silver mullet glutted —
Sleek cormorants abide;
Wild whimbrels scatter seaward
With protests harsh and lorn;
Sad cux-lews in the gloaming
Their ancient griefs bemourn.
And as the dusk her mantle
Enwraps around the day,
The kookaburra’s laughter
Discordant dies away.
The warm Bush, palpitating
With summer, lies a-thrill
In downward sweep of gully
And upward lift of hill;
And, like a veiled maiden,
With trembling, shy delight,
In woodland tryst awaiteth
The coming of the night.
From startled fish, upleaping
In sudden sport or play,
The ripples circle outward
Along the waterway.
They draw my straying vision
To peer the dark’ning weeds,
And eye again the' shallows,
Wherein the black duck feeds.
Across the scrubby hammocks
Comes, wafted cool, to me,
With recollections laden,
A salt breath of the sea,
From shores of High Ambition,
And ports of Good Intent,
Where once, v 7 ith effort freighted,
My ship of Fortune went.
Where now this vessel bideth —
By Islands of Despair
On fruitful Coasts of Promise —
I neither know nor care.
What care 1 how the combats
Of striving cities roll?
Great health is on my sinews,
Great peace rests on my soul.
No mean thoughts hourly haunt me,
No wolf snarls at my door;
Against my trigger finger
A charge of Number Four
Contains my dinner’s promise,
Nor shall I, waking, dread
The morrow or a master
To dole my daily bread.
No rentlord, stern as Fate is,
Doth mar the glad week’s end;
Nor will insistent trades folk
Their sordid missives send.
For yonder on the headland,
Dove-white a tent is furled,
Which has for its environs
The garden of the world.
Beyond its open doorway,
Along untrodden sands,
The ocean sings for ever,
And ever shine the lands.
Why should I for the freehold
Of acres green aspire,
AVho hold in gypsy tenure
The greenness of a shire?
I know the earth I’ll lie in,
When these short days are o’er,
Than his who lords a parish
Shall not be less—or more.
Why should I riches covet
With treasures such as these
That richen my far-spreading
Estate of lands and seas?
The stars are all my jewels;
The sun is all my gold ;
The silver of the waters
Is mine to keep and hold.
The pillars of my mansion
In carven strength uprise
Where throw the forests splendid
Their arches to the skies,
And, draped with richest arras
Of painted leaf and vine,
Its halls anon re-echo
A woodland music fine.
Here hang my pictures splendid,
In blue-domed galleries;'
Here ring the strains unending
Of songs that never cease.
I pity Sultan Mahmoud
Upon his gilded seat,
I pity Demos toiling,
And Dives at his meat.
For I have found the portal
Through which the sages went,
To tread the gentle highway
Of Infinite Content.
But if ye seek the gateway
Who also would be free,
Be sure it will not open
Unto a golden key.
For yokes that fall the lightest
On rebel necks like mine,
Are borne where no conventions
Like streets in order line.
And road that lies the fairest
To vagabonds like me,
Is by the River Careless
That runs through Romany.
VICTORIA.
E. J. BRADY.
The Fool and the Woman.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A SUDDEN flapping of paper ; a rustle ;
a, thump on the floor.
The manager looked round with a
frown, which became a smile as he faced the
first of the sea-breeze. Through the unglazed
windows it had come with a rush that had
blown over a sheaf of invoices, and with
them a small inkbottle.
The sea-breeze had within the last couple
of months become a matter of importance to
Buhner. Bulmer, who, once a burly and
red-faced Briton, had laughed to scorn the
atrocities of the West- African climate.
Deadly? Not a bit of it! The men who
went under were the men who were beaten
before they started the fight. They expected
the place to knock them over, and the place
obligingly knocked them over accordingly.
He had been here ten solid years, and
well, look at him!
And at the time he had been worth look-
ing at. Bluff-built and ruddy, as has been
said, he, had been also clear-eyed, firm-jawed,
and straight as a palm-stem. Now, to put
it shortly, he was none of these things, ex-
cept when for a brief space he was able to
pull himself together. But “pulling your-
self together” means in certain cases only a
greater subsequent shaking of yourself
apart. And Buhner’s was one of those
cases. #
“Can’t make you out at all,” the sun-
dried lath of a doctor said, thoughtfully roll-
ing a black Trichinopoly in his mouth.
“Liver’s a trifle enlarged, but nothing to
speak of. Sound as a bell otherwise. You’ve
no fever, and gastric tonics would be thrown
away upon you in this hell’s brew of a cli-
mate.”
Bulmer wriggled impatiently in his deep
deck-chair. “You doctor-chaps are precious
little use at a pinch,” he said, drearily.
“Strikes me that half of you have never
learnt your trade and the other half have
forgotten it.”
The doctor smiled. “Epigrammatic!” he
said. “Well, I’m not much inclined to con-
tradict the first part of your proposition.
But, tell me”—thoughtfully pulling his grey
moustache —“have you any enemies in the
settlement?”
“Enemies! No. I’m on good terms, as
far as I know, with every man in the place.
There aren’t so many of them, if it comes to
that.”
“Not very many white men.” Dr. Des-
mond looked at the other intently.
“Oh, you’re thinking of the blacks, are
you? Confound the brutes! I have as little
to do with them as possible. No ‘man-and-
brother’ bosh for me. A ‘damn nigger’ is a
‘damn nigger’—and there’s an end of it. But
I don’t know why I should have any enemies
amongst the darkies, or what they could do,
if I had.”
Desmond got up and paced to and fro for
a space before he spoke.
“No; the niggers wouldn’t trouble much
about your abstract estimate of them, so
long as you didn’t take to manhandling
them. But, as to what they could do —
well, that’s another matter.”
Bulmer got up in his turn, and looked
with half-contemptuous smile at the other.
“I suppose you’re referring to ‘jiu-jitsu’,” he
said.
“You mean ‘ju-ju.’ Yes, that’s what I
was thinking of.”
Bulmer laughed uproariously, but it did
not escape the doctor’s trained observation
that there was a note of the hysteric in the
cachinnation. “They laugh best who laugh
last,” he quoted gravely.
“I’m sure I beg your pardon, doctor,” the
other said. “But it certainly was a bit-
amusing to find a' man of your experience
and knowledge of the world”
Desmond checked him. “It is precisely
my experience that teaches me How-
ever, let it pass.” He relighted his cigar
and puffed furiously.
“What does ‘ju-ju’ mean, anyhow?” Bul-
mer asked, after a long pause in the talk.
Desmond avoided his eye. “Oh, it means
a lot of things. Religion, sorcery, ritual,
mystery—in short, anything awful, or horri-
ble, or weird.”
“But, as it affects human beings?” Bulmer
persisted. “Or as it is supposed to affect
them?”
“That’s the way to look at it,” the doctor
said. “Is supposed to affect them.. Because
there’s probably nothing in the notion, after
all.”
His manner was unconvincing. The other
man saw that he wanted to put the subject
aside.
“Well,” Bulmer said, “you seemed just
now to think there was a good deal in it.
Now, look here —has this anything to do
with the infernal hanky-panky you appear
to have in your mind?” He dived a hand
into the breast pocket of his white drill coat
and brought forth a small shark-skin bag,
from which he shook out upon the table,
amongst some dried grass, the skull of a
snake.
The doctor took this up and examined it
as closely as if he had never set eyes on
such a thing before. And his face gave no
sign. “Where’d you get this?” he asked,
with affected carelessness.
“ITound it in my pocket a month ago.
Wondered how it cot there. Threw it away.
Found it in the same place a couple of days
later. Wondered still more, and threw it
away again—this time into the lagoon. In
two days it was in my pocket again.
Pitched it into the lagoon again, and kept
my coat under my head at night. On the
morning of the third day it was in my pocket
once more. Then I smashed the skull up with
a hammer. No use. In three days it, or its
perfect replica, was in my pocket again.
I’ve let it stay there since,” concluded Bui-

mer with a sickly grin, “because —because —I
can’t help myself!”—and the once aggress-
ive Bulmer broke into a miserable whimper.
Desmond strode swiftly into his bottle-
room and returned in ten seconds with a.
draught. “Here, old man,” he said cheer-
fully. “Gobble this, and then go home and
lie down. I’ll come over inside half an hour.
’Fraid you’re in for a touch of fever, after
all; but we’ll take it in time, and pull you
through all right.”
But Desmond knew there was no fever in
the case, and Bulmer knew that he knew.
The doctor, when lie was alone, flung him-
self down into a chair and crossed his hands
behind his grizzled head. “Well, Denis
Desmond,” he said to himself, “here you are,
right up against it again—this damnable
thing that has our boasted Western medical
science beaten to a standstill. ‘Jadoo’ in
India, ‘obi’ in the West Indies, ‘porroh’ in
the interior of this lovely continent, and ‘ju-
ju’ all along this accursed coast. And I be-
lieve it’s all fundamentally the same thing.
And we are absolutely powerless, we pundits
of the West. We’ve just got to sit back
and look on, while what we are pleased to
call barbarism scores a notch above us every
time. As for this poor chap, Bulmer, he’ll
be on t’other side in less than a month.”
Then—with that indifference which, in a
doctor, is at once so natural and so un-
natural—Desmond went across to the Pcrle
d'Afrique for a game of billiards.
And there, when he was on the point of
scoring an easy “in oft the red,” somebody
said: “Queer thing about Bulmer.” Des-
mond missed his shot and turned to the
speaker. The other nodded. “Yes,” lie
said. “Pegged out about ten minutes ago.”
I hen, with tin* carelessness of the dweller
in a place where every white man carried,
all the time, his life in his hand —“Sker-
riek’s pretty sure to slip into his place, I
suppose. He’s not next in seniority, but
lies been coaching himself up in the details
of the business as if he fullv expected to fill
Bulmer’s shoes.”
“He’ll have to go home first, though,”
said another. “Fairland and Coxon won’t
appoint a man to such an important post
without having personal knowledge of him.
Bulmer himself had to make the trip home
and back when Aird went under.”
* * * *
Skerrick, with his heels on the verandah-
rail, smoked and gazed reflectively at the
broad tropic moon, mounting majestically
from the forest-clad ridge at the back of the
settlement. The B.A. boat was due off the
port to-morrow, and Skerrick was a purpos-
ing passenger.
“And the point is,” he said to himself,
“would it be better tc« break off with that
confounded woman to-night, or to leave the
matter till I come back?”
A faint rustle made him turn his head.
The question had settled itself; the “con-
founded woman” was before him.
“Just as well.” he muttered. Then, aloud:
So it s you, Loba. Come to say good-bye,
eh?”
Loba was a half-caste, but the colored
moiety of her wasn t negro. Her mother had.
been an Ibami from the northern Soudan,
and Loba possessed the tall, lithe figure and
bold, aquiline features of the race. Indeed,
she was a determined-looking young person
generally, as she stood there, statue-like in
her long white saka, and made briefly known,
in tolerable English, the purpose of her visit.
Skerrick had kept from her, she said, the
knowledge of his approaching departure.
“Why tins be?” she asked quietly. “You no
meana marree me, as you tell many time?”
Skerrick suddenly determined to take the
situation by the throat. He had never had
the slightest intention of marrying her, and
now was the time to tell her so.
Loba heard it all calmly, though the an-
nouncement was not entirely unseasoned
with the spitefulness so often found in men
of Skerriek’s outward stamp —undersized,
meagre, and sharp-featured.
“So you see, Loba,” he concluded, “the
thing is entirely out of the question now—
whatever it may have been when —when”-
"When you wanta me put ju-ju on Buli-
mah,” the woman slipped in. “Good ! Buli-
mah gone. Now you go, too!”
“Yes. But only ror a little time, Loba.
And I’m sure you won’t bear me any malice
when I come bacK.”
“Malish? What he? Oh, hata you, you
mean. No, 1 no hata you—w’en you com-a
back. An’ now, good-a-bye—l no see you
again.”
“Oh, yes, Loba, you will —when i come
back.”
The woman’s white teeth flashed in the
moonlight.
“Wen you com-a back?” she repeated—
and went, without another word.
Skerrick looked after the gliding white
figure. “Bit of a tragedy-queen,” he said.
“Well, the thing was easier than I expected.
I’m well out of it, at all events.”
lie lit another cheroot and went inside to
pack up.
The big B.A. boat was getting her twin-
screws to work again, after her two hours’
stop outside the bar.
. “Glad to have you on board, Mr. Sker-
rick,” the skipper said. “Going to have
charge out here, I understand. Shouldn’t
wonder if we had you back with us on the
return trip. Know your firm well. They
are riglit-off-the-reelers, and no mistake.
Hullo! —what’s that?”
Skerrick, pulling out his handkerchief to
wipe his streaming brow, had pulled With it
a little sliark-skin bag, which fell on the
deck. lie picked it up and opened it. Some
dry grass, enveloping a snake’s skull.
“Funny little outfit,” said the skipper,
carelessly, but his face was very grave.
“Best heave it overboard.”
“Why, yes,” assented Skerrick. “It cer-
tainly hasn’t much intrinsic value.” He
threw the things over the side. “The puzzle
is how the bag got into my pocket.”
“Some nigger-boy’s trick, 1 suppose,” said
the skipper. But he knew better. He had
been running the West-African coast for 20
years.
“Look here, doc.,” he said to the ship’s
surgeon later on, “d’you know anything of
this ju-ju business they talk so much about?”
The doctor fingered his beard for a few
moments before he spoke.
“Well, of course I have heard plenty about
the thing; but, if you want any explana-
tion of it from me, I tell you plainly I can’t
give it. If any man could throw light on
the subject, it ought to be Desmond, down
there where we’ve just left; but, though he
has been five-and-tbirty years on this devil-
ish coast, lie’s still as much in the dark as
any of us. He knows about some of the
things they use, though. He told me about
a snake’s skull”
“In a little bag, with some dried grass,”
put in the captain eagerly. “Yes; and
there’s a man on board who has just found
the charm, or the curse, or whatever it is, in
Ins pocket.”
“Which man?” said the doctor, and the
captain pointed out Skerrick. “Fairland
and Coxon’s man,” he explained. “Going
home to be made manager, and come out
again.”
“Hum!” said the doctor. “He’ll be lucky
if he ever gets to Liverpool—to say nothing
of coming out again. He threw the thing
overboard, you say?”
“Saw him do it.”
“Oh, well; we’ll wait and see. But if he
keeps on finding the same thing, or some-
thing that can’t be distinguished from it,
he'll never see England again.”
“Good Lord!” said the skipper. “And
can’t we do anything?”
“Nothing,” said the doctor, gloomily, “ex-
cept lock him in his cabin ; and, even if you
were justified in doing that, it wouldn’t save
him.”
* * * *
Skerrick, sitting down to dinner next
evening, found in his serviette, in place of
the customary piece of bread —a little shark-
skin bag! He made no sign, put the thing
in his pocket, and, as soon as he could get
on deck after dinner, flung it overboard.
He wasn’t going to be scared, he bravely
told himself, by any such monkey-tricks as
these. But when, two nights later, he
found the accursed little bag under his pil-
low, he sat down, shivering, and had a bad
live minutes before, with a sudden reaction,
he jumped up, opened the port, and flung
the thing furiously out of it. Then he lay
down and pondered for possibly half an
hour.
“That's it!” he summed up. “Some trick of
legerdemain. Some infernal idiot wasting
his time in trying to frighten Joe Skerrick.
But Joe Skerrick” —a cross-current came
into his thoughts. “There has been nobody
into this cabin since 1 entered it,” he told
himself; “so, of course” — He lifted confi-
dently the pillow—and there lay a little
shark-skin bag!
He sat for a few minutes, paralysed ; then
he seized the bag and tore it open—as before,
a snake’s skull and some dried grass.
Then, suddenly, he knew, and a "voice
sounded again in his ears.
“Good! Bulimah gone. Now you go,
too!”
Double-dyed fool that he had been, to
think that Loba, who at his instigation
had remorselessly done to death a man who
had never injured her, would spare Mm,
who had so coolly broken the promise which
alone had made her his instrument! There
had been no escape for Bulmer. and now
there was no escape for him. His mind—
like that of so many men of hard business
faculty—was but jerry-built in its other de-
partments. It collapsed now like a castle of
cards. He stole out on deck.
The night was dark and thick. A stiffish
nor’-wester was kicking up an ugly head-sea.
The vessel was making noisy plunges; no-
body heard the little splash.
ALEX. B. MONTGOMERY.
Magnificat
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
AT dewy dawn I heard a Voice,
Whose lute-sweet rhythm silenced
strife;
“Rejoice!” it sang, “O man, rejoice
In all the ecstasies of Life!”
It was an angel’s orison;
And then a soft, mysterious sound
I heard, as ’twere the heavenly one
Passed lightly over grassy ground.
Dawn came, and rosebuds breathed sweet
prayers,
And leaves sighed sempiternal vows
To sun-gods, and fantastic airs
Pert sparrows chirruped to wet boughs.
Far eastward shimmered sea and sky,
Merged in phantasmal purple-grey;
In Heaven’s blue the roving eye
Marked not a single bird of prey.
New-washed with radiant summer rain,
New-lit with Hod’s immortal eyes,
All earth heaved glad and green again;
’Tis only human joy that dies.
Earth hath no visionary grief;
She seemed a sleeper, angel-fair,
Waked by love’s kisses. . .past belief
Ilis bright, enchanted presence there!
While o’er the hills I tramped, a throng
Of birds delighting, scarce above
My breath I hummed a little song—
A little song of peace and love.
The joy of life sang in my heart;
’Twas with unruffled rapture that
I sang my tranquil-dreaming part
In earth’s sublime Magnificat.
Far-off the palpitating town,
In keen, surprising sunbeams caught*
Flashed signals, purple, gold and brown;
Such the entrancements God hath wrought!
About each busy, burnished street,
’Mid showers of gold and silver darts,
Young girls were color-bright, and sweet
As sunbuds their up-lifted hearts.
In gardens, green and blossom-dight,
Pods scattered seed for Spring’s return—
Spring, whose soft language of delight
Young birds and lovers love to learn.
Green-fire stirred the inebriate air,
Earth’s joy breathed life in every stone;
Wild songs were warbled everywhere,
In all creation not a moan!
Where rippling water lullabies
A bank that willows dream above,
With tender homage in her eyes
A true giil brimmed mv cup with love!
The gold sun burned the hours away;
My heart was passionately glad...
“Now, surely in all earth to-day
There hideth not a creature sad !”
N.S.W
W. M. WHITNEY.
